Weeks 1–18: Every Topic Explained Simply, With Deep-Dive Practical Examples

IITM Pravartak — Professional Certificate Programme in AI, Machine Learning and Deep Learning

How to use this document

This single guide distills all 18 weeks of the programme into plain-English explanations with worked, practical examples. Each week follows the same shape: a one-line summary, the list of topics, each topic explained simply (with key formulas and a “why it matters” note), one deep-dive worked example, and crisp key takeaways. All formulae and matrices are typeset as proper mathematics so they display clearly, and are also exposed as accessible math text so a screen reader or a text-to-speech tool (such as Speechify) reads them aloud correctly.

Part I — AI: The Big Picture

The course teaches the mathematical and classical-ML foundations in depth (Weeks 1–18). This first part zooms out to the whole field of modern AI — the deep-learning architectures and generative-AI systems the lectures preview but do not fully cover — so you have the complete map before diving into the weeks.

AI Foundations — What AI, Machine Learning and Deep Learning Are

In one line: Artificial Intelligence is the broad project of building machines that perform tasks we associate with human intelligence; Machine Learning is the subset that gets there by learning patterns from data instead of being hand-programmed; and Deep Learning is the subset of Machine Learning that learns those patterns using many-layered neural networks.

What this chapter covers

  • What “Artificial Intelligence” actually means, and the two classic ways of defining it: acting humanly (the Turing Test) and thinking rationally (the “laws of thought”).
  • A short history: symbolic AI and expert systems, the statistical/machine-learning shift, the deep-learning era from ~2012, and today’s generative-AI / large-language-model era.
  • The precise nesting AI ⊃ Machine Learning ⊃ Deep Learning — and what each inner ring adds to the one around it.
  • What “learning from data” means, and how it differs from ordinary rule-based programming.
  • Narrow AI versus general AI, and where today’s systems sit.
  • The core capabilities an intelligent system needs: perception, knowledge representation, reasoning, learning, language, planning, and action.
  • A concrete worked example — spam filtering done three ways — that ties all of this together.

What “Artificial Intelligence” means

Artificial Intelligence (AI) is the branch of computer science concerned with building machines that can carry out tasks that normally require human intelligence — recognising a face, understanding a sentence, planning a route, diagnosing a disease, playing a game well, or answering a question. It is a remarkably old field: the term was coined in 1956, so AI has existed for roughly 75 years, even though the recent wave of public excitement is only about 10–15 years old and was triggered by advances in deep learning.

A useful way to pin down the goal is to ask what we are trying to imitate. The standard textbook (Russell & Norvig) lays out four framings along two axes: imitating humans versus reasoning rationally (ideally), and focusing on thought versus behaviour. Two of these framings appear directly in this course’s own description — making computers “act humanly” and “think rationally” — so they are worth understanding carefully.

The “acting humanly” view is captured by the Turing Test, proposed by Alan Turing in 1950. A machine passes if a human interrogator, communicating only through text, cannot reliably tell whether they are talking to a person or a machine. The point of the test is not philosophy but behaviour: it sidesteps the question of whether the machine really thinks and instead asks whether it acts indistinguishably from a human. To pass, a machine would need natural-language ability, stored knowledge, reasoning to draw conclusions, and learning to adapt.

The “thinking rationally” view goes back much further, to the “laws of thought” — Aristotle’s logic and its modern descendants. Here the goal is not to copy how humans actually reason (humans make mistakes) but to reason correctly: to start from true premises and derive only valid conclusions. This is the intellectual root of symbolic AI, where knowledge is written down as logical statements and an inference engine grinds out consequences. A related, more practical framing is “acting rationally” — the rational agent idea: build a system that, given what it perceives, takes the action expected to best achieve its goals. Most modern AI is best understood this way.

Why it matters: These definitions are not just history. “Act humanly” and “think rationally” describe two genuinely different engineering strategies — imitate observed behaviour versus encode correct reasoning — and the whole arc of AI is largely the story of the first strategy (learning from examples) gradually overtaking the second (writing down rules) for most real-world tasks.

A short history: from rules to learning to generation

For its first few decades, AI was dominated by the symbolic approach (often called “Good Old-Fashioned AI”). Intelligence was assumed to be symbol manipulation: you represent knowledge as explicit facts and logical rules, and you reason over them with search and inference. This produced real successes — theorem provers, planners, and especially expert systems in the 1970s–80s, which encoded a human specialist’s know-how as hundreds of “if-then” rules (for example, MYCIN for diagnosing infections). Expert systems worked impressively inside narrow domains but were brittle: every rule had to be elicited from experts by hand, they did not handle uncertainty or noisy data gracefully, and they could not learn or generalise beyond what was written into them. Periods when funding and optimism collapsed because such promises went unmet are remembered as the “AI winters.”

The response was the statistical / machine-learning shift that gathered force through the 1990s and 2000s. Instead of writing the rules, you let the machine infer them from data using probability and statistics. The course’s own mathematical backbone — linear algebra, calculus, and probability theory — comes from this tradition. Classic techniques from this era (many covered later in this programme) include Bayes and naïve Bayes classifiers, support vector machines, decision trees and random forests, k-means clustering, and linear/logistic regression. These methods were robust, handled uncertainty, and improved as data grew — but they typically relied on humans to first hand-design good features (informative numerical descriptions of the raw data).

The deep-learning era began in earnest around 2012, when a deep convolutional neural network (“AlexNet”) dramatically won the ImageNet image-classification competition. Three forces converged: vastly more data, powerful parallel hardware (GPUs), and improved training methods. The key shift was that deep networks learn the features themselves, directly from raw pixels, audio, or text — removing much of the hand-crafting that earlier ML required. This unlocked rapid progress in vision, speech, and language.

The current generative-AI / large-language-model (LLM) era grew out of the Transformer architecture (2017) and its attention mechanism. Models are now pre-trained on enormous unlabelled corpora and then fine-tuned for specific tasks. Systems like the GPT family (the basis of ChatGPT), along with image and video generators, can produce fluent text, realistic images, and more from a simple prompt. This is the wave responsible for AI’s present prominence — but it stands on the entire stack of ideas before it.

Why it matters: The through-line of this history is a steady migration of effort out of human hands and into data: first the rules were hand-written, then the features were hand-designed, and finally even the features are learned. Understanding that trajectory is the single best way to see why “learning from data” sits at the centre of this course.

AI ⊃ Machine Learning ⊃ Deep Learning — the precise relationship

These three terms are often used loosely, but they nest cleanly, like three concentric rings.

Artificial Intelligence is the outermost ring: the whole goal of making machines behave intelligently, by any means. This explicitly includes approaches that involve no learning at all — hand-coded logic rules, search algorithms, and classic expert systems are all AI. So AI is broader than ML.

Machine Learning is the ring inside AI. What it adds is a method: instead of a programmer specifying the behaviour directly, the system learns the behaviour from data. You supply examples and a model with adjustable internal numbers called parameters, and a training procedure tunes those parameters so the model performs the task well. Crucially, the program’s behaviour is derived from data, not written line by line. Classical ML — Bayes classifiers, SVMs, decision trees, k-means, regression — lives in this ring. Most of these methods still depend on humans to provide good input features.

Deep Learning is the innermost ring, inside ML. What it adds is a particular family of models: artificial neural networks with many layers. Each layer transforms its input a little, and stacking many layers lets the network learn a hierarchy of representations — for an image, early layers might capture edges, later ones shapes, later still whole objects. The defining advantage is automatic feature learning: deep models learn useful features straight from the raw data, so the human no longer has to hand-design them. Convolutional neural networks (for images), recurrent networks and LSTMs (for sequences), and Transformers (now used across text, vision, and speech) all live here.

So every deep-learning system is a machine-learning system, and every machine-learning system is an AI system — but not the other way around. A logic-based chess rule engine is AI but not ML; a decision tree is ML but not deep learning; a Transformer language model is all three.

Why it matters: Knowing exactly what each ring adds tells you what kind of problem each tool is for. Plenty of valuable AI needs no learning. Plenty of valuable ML needs no deep networks. Deep learning earns its cost precisely when you have lots of data and raw, high-dimensional inputs (images, audio, language) where hand-designing features is hard.

Learning from data versus rule-based programming

The cleanest way to grasp machine learning is to contrast it with ordinary programming.

In rule-based (classical) programming, a human writes explicit instructions: input goes in, the rules you wrote process it, and an output comes out. The programmer must anticipate every case and encode the logic by hand. This is wonderful when the rules are known and finite — computing a payroll, sorting a list, validating a form. But for tasks like recognising a handwritten digit or telling a cat from a dog, nobody can write down rules that cover the dizzying variety of real-world inputs.

In machine learning, you flip the arrow. You supply the inputs together with the desired outputs (the examples) and let a training algorithm produce the rules — that is, fit the model’s parameters so that it reproduces the examples and, importantly, generalises to new inputs it has never seen. You are not telling the machine how to decide; you are showing it what the right decisions look like and letting it work out the how.

This shift carries a cost and a risk. The cost is data: ML needs many representative examples, and supervised learning needs them labelled. The risk is overfitting — a model can memorise its training examples (scoring beautifully on them) yet fail on new data, the way a student who memorises past exam answers stumbles on a fresh question. Managing the gap between training performance and real-world performance — through techniques like regularization and proper evaluation on held-out data — is a recurring theme throughout this course.

Why it matters: This reversal — learn the rules from examples rather than write the rules — is the conceptual heart of the entire field. Everything else (which model, which math, which training method) is an answer to the question “how do we make that learning work reliably?”

Narrow AI versus general AI

It is essential to be clear-eyed about what today’s systems are. Virtually all AI in use — including the most impressive image generators and chatbots — is narrow AI (also called weak AI): systems built to perform a specific task or a bounded set of tasks. A spam filter, a face recogniser, a translation system, a game-playing engine, a medical-image classifier — each is extraordinarily capable within its lane and helpless outside it. A champion chess program cannot drive a car or hold a conversation.

General AI (Artificial General Intelligence, strong AI) refers to a hypothetical system with human-level breadth: the ability to understand, learn, and apply intelligence across any task a person can, transferring knowledge fluidly from one domain to another. Despite rapid progress, AGI does not exist today; it remains a research aspiration and a topic of active debate. Modern large language models are notably more general than earlier narrow systems — one model can summarise, translate, write code, and answer questions — which blurs the line, but they are still far from the open-ended, autonomous, fully reliable competence that “general AI” implies.

Why it matters: Keeping narrow-versus-general straight protects you from both hype and despair. The systems you will build in this course are narrow tools — superbly useful precisely because they are focused — and understanding that focus is key to choosing, training, and trusting them appropriately.

The capabilities an intelligent system needs

Whatever approach you take, a system that is to behave intelligently in the real world generally needs some combination of the following capabilities. (This is essentially the checklist implied by the Turing Test, expanded.)

  • Perception — turning raw sensory data into something usable: seeing (interpreting images and video), hearing (interpreting speech and audio), and reading. The three data “modes” this course centres on — text, visual (image/video), and audio (speech/music) — are exactly the natural channels humans use to communicate, which is why perceiving them is so central.
  • Knowledge representation — storing what the system knows about the world in a form it can use, whether as logical facts, probabilities, or the learned internal representations (“embeddings”) inside a neural network.
  • Reasoning — drawing conclusions and inferences from what is known, including reasoning under uncertainty. This is the “thinking rationally” capability made operational.
  • Learning — improving from experience and data rather than being fully pre-programmed; the capability that machine learning supplies.
  • Language — understanding and producing natural language (natural-language understanding and generation), so the system can communicate with people. Sub-tasks include classification, translation, summarisation, and question answering.
  • Planning — choosing a sequence of actions to reach a goal, from scheduling to deciding a robot’s next move.
  • Action — affecting the world: producing an answer, generating an image, controlling a robot or autonomous vehicle. Perception and action together close the loop for an agent operating in an environment.

Why it matters: Real applications combine several of these. A spoken-dialogue assistant must perceive speech, understand and represent the request, reason about an answer, plan a response, and act by speaking back. Seeing AI as this bundle of capabilities — rather than one monolithic thing — is how you decompose any ambitious application into solvable pieces.

A concrete illustration: filtering spam three ways

Imagine you must build an email spam filter. The same problem, solved three ways, makes the AI / ML / DL distinction concrete.

Rule-based AI (no learning). You sit down and hand-write rules: flag any email containing “free money,” more than five exclamation marks, or a known bad sender domain. This is genuine AI — it automates a judgement task — but it is brittle. Spammers simply write “fr€e m0ney,” and your rules miss it. You are forever chasing them, adding rules by hand, and the list never stays current. Nobody told the system to learn; it only ever knows what you typed in.

Classical machine learning (learn from data, with hand-designed features). Instead of writing rules, you collect thousands of emails already labelled “spam” or “not spam.” You decide on useful features — word frequencies, presence of links, sender reputation — and you train a model such as a naïve Bayes classifier or an SVM. The model learns from the labelled examples which feature patterns tend to mean spam, and it generalises to new emails, including tricks it has never seen exactly. You still chose the features by hand, but you no longer wrote the decision rules — the data did.

Deep learning (learn the features too). You feed the raw email text more or less directly into a deep neural network — today, very likely a Transformer. The network learns its own internal features layer by layer, discovering subtle cues (phrasing, structure, context) that a human might never think to specify. Given enough data, this can catch the cleverest disguises, because it is not limited to the features a human imagined in advance. The price is more data and more computation — which is exactly why deep learning is reached for when the input is rich and raw and hand-designed features fall short.

Notice the progression mirrors the history of the whole field: from rules you write, to features you design while the model learns the decision, to a model that learns even the features. That is the entire AI → ML → DL story compressed into one inbox.

Key takeaways

  • AI is the broad goal of intelligent machine behaviour by any means, including non-learning approaches like logic rules and expert systems; it is about 75 years old.
  • AI has classically been defined by acting humanly (the Turing Test) and thinking rationally (the “laws of thought,” i.e. logic); a modern, practical reframing is the rational agent that acts to best achieve its goals.
  • The field’s history is a migration of effort from humans to data: symbolic AI and expert systems → the statistical / ML shift → the deep-learning era (~2012) → today’s generative-AI / LLM era (Transformers, GPT).
  • The three terms nest exactly: AI ⊃ Machine Learning ⊃ Deep Learning. ML adds learning behaviour from data; DL adds many-layered neural networks that also learn the features automatically.
  • Learning from data reverses ordinary programming: you supply inputs and desired outputs and let training produce the rules — powerful, but it needs data and risks overfitting (good on training data, poor on new data).
  • Today’s systems are narrow AI (excellent at specific tasks); general AI (AGI) — human-level breadth across any task — does not yet exist.
  • An intelligent system draws on perception, knowledge representation, reasoning, learning, language, planning, and action, applied across the three core data modes of this course: text, visual, and audio.

AI Foundations — The Machine Learning Landscape

In one line: Machine learning is the discipline of building models that learn patterns from data instead of being explicitly programmed, and this chapter maps the full terrain — the workflow, the learning paradigms, the classical toolbox, and where deep learning fits.

What this chapter covers

  • The end-to-end machine learning (ML) workflow, from raw data to a deployed model.
  • The full map of learning paradigms (supervised, unsupervised, self-supervised, reinforcement, and the rest) with a one-line “what defines it” for each.
  • The classical-ML toolbox: what each algorithm is, and the kind of problem it solves.
  • Where deep learning sits, and how to choose between classical ML and deep learning.
  • The single most important idea for understanding why models fail: the bias–variance tradeoff, and overfitting vs underfitting.

This is the “big picture” chapter. It does not derive the mathematics — later chapters do that. The goal here is to give you a mental map so that every technique you meet later has a place to slot into.

The ML workflow end to end

Almost every machine learning project, however simple or advanced, walks through the same pipeline:

data → features → model → training (optimization) → evaluation → deployment

  • Data. You start with raw observations: text, images, video, audio, sensor readings, transaction logs. Real-world data is messy, so this stage also includes cleaning, handling missing values, and splitting into training/validation/test sets.
  • Features. Raw data is rarely fed in directly. We convert each example into a numerical feature vector \(\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{R}^d\), where \(d\) is the number of features (the dimension). A collection of feature vectors stacks into a matrix \(X\). Encoding categorical values, scaling, and normalization happen here. In classical ML you often design features by hand; in deep learning the model learns them for you.
  • Model. A model is a function \(f(\mathbf{x}; \boldsymbol{\theta})\) with unknown parameters \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\). Choosing a model means choosing the shape of the function (a line, a tree, a network).
  • Training (optimization). Training means estimating \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\) so the model fits the data. We define a loss function \(L(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) measuring error, and minimize it — often by gradient descent, which nudges \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\) downhill against the gradient \(\nabla_{\boldsymbol{\theta}} L\). This is why calculus and optimization underpin the whole field.
  • Evaluation. A model that memorizes its training data is useless. We measure performance on held-out data it never saw, using metrics like accuracy, precision/recall, or mean squared error.
  • Deployment. Finally the trained model is put into production — served behind an API, embedded in an app, monitored, and retrained as new data arrives.

Why it matters: Most failures in practice are not exotic algorithm bugs. They are workflow problems — leaky data splits, bad features, or evaluating on the wrong set. Knowing the pipeline tells you where to look when something goes wrong.

The learning-paradigms map

A “paradigm” is defined by what kind of supervision signal the model learns from. Here is the full map, each in one crisp line:

  • Supervised learning — learns from labeled examples \((\mathbf{x}, y)\), where each input has a known target. Use it for classification and regression (the workhorse of ML; most classical and deep models train this way).
  • Unsupervised learning — learns structure from unlabeled data \(\mathbf{x}\) alone. Use it for clustering and dimensionality reduction, when you have no labels but want to discover groupings or compress data.
  • Semi-supervised learning — uses a small amount of labeled data plus a large pool of unlabeled data. Use it when labels are expensive but raw data is plentiful.
  • Self-supervised learning — the model generates its own labels from the structure of the data (e.g. predicting a masked word). Use it to pre-train large models on huge unlabeled corpora before fine-tuning (the foundation of modern LLMs).
  • Active learning — the model asks for labels on the examples it finds most informative, rather than labeling randomly. Use it to build a labeled set efficiently when human annotation is costly.
  • Reinforcement learning (RL) — an agent learns by trial and error, taking actions in an environment and receiving rewards. Use it for sequential decision-making: game playing, robotics, control.
  • Online learning — the model updates continuously as data streams in, one example at a time, rather than training once on a fixed batch. Use it when data arrives over time or the distribution drifts.
  • Transfer learning / domain adaptation — reuses a model trained on one task or domain as a starting point for a related one. Use it when you have little data for your target task but a related pre-trained model exists.
  • Few-shot / one-shot / zero-shot learning — learns a new task from very few labeled examples (few/one) or none at all (zero, using only a description). Use it when collecting examples for a new class is impractical.
  • Federated learning — trains a shared model across many devices without the raw data ever leaving each device; only model updates are shared. Use it when data is privacy-sensitive or distributed (e.g. phones, hospitals).

Why it matters: Picking the right paradigm is often a bigger decision than picking the algorithm. The question “what supervision can I actually get?” determines everything downstream.

The classical-ML toolbox

These are the pre-deep-learning methods. They remain the right choice for many real problems and are essential for understanding what came after. Grouped by what they do:

Regression (predicting a number) - Linear & polynomial regression — fit a straight line \(\hat{y} = \mathbf{w}^\top \mathbf{x} + b\) (or a curved polynomial) to predict a continuous target. For: trend prediction, baseline forecasting. - Ridge regression / regularization — adds a penalty (e.g. \(\lambda \|\mathbf{w}\|^2\), “quadratic” regularization) to shrink parameters and curb overfitting. For: stabilizing regression when features are many or correlated.

Classification (predicting a category) - k-Nearest Neighbors (kNN) — classifies a point by the majority vote of its \(k\) closest training examples; builds no model, just stores data. For: simple, low-dimensional problems and quick baselines. - Naive Bayes / Bayes classifiers — probabilistic models using Bayes’ rule to pick the most likely class; “naive” assumes features are independent for speed. For: text classification, spam filtering, fast high-dimensional tasks. - Gaussian Mixture Models (GMMs) — represent a class (or cluster) as a blend of several Gaussian “bumps” when one bell curve is too simple. For: density modeling, speaker/language recognition. - Support Vector Machines (SVMs) & kernels — find the separating hyperplane with the largest margin between classes; kernels let them carve curved boundaries in high dimensions. For: clean classification on medium-sized datasets, especially with clear margins. - Decision trees — ask a sequence of yes/no questions about features to reach a class; naturally handle categorical features like color = {red, green, orange}. For: interpretable rules, mixed feature types.

Ensembles (combining many models) - Random forests — average many decision trees trained on random subsets, reducing the instability of a single tree. For: strong, low-tuning tabular performance. - Bagging, boosting, AdaBoost — bagging trains models in parallel on resampled data and averages them (reduces variance); boosting trains them in sequence, each fixing the previous one’s mistakes (AdaBoost is the classic example, reducing bias). For: squeezing maximum accuracy out of weak learners.

Unsupervised methods - k-means & hierarchical clustering — group unlabeled data into clusters; k-means partitions into \(k\) groups around centers, hierarchical builds a tree of nested groupings (no need to fix \(k\) in advance). For: customer segmentation, image grouping, exploratory analysis. - PCA & dimensionality reduction — Principal Component Analysis finds the directions of greatest variance (via eigen-analysis) to compress high-dimensional data with minimal information loss; Fisher discriminant analysis does so while keeping classes separable. For: reducing the curse of dimensionality, visualization, denoising.

Why it matters: Each tool encodes a different assumption about the data (linearity, independence, clusters, margins). Knowing the assumption tells you when the tool will work and when it will quietly fail.

Where deep learning fits

Deep learning uses multi-layered neural networks that learn their own features directly from raw data. Instead of you hand-crafting a feature vector, the network’s early layers discover edges, then shapes, then objects (for images), or words, then phrases, then meaning (for text).

When to prefer classical ML: - Small or medium data. Classical models work well with hundreds to thousands of examples; deep networks are data-hungry and overfit when starved. - Structured / tabular data. For spreadsheet-style rows of well-defined features, ensembles like random forests and boosting often beat deep learning. - Interpretability required. A decision tree or linear model can be read and explained; a deep network is largely a black box. This matters in healthcare, finance, and law. - Limited compute. Classical methods train on a laptop in seconds; deep models often need GPUs.

When to prefer deep learning: - Large data. Performance keeps climbing as data grows, where classical methods plateau. - Unstructured data — images, audio, video, natural language — where hand-designed features are hard and learned features win decisively. - Complex, high-level tasks — captioning, translation, generation, visual question answering — that classical pipelines cannot express.

Why it matters: “Deep learning” is not automatically the answer. The honest default for a new problem is to try a strong classical baseline first; reach for deep learning when data is large and unstructured.

The bias–variance tradeoff

Every model’s error can be split into two competing parts:

  • Bias — error from the model being too simple to capture the true pattern. High bias → underfitting: the model does poorly on both training and test data. (A straight line trying to fit a curve.)
  • Variance — error from the model being too sensitive to the particular training set, memorizing noise instead of signal. High variance → overfitting: great on training data, poor on new data.

The intuition:

\[\text{Total error} \approx \text{Bias}^2 + \text{Variance} + \text{irreducible noise}\]

As you increase model complexity, bias falls but variance rises. The art is finding the sweet spot in the middle. Regularization (like ridge regression, or dropout in neural nets) is the standard lever for pulling an overfit model back toward balance, and more training data is the surest cure for variance.

Why it matters: This single idea explains nearly every “my model looked great in training but failed in production” story. Diagnosing whether you have an underfitting or overfitting problem points you straight to the fix.

A concrete illustration

Suppose you want to classify whether a product review is positive or negative, and watch the right technique change as the problem scales.

  • A few hundred reviews, hand-labeled. Start simple: turn each review into word-count features and train a Naive Bayes classifier or a linear SVM. Fast, interpretable, and surprisingly strong on text. A deep network here would just overfit.
  • Tens of thousands of reviews. Now there is enough data to justify richer models. Try logistic regression with better features, or a random forest / gradient boosting ensemble. You might add PCA if the feature space is huge, to cut dimensionality before training.
  • Millions of reviews, plus unlabeled web text. Hand features hit their ceiling. Switch to deep learning: use a self-supervised pre-trained language model (trained on vast unlabeled text) and fine-tune it on your labeled reviews — transfer learning in action. This captures sentiment, sarcasm, and context no bag-of-words can.
  • A brand-new product category with no labeled reviews yet. Use the same pre-trained model in a zero-shot or few-shot way: describe the task in a prompt, or give it a handful of examples, and it classifies without a dedicated training run.

Notice the through-line: the data size and structure — not fashion — drive the choice, and the bias–variance balance is managed at every step (simple models for small data, regularization and more data as you scale up).

Key takeaways

  • Every ML project follows the same pipeline: data → features → model → training → evaluation → deployment. Most failures are workflow failures.
  • The learning paradigm (what supervision you have) is often a bigger decision than the algorithm. Map your problem to supervised, unsupervised, self-supervised, reinforcement, transfer, few/zero-shot, or federated learning before anything else.
  • The classical toolbox is not obsolete — kNN, Naive Bayes, SVMs, trees, forests, boosting, k-means, and PCA are the right answer for small, structured, or interpretability-critical problems.
  • Deep learning wins when data is large and unstructured (images, audio, text) and learned features beat hand-designed ones — but try a classical baseline first.
  • The bias–variance tradeoff is the lens for diagnosing models: high bias = underfitting (too simple), high variance = overfitting (too sensitive). Regularization and more data are your main levers.

Deep Learning — Neural Networks, Backpropagation and Training

In one line: A neural network stacks simple non-linear units into layers, a loss function scores how wrong its predictions are, and backpropagation plus gradient descent tune the weights to make that loss small.

What this chapter covers

  • The artificial neuron and the perceptron: how a unit turns inputs into an output.
  • Activation functions (sigmoid, tanh, ReLU, softmax) and why non-linearity is essential.
  • The multi-layer perceptron (MLP) / feed-forward network as a stack of these units.
  • Loss/cost functions: mean squared error for regression, cross-entropy for classification.
  • Gradient descent and its variants (batch, stochastic, mini-batch) and the weight-update rule.
  • Backpropagation: how the chain rule pushes error gradients back through the layers.
  • Optimizers that accelerate training: momentum, RMSProp, Adam.
  • The vanishing/exploding gradient problem in deep networks.
  • Regularization (L2 weight decay, dropout, early stopping) to fight overfitting.
  • Normalization (batch norm, layer norm) for faster, more stable training.
  • The everyday training knobs: epochs, learning rate, and the overfitting trade-off.

This chapter follows directly from the Week 1 preview, which introduces the first artificial model of a neuron and the perceptron, then the move to neurons with activation functions, the multi-layer feed-forward network, training by the “error path reduction” method (backpropagation) based on gradient descent and its stochastic variant, the optimization methods used to train deep networks, and the regularization (dropout) and normalization (batch/layer norm) techniques that make deep models trainable.

The artificial neuron and the perceptron

A single artificial neuron takes a vector of inputs \(\mathbf{x}=(x_1,\dots,x_d)\), multiplies each input by a learned weight, adds a bias, and produces a single number called the pre-activation:

\[z = \mathbf{w}^\top \mathbf{x} + b = \sum_{i=1}^{d} w_i x_i + b\]

That number is then passed through an activation function \(f\) to give the neuron’s output \(a = f(z)\). The weights \(\mathbf{w}\) say how much each input matters, and the bias \(b\) shifts the threshold at which the neuron “fires.”

The historical starting point is the perceptron, the very first artificial model of a neuron. It uses a step (sign) activation: output \(1\) if \(z>0\) and \(0\) (or \(-1\)) otherwise. With the perceptron learning rule, it can separate two classes that are linearly separable — that is, classes that can be split by a single straight line (or flat hyperplane in higher dimensions). On each mistake it nudges the weights toward the correct answer:

\[\mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} + \eta\,(y - \hat{y})\,\mathbf{x}\]

Why it matters: The neuron is the atom of every deep network. Understanding that it is just “a weighted sum followed by a non-linear squashing” demystifies the whole field — everything else is many of these wired together. The perceptron also exposes the key limitation that motivates everything that follows: a single linear unit cannot solve problems that are not linearly separable (the classic example being XOR).

Activation functions and why non-linearity matters

The activation function is the small non-linear step applied after the weighted sum. The common choices:

  • Sigmoid (logistic): \(\sigma(z)=\dfrac{1}{1+e^{-z}}\), squashes any input into \((0,1)\). Useful for interpreting an output as a probability, but it saturates (flattens) for large \(|z|\).
  • Tanh: \(\tanh(z)=\dfrac{e^{z}-e^{-z}}{e^{z}+e^{-z}}\), squashes into \((-1,1)\) and is zero-centered, which often trains better than sigmoid in hidden layers.
  • ReLU (rectified linear unit): \(\text{ReLU}(z)=\max(0,z)\). Cheap to compute, does not saturate for positive inputs, and is the default hidden-layer activation in modern networks.
  • Softmax (for the output layer of a multi-class classifier): turns a vector of scores into a probability distribution over \(K\) classes,

\[\text{softmax}(z)_k=\frac{e^{z_k}}{\sum_{j=1}^{K} e^{z_j}}\]

so the outputs are all positive and sum to \(1\).

Why it matters: Without a non-linear activation, stacking layers is pointless — a composition of linear maps is still just one linear map, so a 50-layer “deep” network would collapse into a single linear model and could only draw straight decision boundaries. The non-linearity is exactly what lets a network bend and fold its decision surface to fit complex data. (This is also why ReLU’s variants — leaky ReLU, GELU — exist: to keep the non-linearity while avoiding “dead” units that get permanently stuck at zero.)

The multi-layer perceptron (feed-forward network)

A multi-layer perceptron (MLP), or feed-forward neural network, arranges neurons in layers: an input layer, one or more hidden layers, and an output layer. Each layer’s outputs become the next layer’s inputs, flowing strictly forward (no loops). For layer \(\ell\) we compute everything at once in matrix form:

\[\mathbf{a}^{(\ell)} = f\!\left(W^{(\ell)} \mathbf{a}^{(\ell-1)} + \mathbf{b}^{(\ell)}\right)\]

where \(W^{(\ell)}\) is the weight matrix, \(\mathbf{b}^{(\ell)}\) the bias vector, and \(\mathbf{a}^{(0)}=\mathbf{x}\) is the input. Running input forward through these equations to get a prediction is the forward pass.

Why it matters: Hidden layers let the network learn intermediate features on its own — combinations of the raw inputs that make the final decision easy. The Universal Approximation Theorem says even one sufficiently wide hidden layer can approximate any continuous function, but in practice depth (more layers) is what makes networks learn rich, hierarchical representations efficiently. This MLP is the template the rest of deep learning (CNNs, RNNs, transformers) specializes for images, sequences, and text.

The loss (cost) function

The loss measures how wrong a prediction is; training means making it small on average over the data. Two standards:

  • Mean Squared Error (MSE) for regression (predicting a continuous number):

\[L_{\text{MSE}} = \frac{1}{N}\sum_{n=1}^{N}\left(y_n - \hat{y}_n\right)^2\]

  • Cross-entropy for classification (predicting a class). For multi-class with one-hot true labels \(y_{n,k}\) and predicted probabilities \(\hat{y}_{n,k}\) (from softmax):

\[L_{\text{CE}} = -\frac{1}{N}\sum_{n=1}^{N}\sum_{k=1}^{K} y_{n,k}\,\log \hat{y}_{n,k}\]

Why it matters: The loss is the single scalar the whole training process is trying to minimize — it is the “score” that defines what “good” means. Matching the loss to the task matters: cross-entropy pairs naturally with softmax and gives much stronger, better-behaved gradients for classification than MSE does, so the network learns faster and gets stuck less often.

Gradient descent and its variants

Training adjusts the parameters in the direction that most reduces the loss. The gradient \(\nabla_{\mathbf{w}} L\) points in the direction of steepest increase, so we step the opposite way, scaled by the learning rate \(\eta\):

\[\mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} - \eta\,\nabla_{\mathbf{w}} L \qquad b \leftarrow b - \eta\,\frac{\partial L}{\partial b}\]

How much data we use to estimate each gradient gives three variants:

  • Batch gradient descent: use the entire training set for every update. Accurate gradient, but slow and memory-hungry on large datasets.
  • Stochastic gradient descent (SGD): use one example per update. Very fast and noisy; the noise can actually help escape poor minima.
  • Mini-batch gradient descent: use a small batch (e.g., 32–256 examples) per update. The practical default — it balances stable gradients with speed and uses hardware (GPU) efficiently. (In common usage “SGD” usually means mini-batch SGD.)

Why it matters: This update rule is the engine of essentially all deep learning. The learning rate \(\eta\) is the most important hyperparameter you set: too large and training diverges or oscillates; too small and it crawls. Mini-batching is what makes training on millions of examples feasible at all.

Backpropagation (the chain rule through layers)

To run gradient descent we need the gradient of the loss with respect to every weight, even those buried in early layers. Backpropagation computes these efficiently. The intuition:

  1. Forward pass: push the input through the network and compute the loss.
  2. Backward pass: start at the output, compute how the loss changes with the output, then apply the chain rule to pass that “error signal” backward layer by layer — at each layer, multiply the incoming error by the layer’s local derivatives (the activation’s slope and the weights) to get the error at the layer before it.
  3. Along the way, each weight’s gradient is the error signal at its output side times the activation on its input side.

Symbolically, the chain rule for the error at a hidden unit is

\[\frac{\partial L}{\partial z^{(\ell)}} = \left(W^{(\ell+1)\top}\,\frac{\partial L}{\partial z^{(\ell+1)}}\right)\odot f'\!\left(z^{(\ell)}\right)\]

where \(\odot\) is element-wise multiplication and \(f'\) is the derivative of the activation. This is the “error path reduction” idea named in the Week 1 preview: the error is propagated backward along the same paths the signal traveled forward.

Why it matters: Backpropagation is what made training deep networks practical. It computes all gradients in roughly the same cost as one forward pass, by reusing intermediate results instead of recomputing derivatives from scratch. Every modern framework’s “autograd”/automatic differentiation is backpropagation under the hood.

Optimizers: momentum, RMSProp, Adam

Plain SGD can be slow in ravines and sensitive to the learning rate. Optimizers improve on the basic update:

  • Momentum: accumulate a running velocity of past gradients so updates keep rolling in a consistent direction and damp out oscillations.

\[\mathbf{v} \leftarrow \beta\,\mathbf{v} + (1-\beta)\,\nabla_{\mathbf{w}}L, \qquad \mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} - \eta\,\mathbf{v}\]

  • RMSProp: scale each weight’s step by a running average of its squared gradients, so parameters with large gradients take smaller steps and vice versa — an adaptive, per-parameter learning rate.
  • Adam (Adaptive Moment Estimation): combines momentum (first moment) and RMSProp (second moment), with bias correction. It is the most widely used default optimizer today:

\[\mathbf{m} \leftarrow \beta_1 \mathbf{m} + (1-\beta_1)\,\mathbf{g}, \quad \mathbf{v} \leftarrow \beta_2 \mathbf{v} + (1-\beta_2)\,\mathbf{g}^2, \quad \mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} - \eta\,\frac{\hat{\mathbf{m}}}{\sqrt{\hat{\mathbf{v}}}+\epsilon}\]

where \(\mathbf{g}=\nabla_{\mathbf{w}}L\) and \(\hat{\mathbf{m}},\hat{\mathbf{v}}\) are bias-corrected estimates.

Why it matters: Good optimizers mean faster, more reliable convergence with less fiddling of the learning rate. Adam in particular “just works” on a huge range of problems, which is why it is the go-to choice; the generalized delta rule and adaptive-moments method mentioned in Week 1 are exactly this family.

The vanishing and exploding gradient problem

When backprop multiplies many small numbers together through many layers, the gradient can shrink toward zero (vanishing) — so early layers barely learn. When it multiplies many large numbers, the gradient can blow up (exploding) — so training becomes unstable and diverges. Saturating activations like sigmoid (whose derivative is at most \(0.25\)) make vanishing especially likely in deep stacks.

Common remedies: use ReLU (its derivative is exactly \(1\) for positive inputs, so it doesn’t shrink the signal), careful weight initialization (e.g., Xavier/He scaling), gradient clipping (cap the gradient norm to stop explosions), normalization layers, and architectural shortcuts like residual (skip) connections that give gradients a direct path backward.

Why it matters: This problem is the single biggest reason naive deep networks failed to train for years. Nearly every modern design choice — ReLU, good initialization, batch/layer norm, residual connections — exists in part to keep gradients flowing cleanly through depth.

Regularization: fighting overfitting

Overfitting is when a model memorizes the training data (great training accuracy) but performs poorly on new data — it learned noise instead of the underlying pattern. Regularization techniques discourage this:

  • L2 weight decay: add a penalty on large weights to the loss, \(L_{\text{total}} = L + \lambda \sum_i w_i^2\), which keeps weights small and the function smoother. (Equivalent to shrinking the weights a little on every update.)
  • Dropout: during training, randomly “drop” (zero out) a fraction of units each step, so the network can’t rely on any single unit and must learn redundant, robust features. At test time all units are used.
  • Early stopping: monitor performance on a held-out validation set and stop training when validation loss stops improving, before the model starts memorizing.

Why it matters: A model is only useful if it generalizes to data it has never seen. As Week 1 stresses, overfitting “occurs almost in every machine learning technique,” and these methods are the standard toolkit for controlling it — dropout in particular is the deep-learning workhorse.

Normalization: faster, more stable training

Normalization rescales activations so their distribution stays well-behaved as it passes through the network:

  • Batch normalization: for each feature, normalize across the examples in the current mini-batch to zero mean and unit variance, then apply a learned scale and shift. It stabilizes the distribution of inputs each layer sees, allowing higher learning rates and faster convergence.
  • Layer normalization: normalize across the features within a single example instead of across the batch. It does not depend on batch size, which makes it the natural choice for sequence models and transformers.

A normalized feature is computed as \(\hat{x} = \dfrac{x-\mu}{\sqrt{\sigma^2+\epsilon}}\), followed by a learned \(\gamma\hat{x}+\beta\).

Why it matters: Normalization is one of the most effective practical tricks in deep learning — it speeds up training, reduces sensitivity to initialization and learning rate, and adds a mild regularizing effect. Batch norm dominates in CNNs; layer norm dominates in transformers and RNNs.

Epochs, learning rate, and the overfitting trade-off

A few terms tie the training loop together:

  • An epoch is one full pass over the entire training set. Training usually runs for many epochs, each made of many mini-batch updates.
  • The learning rate \(\eta\) controls step size; it is often scheduled to decrease over time (start large to make progress, shrink to settle into a good minimum).
  • More training reduces training loss, but past a point validation loss starts rising — that gap is overfitting. The art of training is finding the model and stopping point where validation performance is best.

Why it matters: These are the knobs you actually turn in practice. Reading training vs. validation curves tells you whether to train longer, add regularization, or change the learning rate.

A worked illustration

Take a tiny neuron with one input, sigmoid activation, and MSE loss. Suppose \(x = 1\), current weight \(w = 0.5\), bias \(b = 0\), and the target is \(y = 1\).

Forward pass. - Pre-activation: \(z = wx + b = 0.5(1) + 0 = 0.5\). - Output: \(\hat{y} = \sigma(0.5) = \dfrac{1}{1+e^{-0.5}} \approx 0.622\). - Loss: \(L = (y-\hat{y})^2 = (1-0.622)^2 \approx 0.143\).

Backward pass (chain rule, using \(\sigma'(z)=\sigma(z)(1-\sigma(z))\)): - \(\dfrac{\partial L}{\partial \hat{y}} = -2(y-\hat{y}) = -2(0.378) \approx -0.756\). - \(\sigma'(0.5) = 0.622(1-0.622) \approx 0.235\). - \(\dfrac{\partial L}{\partial z} = \dfrac{\partial L}{\partial \hat{y}}\cdot \sigma'(z) \approx (-0.756)(0.235) \approx -0.178\). - \(\dfrac{\partial L}{\partial w} = \dfrac{\partial L}{\partial z}\cdot x \approx -0.178 \times 1 = -0.178\).

Gradient-descent update with learning rate \(\eta = 0.1\):

\[w \leftarrow w - \eta\,\frac{\partial L}{\partial w} = 0.5 - 0.1(-0.178) = 0.5178\]

The weight grew slightly. Check that this helped: with \(w = 0.5178\), \(z = 0.5178\), \(\hat{y} = \sigma(0.5178) \approx 0.627\), so the prediction moved closer to the target \(1\) and the loss dropped to about \((1-0.627)^2 \approx 0.139\). Repeat this forward–backward–update cycle over many examples and epochs, and the network steadily lowers its loss. That is all training is.

Key takeaways

  • A neuron computes \(z=\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}+b\) then a non-linear activation; an MLP is just many of these stacked into layers.
  • Non-linear activations (ReLU, sigmoid, tanh, softmax) are what give depth its power — without them the whole network collapses to a single linear map.
  • The loss function defines “wrong” (MSE for regression, cross-entropy for classification) and is the scalar training minimizes.
  • Gradient descent updates weights via \(\mathbf{w}\leftarrow\mathbf{w}-\eta\nabla_{\mathbf{w}}L\); mini-batch SGD is the practical default, and the learning rate is the key knob.
  • Backpropagation uses the chain rule to compute every gradient in one efficient backward pass — it is the algorithm that makes deep learning trainable.
  • Optimizers (momentum, RMSProp, Adam) speed up and stabilize convergence; Adam is the common default.
  • Vanishing/exploding gradients are the core obstacle of depth, addressed by ReLU, good initialization, normalization, and residual connections.
  • Regularization (L2, dropout, early stopping) and normalization (batch/layer norm) are the standard tools for generalization and stable, fast training.

Deep Learning — Convolutional Neural Networks (CNNs)

In one line: CNNs are neural networks that learn visual patterns by sliding small filters across an image, reusing the same weights everywhere so they can recognise objects regardless of where in the picture they appear.

What this chapter covers

  • Why ordinary fully-connected networks break down on images, and what “spatial structure” means.
  • The convolution operation: filters/kernels, feature maps, and the three big ideas (local receptive fields, parameter sharing, translation invariance).
  • The knobs that control convolution: stride and padding.
  • Pooling for downsampling, and how a full CNN is stacked together.
  • The landmark architectures everyone names: LeNet, AlexNet, VGG, GoogLeNet/Inception, ResNet.
  • What CNNs are used for: classification, object detection (YOLO, bounding boxes), and segmentation (U-Net).
  • Beyond 2D: 1D convolution for sequences/audio and 3D convolution for video.

Why fully-connected networks fail on images

A plain (“fully-connected” or “dense”) neural network connects every input to every neuron in the next layer. That is fine for a short feature vector, but an image is huge. A modest colour photo of \(224 \times 224\) pixels with 3 colour channels (red, green, blue) is \(224 \times 224 \times 3 = 150{,}528\) numbers. If the first hidden layer had just 1,000 neurons, you would need about \(150{,}528 \times 1000 \approx 150\) million weights in the first layer alone. The model becomes enormous, slow to train, and prone to overfitting (memorising the training images instead of generalising).

Worse, a dense layer throws away the spatial structure of the image. To feed an image into a dense layer you first “flatten” it into one long list of numbers, which destroys the fact that neighbouring pixels belong together. A network that has to learn from scratch that pixel 5 and pixel 6 are neighbours — and relearn that fact for every position — is fighting the problem the hard way. And if the same object shifts a few pixels to the right, a dense network sees a completely different input and may fail to recognise it.

Why it matters: Images have two properties dense layers ignore — they are large and they are local and repeatable (an edge is an edge wherever it appears). CNNs are designed around exactly these properties, which is why they replaced dense networks for vision.

The convolution operation

The core idea is to slide a small window — called a filter or kernel — across the image. A typical filter is tiny, say \(3 \times 3\) or \(5 \times 5\) pixels. At each position, you place the filter over a small patch of the image, multiply each filter weight by the pixel underneath it, add up all those products (plus a bias), and write the single resulting number into an output grid. Slide the filter one step, repeat, and you fill out the whole output. This output grid is called a feature map (or activation map).

Mathematically, for one output position the value is \[y = \sum_{i}\sum_{j} w_{ij}\, x_{ij} + b,\] where \(w_{ij}\) are the filter weights, \(x_{ij}\) are the pixels under the filter, and \(b\) is a bias. This is just a weighted sum of a local patch — a dot product between the filter and the patch.

A filter responds strongly when the patch underneath it looks like the pattern the filter encodes. One filter might fire on vertical edges, another on a patch of a certain colour, another on a corner. A convolutional layer holds many filters (say 64), so it produces many feature maps — one per filter. Stack those feature maps and the layer’s output is itself a small “image” with many channels, ready to be fed into the next convolutional layer, which learns to combine simple patterns into more complex ones (edges → textures → shapes → object parts).

Why it matters: Convolution is the operation that lets a network detect a pattern using only a handful of weights, and reuse that same detector everywhere in the image. The lecture notes this is “a basic operation in signal processing called the convolution operation — that’s the reason it’s called the convolutional neural network.”

Local receptive fields, parameter sharing, translation invariance

Three ideas make convolution powerful, and they are worth naming separately:

  • Local receptive fields. Each output neuron looks at only a small patch of the input (the filter’s window), not the whole image. This matches how vision works — to detect an edge you only need to look at a tiny neighbourhood, not the entire scene.
  • Parameter sharing. The same filter weights are used at every position as the filter slides across the image. So instead of learning separate weights for every pixel location, the network learns one small set of weights per filter and reuses it everywhere. This is the source of the massive parameter savings: a \(3 \times 3\) filter has just 9 weights (plus a bias) regardless of image size.
  • Translation invariance (more precisely, equivariance). Because the same filter scans the whole image, a pattern is detected the same way no matter where it appears. A cat in the top-left and a cat in the bottom-right trigger the same filters. Pooling (below) then helps the final decision become insensitive to small shifts.

Why it matters: These three properties are exactly what was missing from dense networks. They cut the parameter count by orders of magnitude and build in the prior knowledge that visual patterns are local and position-independent — which is why CNNs generalise so well and need far less data than a dense network would for the same task.

Stride and padding

Two settings control how the filter sweeps across the image:

  • Stride is the step size. A stride of 1 moves the filter one pixel at a time and produces a feature map almost as large as the input. A stride of 2 skips every other position, halving the output’s width and height — a quick way to shrink (downsample) the data.
  • Padding adds a border of zeros around the image before convolving. Without padding, the filter cannot be centred on edge pixels, so the output shrinks slightly and edge information is under-used. “Same” padding adds just enough zeros so the output is the same size as the input; “valid” padding adds none and lets the output shrink.

For an input of width \(W\), filter size \(F\), padding \(P\), and stride \(S\), the output width is \[W_{\text{out}} = \left\lfloor \frac{W - F + 2P}{S} \right\rfloor + 1.\]

Why it matters: Stride and padding are how you control the size of each layer’s output and how much spatial detail you keep. They are small knobs with a big effect on both accuracy and computational cost.

Pooling (downsampling)

After convolution, a pooling layer shrinks each feature map while keeping the important information. The most common is max pooling: slide a small window (often \(2 \times 2\)) over the feature map and keep only the largest value in each window, discarding the rest. Average pooling keeps the average of the window instead. A \(2 \times 2\) pooling with stride 2 halves the width and height, cutting the number of values by four.

Pooling does two things. It reduces the amount of computation for later layers, and it makes the representation a little more robust to small shifts and distortions — if the strongest response moves by one pixel, max pooling often returns the same value. This adds to the network’s tolerance for the kind of variation the W10 lecture highlights, where the same object appears in different positions, sizes, and angles.

Why it matters: Pooling is the network’s “zoom out” step. It throws away precise location detail (which we usually don’t need) in exchange for a smaller, more stable summary — letting deeper layers reason about larger regions of the image.

A typical CNN stack

A standard CNN is a repeated pattern. A common building block is: \[\text{convolution} \rightarrow \text{activation (ReLU)} \rightarrow \text{pooling}.\] This block is repeated several times, each repetition working on a smaller, deeper representation that captures more abstract features. The activation is almost always ReLU (Rectified Linear Unit), defined as \(\text{ReLU}(x) = \max(0, x)\) — it simply zeroes out negatives. ReLU is fast and avoids some training problems of older sigmoid activations; the W1 overview calls these “ReLU units.”

After the convolutional and pooling blocks have extracted features, the resulting feature maps are flattened and fed into one or more fully-connected layers, and finally a softmax layer turns the network’s outputs into class probabilities that sum to 1. The whole pipeline reads: \[\text{conv} \rightarrow \text{ReLU} \rightarrow \text{pool} \rightarrow \dots \rightarrow \text{flatten} \rightarrow \text{fully-connected} \rightarrow \text{softmax}.\] The convolutional part is the “feature extractor”; the dense part at the end is the “classifier.”

Why it matters: This stack is the template behind almost every image classifier. Understanding it lets you read any CNN architecture as variations on the same theme: stack feature extractors, then classify.

Landmark architectures (named simply)

Vision progress is told through a few famous networks, all of which appear in the W1 overview:

  • LeNet (1998). The original CNN, built by Yann LeCun to read handwritten digits (like postal ZIP codes). Small, with a couple of conv+pool blocks and dense layers. It proved the whole idea works.
  • AlexNet (2012). A deeper, wider LeNet that won the ImageNet competition by a huge margin and kicked off the modern deep-learning era. It popularised ReLU, dropout, and training on GPUs.
  • VGG (2014). Showed that depth helps and that you can build everything from small \(3 \times 3\) filters stacked many times. Simple and uniform, but heavy (many parameters).
  • GoogLeNet / Inception (2014). Introduced the “Inception module,” which runs filters of several sizes in parallel at each layer and combines them, getting more accuracy with far fewer parameters than VGG.
  • ResNet (2015) and skip connections. As networks got very deep, they paradoxically became harder to train (the signal degraded). ResNet added skip connections (also called residual or shortcut connections) that let a layer’s input bypass a block and be added back to its output. The block then only has to learn the difference (the “residual”). This simple trick made it possible to train networks hundreds of layers deep. The W1 lecture refers to this family as well (LeNet, VGGNet, GoogLeNet, ResNet).

Why it matters: These names are the vocabulary of computer vision. Each introduced one durable idea — depth (VGG), parallel multi-scale filters (Inception), and skip connections (ResNet) — that still appears in today’s models.

CNNs for different tasks

A CNN’s feature extractor can be pointed at several different visual problems:

  • Image classification. Assign one label to the whole image (“this is a tiger”). This is the task the W10 lecture opens with, including the challenge of intra-class variability (tigers look different across photos) and inter-class similarity (a cheetah resembles a tiger). The standard CNN stack ending in softmax handles this.
  • Object detection. Find and locate every object, drawing a bounding box around each and labelling it (the W10 example of marking people in blue boxes and bicycles in red). A leading approach is YOLO (“You Only Look Once”), which runs the whole image through a CNN once and predicts all boxes and classes in a single pass, making it fast enough for real-time use.
  • Segmentation. Label every pixel — outline exactly which pixels belong to the cat (the W10 segmentation example). U-Net is the classic architecture: an encoder path that downsamples to capture context, a decoder path that upsamples back to full resolution, and skip connections that copy fine detail across so the output boundaries stay sharp. Its U-shape gives it its name.

Why it matters: The same convolutional backbone, with different “heads” on top, solves a whole family of vision tasks. Knowing which task you have (one label, boxes, or per-pixel masks) tells you which architecture to reach for.

Beyond 2D: 1D and 3D convolution

Convolution is not limited to flat images. The filter just slides over whatever dimensions the data has:

  • 1D convolution slides a filter along a single axis — time. This suits sequences and audio: a 1D CNN can scan an audio waveform or a sequence of words/feature vectors, detecting local patterns over a short window of time. The W1 overview notes “a one-dimensional version of CNN can be used for processing the text and the audio data.”
  • 3D convolution slides a filter through width, height, and time. This suits video, which is a stack of image frames. A 3D CNN can pick up motion patterns across consecutive frames, not just appearance within one frame. The W1 overview calls these “three-dimensional CNNs” used for video classification.

Why it matters: The convolution idea — local filters, shared weights — transfers to any data with local structure. The dimensionality (1D, 2D, 3D) just follows the shape of the signal: time, image, or video.

A concrete illustration

Picture a small grayscale image, \(6 \times 6\) pixels, so its shape is \(6 \times 6 \times 1\) (height \(\times\) width \(\times\) channels; one channel because it’s gray). We want to find vertical edges, so we use a simple \(3 \times 3\) edge-detecting filter:

\[ \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 0 & -1 \\ 1 & 0 & -1 \\ 1 & 0 & -1 \end{bmatrix} \]

This filter compares the brightness on its left side against its right side. Slide it over the image with stride 1 and no padding:

  1. Place the filter on the top-left \(3 \times 3\) patch.
  2. Multiply and sum: if the left column of the patch is bright and the right column is dark (a vertical edge), the left \(+1\)s pile up and the right \(-1\)s subtract little, giving a large positive number. If the patch is uniform (no edge), the \(+1\)s and \(-1\)s cancel and the output is near zero.
  3. Write that single number into the output feature map, slide one pixel right, and repeat across the whole image.

With a \(6 \times 6\) input, a \(3 \times 3\) filter, stride 1, and no padding, the output feature map is \(\frac{6-3}{1}+1 = 4\) on each side — a \(4 \times 4\) map that lights up exactly where vertical edges occur and is dark elsewhere. Notice that the same nine weights detected edges across the entire image (parameter sharing), and each output looked at only a \(3 \times 3\) neighbourhood (local receptive field).

Next, apply ReLU to keep only positive edge responses, then max pool with a \(2 \times 2\) window and stride 2. The \(4 \times 4\) map becomes \(2 \times 2\) — each pooled value is the strongest edge response in its quarter of the map. We have gone from \(6 \times 6\) pixels to a tiny \(2 \times 2\) summary that says “there are strong vertical edges roughly here and here,” shrinking the data while keeping what matters.

In a real network this is just the first step: a layer would have many filters (vertical edges, horizontal edges, colours, corners…), producing many feature maps stacked into a tensor like \(H \times W \times C\) (for example \(32 \times 32 \times 64\) — 64 feature maps). The next convolutional layer treats those 64 maps as its input channels and learns to combine simple edges into corners, then corners into object parts, and so on up the stack until the final softmax names the object.

Key takeaways

  • Dense networks fail on images because they need far too many parameters and ignore spatial structure; CNNs fix both by using small, shared filters over local patches.
  • Convolution slides a filter over the input to produce a feature map; a layer has many filters and produces many feature maps that deeper layers combine into higher-level features.
  • The three pillars are local receptive fields, parameter sharing, and translation invariance — together they slash the parameter count and bake in the right prior for vision.
  • Stride and padding control output size; pooling (usually max) downsamples and adds robustness to small shifts.
  • A standard CNN repeats conv → ReLU → pool, then ends in fully-connected → softmax; the conv part extracts features, the dense part classifies.
  • Landmark architectures each contributed one durable idea: LeNet (proof of concept), AlexNet (the deep-learning takeoff), VGG (depth with small filters), GoogLeNet/Inception (parallel multi-scale filters), ResNet (skip connections enabling very deep networks).
  • The same backbone powers many tasks: classification, object detection (YOLO, bounding boxes), and segmentation (U-Net), while 1D convolution handles audio/sequences and 3D convolution handles video.

Deep Learning — Recurrent Networks (RNN, LSTM, GRU)

In one line: Recurrent networks process data that comes as a sequence by carrying a memory (a hidden state) from one step to the next, so that what the model saw earlier shapes how it reads what comes later.

What this chapter covers

  • Why ordinary (feed-forward) networks struggle with sequences, and why text, speech, and time series need memory.
  • The recurrent neuron and the hidden state — the core recurrence equation and what each piece does.
  • Unrolling a recurrent network through time and training it with backpropagation through time (BPTT).
  • The vanishing and exploding gradient problem that makes plain RNNs forget long-range information.
  • LSTM cells (forget / input / output gates and the cell state) explained in intuition, not just equations.
  • GRU, a simpler gated variant that often works just as well.
  • Bidirectional and deep / stacked RNNs.
  • Sequence-to-sequence models with an encoder–decoder, the backbone of machine translation.
  • Where RNNs are used, and why Transformers have largely replaced them.

Why sequences need memory

Most of the early machine learning models you meet — a Bayes classifier, an SVM, or a plain feed-forward neural network — take a single fixed-size feature vector as input and produce one output. That works well for a static pattern: one image, one row of numbers, one snapshot.

But a lot of the data humans care about is not a single snapshot — it is an ordered sequence:

  • Text: a sentence is a sequence of words; the meaning depends on order. “The food was good, not bad at all” is positive, while “The food was bad, not good at all” — the same words in a different order — is negative.
  • Speech: an utterance is a sequence of audio frames over time.
  • Time series: stock prices, sensor readings, or weather measurements arrive one time step after another.

Two things make sequences hard for a plain feed-forward network:

  1. Variable length. Different sentences have different numbers of words. A fixed-size input layer cannot naturally accept inputs of different lengths.
  2. Order and context matter. The interpretation of a word depends on the words before it. To classify “not bad,” the model must remember the “not” while it reads “bad.”

A feed-forward network has no notion of “what came before” — it treats each input independently. A recurrent neural network (RNN) fixes this by adding a loop: it processes one element of the sequence at a time and keeps a running summary, the hidden state, that is passed forward to the next step. That hidden state is the network’s memory.

Why it matters: Without memory you cannot model context, and without modelling context you cannot understand language, speech, or any signal that unfolds over time. The recurrent loop is the simplest mechanism that gives a neural network a sense of “before” and “after.”

The recurrent neuron and the hidden state

At each time step \(t\) the RNN receives the current input \(\mathbf{x}_t\) (for text, the vector representing the \(t\)-th word) and the previous hidden state \(h_{t-1}\), and computes a new hidden state:

\[h_t = f\big(\mathbf{W}_x \mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{W}_h\, h_{t-1} + b\big)\]

Reading this piece by piece:

  • \(\mathbf{x}_t\) — the input at this step.
  • \(h_{t-1}\) — the memory carried over from all previous steps.
  • \(\mathbf{W}_x\) — weights applied to the new input.
  • \(\mathbf{W}_h\) — weights applied to the carried-over memory.
  • \(b\) — a bias term.
  • \(f\) — a nonlinear activation, classically \(\tanh\) (early RNNs used sigmoidal-type activations).

The output at each step (for example, a predicted next word or a class) is then read off from the hidden state, typically with another weight matrix and a softmax:

\[\hat{y}_t = \text{softmax}\big(\mathbf{W}_y\, h_t + b_y\big)\]

The single most important detail is that the same weights \(\mathbf{W}_x\), \(\mathbf{W}_h\), \(b\) are reused at every time step. The network does not learn a different rule for word 1, word 2, word 3; it learns one update rule and applies it repeatedly. This weight sharing is what lets a single RNN handle sequences of any length, and it dramatically reduces the number of parameters.

Why it matters: The hidden state \(h_t\) is a compressed summary of everything the model has seen up to time \(t\). Each new step folds the latest input into that summary. This is the whole idea of a recurrent network in one equation.

Unrolling through time

The loop is convenient to draw but awkward to reason about. So we unroll (or “unfold”) the RNN: we lay out one copy of the cell for each time step, feeding \(h_{t-1}\) into the next copy.

x₁      x₂      x₃            x_T
 │       │       │             │
 ▼       ▼       ▼             ▼
[A] ──► [A] ──► [A] ──► ... ──► [A]
 │  h₁   │  h₂   │  h₃          │  h_T
 ▼       ▼       ▼             ▼
ŷ₁      ŷ₂      ŷ₃            ŷ_T

Each box [A] is the same cell with the same weights; the arrows carrying \(h_t\) are the memory flowing left to right. An unrolled RNN looks exactly like a very deep feed-forward network — but one whose depth equals the length of the sequence, and whose layers all share weights.

Different tasks use different slices of this picture:

  • Many-to-one (e.g. sentiment classification): read the whole sentence, output one label at the end.
  • Many-to-many, aligned (e.g. part-of-speech tagging, named entity recognition): output one label per input word.
  • Many-to-many, unaligned (e.g. machine translation): read the whole input, then produce an output sequence of a possibly different length (handled by the encoder–decoder design below).

Why it matters: Unrolling turns “a network with a loop” into “a deep network we already know how to train,” which is exactly what makes the next step — backpropagation through time — possible.

Backpropagation through time (BPTT)

To train any neural network we need gradients: how much each weight should change to reduce the loss. For a feed-forward net this is ordinary backpropagation. For an RNN we apply the same idea to the unrolled network — this is called backpropagation through time (BPTT).

The recipe:

  1. Forward pass: run the sequence through, step by step, computing every \(h_t\) and the loss at each output.
  2. Backward pass: propagate the error gradients backward through the unrolled chain — from the last time step back toward the first.
  3. Accumulate and update: because the same weights \(\mathbf{W}_x, \mathbf{W}_h\) are used at every step, the gradient contributions from all time steps are summed before a single update is made.

In practice, very long sequences are split into chunks and the backward pass is limited to a fixed window — this is truncated BPTT — to keep memory and computation manageable.

Why it matters: BPTT is simply backpropagation applied to a network unrolled in time. But the long chain it creates is exactly where the central weakness of plain RNNs comes from, which is the next topic.

The vanishing and exploding gradient problem

Because the unrolled RNN can be hundreds of steps deep, the backward pass multiplies many derivative terms together as the gradient travels back through time. Repeated multiplication has two failure modes:

  • Vanishing gradients: if the multiplied factors are smaller than 1, the gradient shrinks toward zero as it travels back. The result: the network cannot learn long-range dependencies — by the time the error signal reaches the early words, it is too faint to update them. The model effectively forgets the distant past.
  • Exploding gradients: if the factors are larger than 1, the gradient blows up to huge values, and training becomes unstable (weights swing wildly, loss becomes NaN).

A concrete example of the long-range problem: in the sentence “The trophy would not fit in the brown suitcase because it was too big,” deciding what “it” refers to requires reaching all the way back to “trophy.” A plain RNN’s gradient often vanishes before it can wire that connection.

Some partial remedies exist — gradient clipping (capping the gradient norm) tames exploding gradients, and using \(\tanh\)/ReLU-style activations or careful initialization helps a little. But the fundamental cure for vanishing gradients on long sequences is a better cell design: the LSTM.

Why it matters: This single problem is why “vanilla” RNNs perform poorly on anything but short sequences, and it is the reason LSTMs and GRUs were invented.

LSTM cells — intuition

The Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM) unit redesigns the recurrent cell so that information can flow across many time steps without being repeatedly squashed. Its key innovation is a separate cell state \(C_t\) — a kind of conveyor belt of memory that runs straight through the sequence with only minor, gated edits at each step. Because information can ride this belt with little change, gradients can travel back across many steps without vanishing.

The LSTM controls what happens to this memory using three gates. A gate is a small neural layer with a sigmoid output between 0 and 1 that decides “how much” of a signal to let through (0 = block completely, 1 = let through fully):

  • Forget gate \(f_t\) — looks at the previous hidden state and current input and decides what to erase from the cell state. (“We just saw a new subject; forget the gender of the old one.”)
  • Input gate \(i_t\) — decides what new information to write into the cell state. (“Store that the new subject is plural.”)
  • Output gate \(o_t\) — decides how much of the cell state to expose as the hidden state / output at this step.

A common way to write it:

\[f_t = \sigma(\mathbf{W}_f[h_{t-1},\mathbf{x}_t]+b_f), \quad i_t = \sigma(\mathbf{W}_i[h_{t-1},\mathbf{x}_t]+b_i), \quad o_t = \sigma(\mathbf{W}_o[h_{t-1},\mathbf{x}_t]+b_o)\]

\[\tilde{C}_t = \tanh(\mathbf{W}_C[h_{t-1},\mathbf{x}_t]+b_C)\]

\[C_t = f_t \odot C_{t-1} + i_t \odot \tilde{C}_t, \qquad h_t = o_t \odot \tanh(C_t)\]

Here \(\odot\) is element-wise multiplication and \(\tilde{C}_t\) is the candidate new memory. The line \(C_t = f_t \odot C_{t-1} + i_t \odot \tilde{C}_t\) is the heart of the LSTM: keep part of the old memory (scaled by the forget gate), add part of the new memory (scaled by the input gate). The additive update to \(C_t\) is precisely what keeps gradients from vanishing.

Why it matters: Gates let the network learn what to remember and what to forget over long spans, rather than being forced to squeeze everything through one \(\tanh\) at every step. This is what made RNNs genuinely useful for language and speech.

GRU — a simpler gated variant

The Gated Recurrent Unit (GRU) keeps the spirit of the LSTM but with fewer parts. It merges the cell state and hidden state into one, and uses just two gates:

  • Update gate \(z_t\) — decides how much of the past hidden state to keep versus how much new information to take in (it does the job of the LSTM’s forget and input gates combined).
  • Reset gate \(r_t\) — decides how much of the past to ignore when computing the new candidate.

\[z_t = \sigma(\mathbf{W}_z[h_{t-1},\mathbf{x}_t]), \quad r_t = \sigma(\mathbf{W}_r[h_{t-1},\mathbf{x}_t])\]

\[\tilde{h}_t = \tanh\big(\mathbf{W}[\,r_t \odot h_{t-1},\ \mathbf{x}_t\,]\big), \qquad h_t = (1-z_t)\odot h_{t-1} + z_t \odot \tilde{h}_t\]

Because a GRU has fewer gates and no separate cell state, it has fewer parameters, trains a bit faster, and needs less data — while in practice performing comparably to an LSTM on many tasks. LSTM versus GRU is usually an empirical choice: try both, keep whichever generalizes better.

Why it matters: The GRU shows that the gating idea — learnable, additive memory updates — is what matters, and that you can often get LSTM-level performance with a leaner, cheaper cell.

Bidirectional and deep / stacked RNNs

Bidirectional RNNs. A plain RNN reads left to right, so the state at word \(t\) only knows about words before it. But for understanding text, what comes after a word is just as informative — to tag “bank” in “river bank,” you need the word “river” which may appear on either side.
