A bidirectional RNN runs two RNNs: one forward over the sequence and one backward, then concatenates their hidden states. Now each position’s representation is informed by the entire sentence, both past and future. This is especially valuable for tagging, named entity recognition, and any task where the whole input is available at once. (It is not usable when you must generate outputs online, before the future is known.)

Deep / stacked RNNs. The simplest RNN has a single hidden (recurrent) layer. A deep RNN stacks several recurrent layers, feeding the hidden-state sequence of one layer as the input sequence to the next. Lower layers capture short, local patterns; higher layers capture more abstract, longer-range structure — much like depth helps in feed-forward and convolutional networks. Stacking and bidirectionality combine: deep bidirectional RNNs are a common choice for hard sequence-labelling problems.

Why it matters: Bidirectionality adds future context; depth adds abstraction. Together they push RNNs from toy demos to systems strong enough for real speech recognition and tagging.

Sequence-to-sequence with an encoder–decoder

Many of the most important NLP tasks — machine translation, summarization, question answering — map an input sequence to an output sequence of a different length, with no word-by-word alignment. (Translating English to Japanese even reorders Subject-Verb-Object into Subject-Object-Verb.) The sequence-to-sequence (seq2seq) architecture handles this with two RNNs:

  1. Encoder: an RNN (often a deep bidirectional LSTM/GRU) reads the entire input sequence and compresses it into a fixed-size summary vector — the context vector — usually its final hidden state.
  2. Decoder: a second RNN takes that context vector as its starting memory and generates the output sequence one token at a time, feeding each generated token back in as the next input until it emits an end-of-sequence marker.
   Encoder                         Decoder
x₁→x₂→x₃→x₄ ──► [context vector] ──► y₁→y₂→y₃→<end>

The weakness of the basic version is the bottleneck: cramming an entire long sentence into one fixed vector loses information. The fix was the attention mechanism, which lets the decoder look back at all of the encoder’s hidden states and focus on the most relevant input words at each output step. Attention dramatically improved translation — and, importantly, it was the seed of the Transformer.

Why it matters: Encoder–decoder is the template behind translation, summarization, and chat-style generation. Understanding its bottleneck is the cleanest way to understand why attention, and then Transformers, took over.

Where RNNs are used — and why Transformers replaced them

RNNs (especially LSTMs and GRUs) were, for years, the standard tool for sequential data:

  • Language modelling — predicting the next word/character; the basis of text generation.
  • Machine translation — the original seq2seq application.
  • Speech recognition — converting audio frames to text (deep bidirectional LSTMs were dominant).
  • Time-series forecasting — stock prices, demand, sensor signals.
  • Tagging tasks — part-of-speech tagging, named entity recognition, sentiment analysis.

Despite their success, RNNs have two inherent limitations:

  1. They are sequential, so they cannot be parallelized over time. Step \(t\) needs \(h_{t-1}\), so you must process the sequence one element after another — slow to train on modern hardware.
  2. Long-range dependencies remain hard. Even LSTMs and GRUs strain to connect information across very long distances; the signal still has to pass through every intermediate step.

The Transformer (introduced in 2017, “Attention Is All You Need”) drops recurrence entirely and relies on self-attention, which lets every position directly attend to every other position in one shot. This makes training fully parallel across the sequence and makes long-range connections direct rather than relayed step by step. Transformers — and the pre-trained models built on them, such as BERT and GPT — now dominate NLP and have spread to vision and speech as well.

Why it matters: RNNs taught the field how to model sequences and remain useful for streaming and resource-constrained settings, but their two structural limits are exactly the ones Transformers were designed to remove. Knowing the RNN story is what makes the Transformer’s design choices feel inevitable rather than arbitrary.

A concrete illustration: predicting the next character

Suppose we train a small RNN as a character-level language model on the word “hello,” teaching it to predict the next character given the characters seen so far.

We feed the characters one at a time. The hidden state acts as the model’s running memory of what it has read:

Step \(t\) Input \(\mathbf{x}_t\) Hidden state \(h_t\) (memory) Predicted next char \(\hat{y}_t\)
1 h summary of “h” e
2 e summary of “he” l
3 l summary of “hel” l
4 l summary of “hell” o

Notice steps 3 and 4: the input is the same character l both times, yet the correct prediction differs — first another l, then o. A memoryless model could never get both right, because the input alone is identical. The RNN succeeds because \(h_3\) and \(h_4\) are different: the hidden state remembers how far into the word we are. That carried-over context is exactly what the recurrence \(h_t = f(\mathbf{W}_x \mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{W}_h h_{t-1} + b)\) provides.

Scale this same idea up — from characters to words, from one word to whole documents, from a single layer to deep bidirectional LSTMs — and you get the language models, translators, and speech recognizers that RNNs powered before Transformers arrived.

Key takeaways

  • Sequences need memory. RNNs add a recurrent loop and a hidden state \(h_t = f(\mathbf{W}_x\mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{W}_h h_{t-1} + b)\) that summarizes everything seen so far, letting context shape later predictions.
  • Weight sharing across time lets one cell handle sequences of any length with few parameters; unrolling reveals the RNN as a deep, time-shared network trained by backpropagation through time (BPTT).
  • Vanishing/exploding gradients are the core weakness: plain RNNs cannot learn long-range dependencies. Gradient clipping tames exploding gradients; gated cells cure vanishing ones.
  • LSTMs use a cell state plus forget/input/output gates so memory can flow across many steps via additive updates; GRUs achieve similar results with two gates and fewer parameters.
  • Bidirectional RNNs add future context; deep/stacked RNNs add abstraction; encoder–decoder (seq2seq) maps one sequence to another and underpins translation, summarization, and QA.
  • Transformers replaced RNNs by using self-attention for full parallelism and direct long-range connections — but the RNN ideas of hidden state, gating, and encoder–decoder remain the conceptual foundation.

Deep Learning — Transformers and the Attention Mechanism

In one line: The Transformer replaced step-by-step recurrence with an attention mechanism that lets every word in a sequence look directly at every other word, making models that are easier to parallelize, better at long-range relationships, and the foundation of modern systems like BERT and GPT.

What this chapter covers

  • Why recurrent neural networks (RNNs) struggled, and what problem attention was invented to solve.
  • The core intuition of attention: each position decides how much to focus on every other position.
  • The query/key/value picture and the scaled dot-product attention formula.
  • Self-attention vs. cross-attention, and why “multiple heads” help.
  • Positional encoding — how a non-recurrent model still knows word order.
  • The Transformer block: attention + feed-forward + residual connections + layer normalization.
  • Why this design parallelizes and scales so well.
  • The three model families — encoder-only (BERT), decoder-only (GPT), encoder-decoder (T5).
  • The pre-training + fine-tuning recipe that powers almost all of them.

1. The limitation of RNNs that motivated attention

Earlier deep learning models for sequences — text, speech, time series — used recurrent neural networks (RNNs), including their stronger variants LSTM and GRU. An RNN reads a sentence one token at a time, maintaining a hidden “memory” vector that it updates at each step. Word 1 updates the memory, then word 2, then word 3, and so on.

This design has three painful limitations:

  • It is sequential. Step \(t\) cannot be computed until step \(t-1\) is done. You cannot process all words at once, so training is slow and does not fully exploit modern parallel hardware (GPUs).
  • Long-range memory fades. Information from early words has to survive being rewritten at every step. By the end of a long sentence, the influence of the first word has often been diluted or lost — the “vanishing gradient” problem. LSTMs ease this but do not eliminate it.
  • A fixed-size bottleneck. In translation, classic encoder-decoder RNNs compressed the entire source sentence into one fixed-length vector. Long or complex sentences simply do not fit.

Why it matters: These bottlenecks capped how well sequence models could perform and how large they could be trained. Attention removed all three at once, which is why it triggered a step-change in NLP and beyond.

2. The intuition of attention

The core idea is simple to state: each word looks at every other word in the sentence and decides what to focus on.

Instead of squeezing the whole sentence through a sequential memory, attention lets every position reach directly across the sentence and pull in exactly the information it needs. When the model builds a representation of the word “bank,” it can look at nearby words like “river” or “money” and weight them, deciding which context is relevant. Relevant words get a high weight; irrelevant ones get a low weight. The new representation of “bank” becomes a weighted blend of the words it chose to attend to.

Crucially, the distance between two words no longer matters — a word at position 2 can attend to a word at position 50 just as directly as to its neighbor. There is no chain of steps in between to lose the signal.

Why it matters: This single mechanism solves long-range dependencies (any-to-any connections) and, because all positions can be computed at the same time, removes the sequential bottleneck.

3. Query, key, value and scaled dot-product attention

Attention is made precise using three learned projections of each input vector, named by analogy to looking something up in a database:

  • Query (\(Q\)) — what this word is looking for.
  • Key (\(K\)) — what each word offers as a label/advertisement.
  • Value (\(V\)) — the actual content each word will contribute if attended to.

For one query, you compare it against every key (using a dot product) to get a similarity score. High similarity means “this word is relevant to me.” Those scores are turned into weights with a softmax (so they are positive and sum to 1), and the output is the weighted sum of the values.

Stacking all queries, keys, and values into matrices \(Q\), \(K\), \(V\), the whole operation is:

\[\text{Attention}(Q,K,V)=\text{softmax}\!\left(\frac{QK^\top}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right)V\]

Reading it piece by piece:

  • \(QK^\top\) — every query dotted with every key, producing a matrix of raw relevance scores (one row per word, one column per word).
  • \(\dfrac{1}{\sqrt{d_k}}\) — a scaling factor, where \(d_k\) is the dimension of the keys. Without it, dot products grow large when \(d_k\) is large, pushing the softmax into regions with tiny gradients. Dividing by \(\sqrt{d_k}\) keeps the scores in a healthy range. This is why it is called scaled dot-product attention.
  • \(\text{softmax}(\cdot)\) — turns each row of scores into attention weights between 0 and 1 that sum to 1.
  • multiplying by \(V\) — produces, for each word, a weighted average of all the value vectors.

Why it matters: This is the engine of the entire architecture. It is just matrix multiplications and a softmax — operations GPUs perform extremely fast in parallel — yet it expresses the rich “everyone looks at everyone” behavior.

4. Self-attention vs. cross-attention

The same formula serves two roles depending on where \(Q\), \(K\), and \(V\) come from:

  • Self-attention: \(Q\), \(K\), and \(V\) all come from the same sequence. The sentence attends to itself, letting each word gather context from the other words around it. This is how a model builds context-aware word representations.
  • Cross-attention: \(Q\) comes from one sequence while \(K\) and \(V\) come from another. In translation, the decoder (generating the target sentence) uses queries from the target while attending to keys and values from the encoded source sentence — this is how the output sentence “looks back” at the input it is translating.

Why it matters: Self-attention builds understanding within a sequence; cross-attention connects two sequences. Together they let one architecture handle understanding, generation, and translation.

5. Multi-head attention

Rather than computing attention once, the Transformer does it several times in parallel with different learned \(Q/K/V\) projections. Each parallel copy is called a head. The heads’ outputs are concatenated and passed through a final linear layer.

The intuition: one head might learn to track grammatical subject–verb agreement, another might resolve pronouns, another might link adjectives to the nouns they modify. A single attention computation would force all these relationships into one weighting; multiple heads let the model attend to different kinds of relationships in different “representation subspaces” simultaneously.

Why it matters: Multiple heads give the model several complementary views of the sentence at once, substantially improving what it can represent — at little extra cost, since each head works on a smaller slice of the dimensions.

6. Positional encoding

Attention has a subtle catch: it is permutation-invariant. Because it computes weighted sums over a set of words, it has no built-in notion of order — “dog bites man” and “man bites dog” would look identical to raw attention. RNNs got order for free from their step-by-step processing, but the Transformer has no recurrence.

The fix is positional encoding: a position-dependent vector is added to each word’s embedding before it enters the network, stamping each token with information about where it sits in the sequence. The original Transformer used fixed sinusoidal functions of different frequencies:

\[PE_{(pos,\,2i)} = \sin\!\left(\frac{pos}{10000^{\,2i/d}}\right), \qquad PE_{(pos,\,2i+1)} = \cos\!\left(\frac{pos}{10000^{\,2i/d}}\right)\]

where \(pos\) is the position and \(i\) indexes the dimension. Later models often use learned positional embeddings instead, but the purpose is the same: give the otherwise order-blind model a sense of sequence.

Why it matters: Word order carries meaning (recall the W9 example — “the food was good, not bad” vs. “the food was bad, not good,” same words, opposite sentiment). Positional encoding is what lets a non-recurrent model use that order.

7. The Transformer block: putting it together

A Transformer is a stack of identical blocks. Each block combines a small number of standard ingredients:

  1. Multi-head self-attention — mix information across positions.
  2. Position-wise feed-forward network — a small two-layer neural network applied to each position independently, adding non-linear processing power.
  3. Residual (skip) connections — the input of each sub-layer is added back to its output. This gives gradients a direct path and makes deep stacks trainable.
  4. Layer normalization — normalizes each layer’s activations to stabilize and speed up training.

A common way to write one sub-layer is:

\[\text{LayerNorm}\big(x + \text{Sublayer}(x)\big)\]

where \(\text{Sublayer}\) is either the attention or the feed-forward component.

The encoder–decoder layout (from the original “Attention Is All You Need” architecture) has two halves:

  • Encoder — a stack of blocks using self-attention to build a rich representation of the input sequence.
  • Decoder — a stack of blocks that uses masked self-attention (a token may only attend to earlier tokens, so it cannot “peek” at the future word it is supposed to predict) plus cross-attention to the encoder’s output, generating the result one token at a time.

Why it matters: This handful of reusable parts — attention, feed-forward, residuals, layer norm — stacks cleanly into very deep, very large models. Almost every modern large language model is some arrangement of these same blocks.

8. Why Transformers parallelize and scale

Because self-attention processes all positions at once (a few big matrix multiplications) rather than stepping through them one by one, an entire sentence — or a whole batch of sentences — can be computed in parallel during training. This maps perfectly onto GPU/TPU hardware.

That parallelism, combined with the architecture’s clean stackability, means Transformers keep improving as you add more layers, more parameters, and more data — the property that made today’s very large models practical.

Why it matters: Parallelism is the practical reason Transformers, not RNNs, became the substrate for large-scale models. Scaling them up reliably produces better performance.

9. The three model families

The same building blocks, arranged differently, give three families suited to different tasks:

  • Encoder-only — e.g. BERT (Bidirectional Encoder Representations from Transformers). Uses only the encoder, so every token sees context on both sides at once. Excellent for understanding tasks: classification, sentiment analysis, named entity recognition, question answering over a passage.
  • Decoder-only — e.g. GPT (Generative Pre-trained Transformer). Uses only the decoder with masked self-attention, predicting the next token given everything before it. Excellent for generation: writing text, completing prompts, conversation (ChatGPT is a fine-tuned descendant of this line).
  • Encoder-decoder — e.g. T5 (and the original Transformer). Keeps both halves, framing problems as “text in → text out.” Natural for sequence-to-sequence tasks like machine translation and summarization, where an input must be understood and then a new sequence generated.

Why it matters: Whether a task needs deep bidirectional understanding, fluent left-to-right generation, or a translation from one sequence to another, there is a Transformer variant whose structure matches it.

10. Pre-training + fine-tuning

A Transformer has a huge number of parameters. Training one from scratch for a specific task would require an impractical amount of labeled data. The standard recipe sidesteps this in two phases:

  • Pre-training — train the model on enormous amounts of unlabeled text using self-supervised objectives, where the text supplies its own labels. BERT masks out random words and learns to fill them in; GPT learns to predict the next word. No human annotation is needed, so the model can learn from essentially the whole web and absorb broad knowledge of language.
  • Fine-tuning — take the pre-trained model and continue training it briefly on a smaller labeled dataset for a specific downstream task (e.g. sentiment classification or medical question answering).

Why it matters: This is a form of transfer learning. The expensive, general-knowledge phase is done once; adapting to each new task is cheap and needs comparatively little labeled data. It is the reason a handful of large pre-trained models can be specialized for thousands of applications.

A concrete illustration

Take the sentence:

“The trophy did not fit in the suitcase because it was too big.”

What does “it” refer to — the trophy or the suitcase? A human resolves this instantly: a trophy too big won’t fit, so “it” must be the trophy. (Swap “big” for “small” and the answer flips to the suitcase.)

Here is how self-attention handles it. When the model processes the word “it,” that word’s query vector is compared against the keys of every other word. The dot products produce relevance scores, the softmax turns them into attention weights, and those weights determine which words “it” pulls its meaning from. Trained well, the model places a high attention weight from “it” onto “trophy” and a lower one onto “suitcase,” partly because it has also attended to “big.” The output representation of “it” then becomes mostly a blend of the trophy’s value vector — the model has, in effect, resolved the pronoun.

Notice what made this possible: “it” attended directly to “trophy” several words back, in a single step, with no fading memory in between. With one of the multi-head attention heads possibly specializing in exactly this kind of reference resolution, the whole sentence is processed at once. That is the attention mechanism doing its job.

Key takeaways

  • RNNs were sequential and forgetful. Their step-by-step processing prevented parallelism and weakened long-range memory; attention was invented to fix both.
  • Attention = each position looks at every other position and takes a weighted blend, with weights set by how relevant each other position is.
  • The whole mechanism is one formula: \(\text{Attention}(Q,K,V)=\text{softmax}\!\left(\frac{QK^\top}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right)V\) — queries score against keys, softmax makes weights, weights blend the values.
  • Self-attention builds context within a sequence; cross-attention links two sequences; multiple heads capture several kinds of relationships at once.
  • Positional encoding restores word order, which raw attention would otherwise ignore.
  • A Transformer block = multi-head attention + feed-forward + residual connections + layer normalization, stacked deep.
  • Transformers parallelize, so they scale up with more data and parameters — the reason they replaced RNNs at large scale.
  • Three families: encoder-only (BERT) for understanding, decoder-only (GPT) for generation, encoder-decoder (T5) for sequence-to-sequence.
  • Pre-train then fine-tune: learn language broadly from unlabeled text once, then cheaply specialize to each task with a little labeled data.

Generative AI — GANs, Diffusion and Large Language Models

In one line: Generative AI is the family of models that create new data — text, images, audio — by learning the underlying patterns of a training set, rather than just labelling existing data.

What this chapter covers

  • The split between discriminative models (predict a label) and generative models (produce new data).
  • Autoencoders and variational autoencoders (VAEs) as a first way to learn a compact, generative representation.
  • Generative Adversarial Networks (GANs): the generator-vs-discriminator game.
  • Diffusion models: add noise, then learn to remove it — the engine behind Stable Diffusion and DALL·E.
  • Large Language Models (LLMs): next-token prediction, pre-training, tokens and embeddings, emergent abilities, fine-tuning, instruction tuning, RLHF, prompting / in-context learning, RAG, and hallucination.
  • Text-to-image and multimodal models that connect language with other kinds of data.

Discriminative vs generative models

A discriminative model learns to tell classes apart. Given an input \(x\) (an image, a sentence), it estimates \(P(y \mid x)\) — the probability of a label \(y\). A spam filter, an image classifier, and a sentiment detector are all discriminative: they draw a boundary between categories.

A generative model instead learns what the data itself looks like. It models the distribution \(P(x)\) (or \(P(x \mid y)\), the data for a given class), so that it can sample new examples that resemble the training data — a new face, a new paragraph, a new melody. Where a discriminative model answers “which class is this?”, a generative model answers “what does a plausible example look like?”.

Why it matters: Almost every “AI that makes something” — ChatGPT writing text, an image model painting a scene — is generative. Understanding the \(P(y\mid x)\) vs \(P(x)\) distinction is the cleanest way to see why generation is a fundamentally different (and harder) task than classification.

Autoencoders and variational autoencoders (VAEs)

An autoencoder is a neural network trained to copy its input to its output through a narrow middle layer. An encoder compresses the input \(x\) into a small latent vector \(z\); a decoder reconstructs \(\hat{x}\) from \(z\). By forcing everything through the bottleneck, the network is pushed to keep only the most important structure — a learned, compressed code of the data.

A plain autoencoder is good at compression but not at generating: its latent space has “holes”, so a random \(z\) usually decodes to nonsense. A variational autoencoder (VAE) fixes this by encoding each input not as a single point but as a small probability distribution (typically a Gaussian with mean \(\mu\) and variance \(\sigma^2\)). Training encourages this latent space to be smooth and continuous, so you can sample a random \(z\) and decode it into a brand-new, realistic example. The loss combines a reconstruction term (the output should match the input) with a regularisation term (the latent distribution should stay close to a standard Gaussian).

Why it matters: VAEs introduced the central idea of generative modelling — learn a tidy latent space, then sample from it to create new data. That “compress to a latent code, then generate from it” pattern reappears, in a more powerful form, inside modern diffusion image generators.

Generative Adversarial Networks (GANs)

A GAN trains two networks that compete: - The generator \(G\) takes random noise \(z\) and tries to turn it into a realistic fake sample \(G(z)\). - The discriminator \(D\) is shown a mix of real data and the generator’s fakes, and tries to tell which is which.

They improve by working against each other: the discriminator gets better at spotting fakes, which forces the generator to produce more convincing samples, which forces the discriminator to sharpen again. This is the minimax game — the two play opposing objectives: \[\min_G \max_D \; \mathbb{E}_{x}[\log D(x)] + \mathbb{E}_{z}[\log(1 - D(G(z)))]\] \(D\) wants to push this value up (classify correctly); \(G\) wants to push it down (fool \(D\)). At the ideal equilibrium, the generator’s output is indistinguishable from real data, and the discriminator can do no better than a coin flip.

A useful analogy: a counterfeiter (generator) and a detective (discriminator). The counterfeiter keeps refining fake notes; the detective keeps learning to catch them; over time the fakes become near-perfect.

GANs produced the first wave of strikingly realistic synthetic faces and powered image-to-image tasks (e.g. turning a sketch of a face into a photo-like image). Their weakness is training instability — the two-player balance can collapse, including “mode collapse”, where the generator produces only a few kinds of output.

Why it matters: GANs were the breakthrough that made generated images look real, and they remain a key reference point. The source overview lists GANs and their image-processing applications precisely because they opened the door to high-quality visual generation.

Diffusion models

Diffusion models generate data by learning to reverse a gradual corruption process. Training has two directions: 1. Forward (fixed): start with a real image and add a tiny bit of random noise repeatedly, over many steps, until the image is pure static. 2. Reverse (learned): train a network to undo one step of noise at a time — given a noisy image, predict the noise that was added so it can be subtracted.

Once trained, you generate by starting from pure random noise and running the learned denoiser repeatedly. Step by step, structure emerges from the static and a coherent image appears. To control what is generated, the denoiser is conditioned on a text prompt, so the denoising is steered toward an image matching the words.

Modern systems run this process not on raw pixels but in a compressed latent space (a latent diffusion model), which is what makes high-resolution generation fast enough to be practical. This is the basis of today’s leading image generators — Stable Diffusion, DALL·E, Midjourney, Imagen.

Why it matters: Diffusion has largely overtaken GANs for state-of-the-art image generation because it is more stable to train and produces more diverse, higher-quality images. “Add noise, then learn to remove it” is the single most important idea behind the current generation of text-to-image tools.

Large Language Models (LLMs)

An LLM is a very large neural network — almost always a Transformer — trained to do one deceptively simple thing: predict the next token. Given a sequence of text so far, it outputs a probability distribution over what comes next, and generates by repeatedly sampling one token and feeding it back in.

Tokens and embeddings. Text is first split into tokens — sub-word chunks (a token might be a whole short word, or a piece like gener + ation). Each token is mapped to a high-dimensional vector called an embedding, so that words with related meanings sit near each other in vector space. The model operates on these vectors, not on raw characters.

Pre-training. The model is trained on enormous amounts of text (web pages, books, code) in a self-supervised way: the “label” for predicting the next token is simply the next token already present in the text, so no human labelling is needed. To predict the next word well across billions of examples, the model is forced to absorb grammar, facts, reasoning patterns, and style.

Emergent abilities. As models grow in parameters and training data, they begin to display capabilities that were not explicitly trained — translation, arithmetic, code generation, step-by-step reasoning — that appear or sharpen only past a certain scale. These are called emergent abilities.

Fine-tuning and instruction tuning. A pre-trained “base” model is a powerful text-continuer but not naturally helpful or obedient. Fine-tuning continues training on a narrower, task-specific dataset. Instruction tuning is a special case: the model is trained on many examples of (instruction, good response) pairs so it learns to follow instructions and answer questions rather than just continue text.

RLHF (reinforcement learning from human feedback). To align the model with what people actually want — helpful, honest, harmless answers — humans rank multiple candidate responses. Those rankings train a reward model that scores responses, and the LLM is then optimised (via reinforcement learning) to produce high-scoring answers. RLHF is the key step that turned a raw GPT-style model into a usable assistant like ChatGPT.

Prompting and in-context learning. At use time, you steer the model with a prompt. Remarkably, an LLM can perform a new task from just a few examples placed in the prompt itself, with no weight updates — this is in-context learning (zero-shot with no examples, few-shot with a handful).

Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG). An LLM only “knows” what was in its training data, frozen at training time. RAG connects it to an external knowledge source: relevant documents are retrieved (often via embedding similarity search) and inserted into the prompt, so the model answers using fresh, specific, or private information it never memorised. This improves accuracy and lets answers cite sources.

Hallucination (a key limitation). Because an LLM generates fluent, plausible-sounding text by predicting tokens — not by checking facts — it can confidently produce statements that are wrong or entirely made up. This is called hallucination, and it is why outputs on factual matters should be verified. RAG and grounding reduce, but do not eliminate, the problem.

Why it matters: LLMs are the backbone of the current AI wave. The single objective of next-token prediction, applied at massive scale and then aligned with instruction tuning and RLHF, is what produced GPT, ChatGPT, and their relatives — the tools the overview points to when it mentions GPT and text generation.

Text-to-image and multimodal models

A text-to-image model takes a natural-language prompt and produces a matching image — today almost always via a diffusion model conditioned on text. The crucial ingredient is a shared understanding between words and pixels: a model such as CLIP is trained on huge numbers of image–caption pairs so that an image and its description land at nearby points in a shared embedding space. That alignment lets the image generator be guided by language.

More broadly, multimodal models handle more than one type of data at once — text and images, and increasingly audio and video. This enables tasks like image captioning (describe a picture), visual question answering (answer a question about an image), and assistants that can both read an image and discuss it in words. The same Transformer machinery that powers LLMs, extended to take in image or audio tokens, underlies these systems.

Why it matters: Multimodal models are where generative AI is heading — single systems that see, read, listen, and generate across formats. They tie together the text and vision threads from the overview into one unified capability.

A concrete illustration

How an LLM continues a prompt, token by token. Suppose the prompt is: The capital of France is. The model: 1. Splits it into tokens and looks up each token’s embedding. 2. Processes the sequence and outputs a probability distribution over the next token — e.g. Paris (0.92), a (0.03), located (0.02), … 3. Picks a token (say Paris), appends it, and repeats with The capital of France is Paris as the new input. 4. Continues — perhaps ., then a stop — producing The capital of France is Paris.

Every word you see from ChatGPT is generated exactly this way: one token at a time, each chosen from a probability distribution conditioned on everything written so far. Note the catch — if a fact wasn’t well represented in training, step 2 can assign high probability to a wrong token, and the model will state it just as fluently. That is hallucination in action.

How a text-to-image prompt becomes a picture. Prompt: a red fox sitting in snow, photorealistic. The system encodes this text into a vector, then starts from a canvas of pure random noise. The diffusion model denoises it over many steps, each step nudged by the text vector toward “fox”, “snow”, “red”, “photorealistic”. Early steps fix rough shapes and colours; later steps add fine detail like fur and texture. After the final step, the static has been transformed into a coherent image of a red fox in snow.

Key takeaways

  • Discriminative models predict a label, \(P(y\mid x)\); generative models learn the data itself, \(P(x)\), so they can create new examples.
  • VAEs learn a smooth latent space you can sample from — the foundational generative idea.
  • GANs pit a generator against a discriminator in a minimax game; powerful for realistic images but tricky to train.
  • Diffusion models add noise then learn to denoise, and now drive the best text-to-image systems (Stable Diffusion, DALL·E).
  • LLMs are Transformers trained to predict the next token over huge text; scale brings emergent abilities.
  • Instruction tuning and RLHF turn a raw LLM into a helpful assistant (e.g. ChatGPT); prompting and in-context learning steer it without retraining.
  • RAG grounds an LLM in external documents; hallucination — confident but false output — remains a core limitation to guard against.
  • Text-to-image and multimodal models bridge language with vision (and audio), enabled by shared text–image embeddings like CLIP.

Reinforcement Learning

In one line: Reinforcement learning is how an agent learns to make good decisions by interacting with an environment through trial and error, choosing actions that maximize the long-run cumulative reward it receives as feedback.

What this chapter covers

  • Why reinforcement learning (RL) is a third paradigm, distinct from supervised and unsupervised learning.
  • The agent-environment loop and its core pieces: agent, environment, state, action, reward, and policy.
  • The goal of RL: maximizing expected cumulative discounted reward (the return).
  • The Markov Decision Process (MDP) as the formal model behind RL.
  • Value functions and the Q-function, and the Bellman equation that links them.
  • The exploration vs. exploitation trade-off and the epsilon-greedy strategy.
  • Q-learning, its update rule, and how Deep Q-Networks (DQN) scale it up.
  • Policy-gradient and actor-critic methods at a high level.
  • Where RL shines in practice: game playing (AlphaGo), robotics, and control.
  • A worked gridworld example showing learning in action.

Reinforcement learning is a different paradigm

In supervised learning, every training example comes with a correct label, and the model learns the mapping from input to label. In unsupervised learning, there are no labels at all, and the model finds structure in the data. Reinforcement learning is neither. There is no teacher handing over the right answer for each step. Instead, an agent acts in an environment and receives only a reward signal telling it how good or bad things turned out. The agent must figure out, on its own, which sequences of actions lead to good outcomes.

This is much closer to how a human or an animal learns: nobody labels every move you make while learning to ride a bicycle, but you get feedback (you stay up, or you fall) and you adjust. RL formalizes this learning through experience by trial and error.

Why it matters: Many real problems are about making a sequence of decisions over time where the right action depends on the situation and the consequences are delayed. Supervised learning cannot express “act now, get rewarded later.” RL can, which is why it powers robotics, game-playing AI, and control systems.

The agent-environment loop

RL is built around a repeating interaction loop between two entities:

  • Agent — the decision maker (a piece of software, an algorithm, a robot’s controller). It observes the situation and chooses what to do.
  • Environment — everything outside the agent that it acts upon and that responds to it. It contains all the external factors that influence the outcome.
  • State (\(s\)) — a description of the situation the agent is currently in (e.g., the robot’s position in a maze).
  • Action (\(a\)) — a choice the agent makes from the available options (e.g., move forward, turn left).
  • Reward (\(r\)) — a number the environment returns after each action, signalling how good that step was. Positive reward encourages a behavior; negative reward (a penalty) discourages it.
  • Policy (\(\pi\)) — the agent’s strategy: the rule that maps states to actions, i.e., what to do in each situation. This is ultimately what the agent is learning.

The loop runs like this, step by step (using time index \(t\)):

  1. The agent observes the current state \(s_t\).
  2. Using its policy, it picks an action \(a_t = \pi(s_t)\).
  3. The environment transitions to a new state \(s_{t+1}\) and returns a reward \(r_{t+1}\).
  4. The agent uses this feedback to update its policy, then repeats.

A robot in a maze illustrates every piece: the robot is the agent, the maze is the environment, its location is the state, “step forward” is an action, reaching the exit gives positive reward while bumping an obstacle gives negative reward, and its rule for choosing moves is the policy.

Why it matters: This loop is the entire skeleton of RL. Every algorithm in the field is just a different recipe for step 4 — how to turn rewards into a better policy.

The goal: maximize expected cumulative (discounted) reward

The agent does not try to grab the biggest immediate reward at each step. It tries to maximize the total reward accumulated over the whole future. This total is called the return, written \(G_t\). The catch is that rewards far in the future are worth a bit less than rewards right now, so we apply a discount factor \(\gamma\) (gamma), with \(0 \le \gamma \le 1\):

\[G_t = r_{t+1} + \gamma\, r_{t+2} + \gamma^2 r_{t+3} + \cdots = \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \gamma^k\, r_{t+k+1}\]

A compact way to write the same idea is \(G_t = \sum_k \gamma^k r_{t+k}\).

The discount \(\gamma\) does two jobs. First, it expresses a preference for sooner rewards (a reward now is more certain than one many steps away). Second, it keeps the sum finite and mathematically well-behaved even when the task never ends. When \(\gamma\) is close to \(0\), the agent is short-sighted and cares mostly about immediate reward; when \(\gamma\) is close to \(1\), it is far-sighted and plans for the long run.

Because the environment can be uncertain (the same action might not always give the same outcome), the true objective is the expected return, \(\mathbb{E}[G_t]\).

Why it matters: This single quantity — expected cumulative discounted reward — is the yardstick the agent optimizes. It captures the crucial idea that a good agent may sacrifice a small reward now to reach a much larger reward later (e.g., a chess engine giving up a pawn to win the game).

The Markov Decision Process (MDP)

The standard mathematical model of an RL problem is the Markov Decision Process. An MDP is defined by five ingredients:

  • States \(S\) — the set of all situations the agent can be in.
  • Actions \(A\) — the set of moves available.
  • Transition function \(P(s' \mid s, a)\) — the probability of landing in state \(s'\) after taking action \(a\) in state \(s\).
  • Reward function \(R(s, a)\) — the reward expected for taking action \(a\) in state \(s\).
  • Discount factor \(\gamma\) — how much future rewards are discounted.

The word Markov refers to the Markov property: the future depends only on the current state and action, not on the full history of how the agent got there. In other words, the current state already summarizes everything relevant for deciding what to do next.

\[P(s_{t+1} \mid s_t, a_t) = P(s_{t+1} \mid s_t, a_t, s_{t-1}, a_{t-1}, \dots)\]

Why it matters: The MDP gives RL a precise, shared language. Once a problem is framed as an MDP, the whole toolbox of value functions, the Bellman equation, and Q-learning becomes available. The Markov property is what makes the problem tractable: the agent only needs to reason about the present state, not an ever-growing history.

Value functions and the Q-function

To act well, the agent needs to estimate “how good” states and actions are in the long run. Two quantities capture this.

The state-value function \(V^\pi(s)\) is the expected return if the agent starts in state \(s\) and then follows policy \(\pi\):

\[V^\pi(s) = \mathbb{E}_\pi\!\left[\, G_t \mid s_t = s \,\right]\]

The action-value function, or Q-function, \(Q^\pi(s, a)\) is the expected return if the agent starts in state \(s\), takes action \(a\), and then follows policy \(\pi\):

\[Q^\pi(s, a) = \mathbb{E}_\pi\!\left[\, G_t \mid s_t = s,\ a_t = a \,\right]\]

The Q-function is especially handy: if you know \(Q\) for every action in a state, you can pick the best action just by choosing the one with the highest Q-value. That is exactly how a learned Q-function becomes a policy.

Why it matters: Value functions turn the vague goal “maximize long-run reward” into concrete numbers attached to states and actions. Comparing these numbers is how the agent decides what to do, and improving these estimates is how it learns.

The Bellman equation (intuition)

The Bellman equation is the key recursive insight of RL. It says the value of where you are now equals the reward you get next, plus the (discounted) value of where you end up. Value at one step is defined in terms of value at the next step:

\[Q(s, a) = \mathbb{E}\!\left[\, r + \gamma \max_{a'} Q(s', a') \,\right]\]

Read it in plain English: the worth of taking action \(a\) in state \(s\) is the immediate reward \(r\), plus \(\gamma\) times the worth of acting optimally from the next state \(s'\). The \(\max_{a'}\) assumes that, once in \(s'\), you will again choose the best available action.

This recursion is powerful because it lets the agent bootstrap — improve its estimate of one value using its current estimate of the next value, rather than waiting to see the entire future play out.

Why it matters: Nearly every RL algorithm is, at heart, a way to make the agent’s value estimates satisfy the Bellman equation. It is the engine that propagates information about good (and bad) outcomes backward through time to the decisions that caused them.

Exploration vs. exploitation (epsilon-greedy)

An agent faces a fundamental dilemma at every step:

  • Exploitation — use what it already knows and pick the action it currently believes is best (to earn reward now).
  • Exploration — try a different, possibly worse-looking action (to discover whether something even better exists).

Pure exploitation risks getting stuck with a mediocre strategy because the agent never tries the alternatives that might be superior. Pure exploration wastes reward by acting randomly. A good agent must balance the two.

A simple and widely used balance is the epsilon-greedy strategy. With a small probability \(\epsilon\) the agent explores (picks a random action), and the rest of the time it exploits (picks the best-known action):

\[ a_t = \begin{cases} \text{random action}, & \text{with probability } \epsilon \\[4pt] \arg\max_a Q(s_t, a), & \text{with probability } 1 - \epsilon \end{cases} \]

A common practice is to start with a large \(\epsilon\) (explore a lot early, when the agent knows little) and gradually shrink it (exploit more as the agent’s estimates become reliable).

Why it matters: Without exploration, an agent can never improve beyond its first lucky guesses. The exploration-exploitation trade-off is one of the defining challenges that separates RL from supervised learning.

Q-learning

Q-learning is a classic algorithm that learns the Q-function directly from experience, without ever needing a model of the environment’s transition probabilities (it is model-free). After each step the agent observes \((s, a, r, s')\) and nudges its current estimate of \(Q(s, a)\) toward the Bellman target:

\[Q(s, a) \leftarrow Q(s, a) + \alpha \Big[\, r + \gamma \max_{a'} Q(s', a') - Q(s, a) \,\Big]\]

The pieces:

  • \(\alpha\) is the learning rate, controlling how big each update step is.
  • \(r + \gamma \max_{a'} Q(s', a')\) is the target: the new, better estimate based on the reward just seen plus the best achievable value from the next state.
  • \(r + \gamma \max_{a'} Q(s', a') - Q(s, a)\) is the temporal-difference (TD) error: the gap between the old estimate and the new target. The update shrinks this gap.

Repeating this update across many experiences, while exploring with something like epsilon-greedy, drives the Q-values toward their correct values, and the greedy policy over those Q-values becomes near-optimal.

Why it matters: Q-learning is a complete, practical learning rule that needs nothing but experience tuples \((s, a, r, s')\). It is the conceptual foundation for much of modern deep RL.

Deep Q-Networks (DQN)

Plain Q-learning stores \(Q(s, a)\) in a table with one entry per state-action pair. That works for small problems but breaks down when states are huge or continuous — for example, the raw screen pixels of a video game, where there are astronomically many possible states.

A Deep Q-Network (DQN) replaces the table with a neural network that takes a state as input and outputs the estimated Q-value for each action: \(Q(s, a; \theta)\), where \(\theta\) are the network’s weights. The network generalizes across similar states, so the agent does not need to visit every state to learn something useful about it. DQN is trained to minimize the TD error using gradient descent, and famously, with two stabilizing tricks:

  • Experience replay — store past experiences in a buffer and train on random mini-batches of them, breaking harmful correlations between consecutive steps.
  • A target network — a slowly updated copy of the network used to compute the Bellman target, which keeps training stable.

DQN is what allowed a single architecture to learn to play many Atari games directly from pixels at human level.

Why it matters: DQN bridges classical RL and deep learning, making RL usable on high-dimensional, real-world inputs like images. It launched the modern wave of deep reinforcement learning.

Policy-gradient and actor-critic methods (high level)

Q-learning and DQN are value-based: they learn values and derive the policy by taking the best-valued action. An alternative family learns the policy directly.

Policy-gradient methods represent the policy as a parameterized function \(\pi_\theta(a \mid s)\) — typically a neural network that outputs a probability for each action — and adjust the parameters \(\theta\) to increase the expected return. Loosely, they push up the probability of actions that led to high return and push down those that led to low return:

\[\nabla_\theta J(\theta) \;\propto\; \mathbb{E}\!\left[\, \nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a \mid s)\; G_t \,\right]\]

Policy-gradient methods handle continuous action spaces naturally (e.g., the exact torque on a robot joint), which value-based methods struggle with.

Actor-critic methods combine both ideas. They keep two components:

  • The actor — the policy \(\pi_\theta(a \mid s)\) that chooses actions.
  • The critic — a value estimate (like \(V\) or \(Q\)) that judges how good the actor’s actions are.

The critic’s feedback gives the actor a lower-variance, more informative learning signal than raw returns alone, so the policy improves more stably. Modern algorithms such as A2C, PPO, and DDPG are actor-critic methods.

Why it matters: Policy-based and actor-critic methods extend RL to problems with continuous or very large action spaces, and they tend to train more stably on hard control tasks. They are the workhorses behind much of today’s robotics and continuous-control RL.

Example applications

  • Game playing — RL reaches superhuman skill in games. AlphaGo (and its successor AlphaZero) combined deep neural networks with RL and search to beat the world’s best Go players; DQN mastered Atari games from pixels.
  • Robotics — robots learn locomotion, grasping, and manipulation by trial and error, with reward shaped around task success and safety. Movement and control of robots are increasingly driven by RL.
  • Control systems — managing data-center cooling, optimizing traffic signals, balancing power grids, and other sequential decision problems where actions affect future states.

Why it matters: These are domains where there is no labeled “correct action” dataset, but there is a clear notion of success or reward — exactly the setting RL was built for.

A concrete illustration

Picture a small 4 x 4 gridworld. The agent starts in the top-left cell (S). One specific cell holds a goal with reward +10; one cell is a pit with reward -10; every ordinary step costs a tiny -1 (to encourage short paths). The available actions in each cell are up, down, left, right.

+----+----+----+----+
| S  |    |    |    |
+----+----+----+----+
|    | ## |    |    |     S  = start
+----+----+----+----+     ## = pit  (reward -10)
|    |    |    | GG |     GG = goal (reward +10)
+----+----+----+----+     each ordinary step: reward -1
|    |    |    |    |
+----+----+----+----+

Here the state is which cell the agent occupies, an action is a move direction, and the reward comes from the cell it lands in.

Episode 1 (knows nothing). The agent’s Q-values all start at \(0\), so it explores essentially at random. Suppose it wanders down and right, accidentally steps into the pit, and receives \(-10\). Using the Q-learning update with, say, \(\alpha = 0.5\) and \(\gamma = 0.9\), the value of the move that led into the pit is pushed sharply downward:

\[Q(s_{\text{near pit}},\ \text{into pit}) \leftarrow 0 + 0.5\,[\,-10 + 0.9\cdot 0 - 0\,] = -5\]

Now that state-action pair looks bad, so the agent will tend to avoid repeating it.

Later episodes (learning propagates). On another run the agent happens to reach the goal cell and receives \(+10\). The Q-value of the move that entered the goal jumps up. On the next visits, the Bellman bootstrap carries that good news backward: the cell just before the goal gains value because \(\max_{a'} Q(s', a')\) from there is now high, then the cell before that gains value, and so on. Step by step, a trail of increasing Q-values forms, pointing from the start toward the goal and away from the pit.

Convergence. After many episodes — exploring with epsilon-greedy, occasionally trying odd moves, but mostly exploiting — the greedy policy “always move toward the higher-valued neighbor” traces the shortest safe path from S to GG while steering clear of the pit. The agent was never told the correct route. It discovered it purely from rewards, by trial and error, exactly as the agent-environment loop prescribes.

Key takeaways

  • RL is a third paradigm, distinct from supervised and unsupervised learning: an agent learns from reward feedback through trial and error, not from labeled examples.
  • The core loop is agent → action → environment → (new state, reward) → agent, and the thing being learned is the policy \(\pi\), the agent’s strategy for choosing actions.
  • The objective is the expected cumulative discounted reward \(G_t = \sum_k \gamma^k r_{t+k}\), where the discount \(\gamma\) trades off immediate vs. future reward.
  • RL problems are formalized as Markov Decision Processes (states, actions, transitions, rewards, \(\gamma\)), built on the Markov property that the current state captures all that matters.
  • Value functions (\(V\)) and the Q-function (\(Q\)) measure long-run goodness; the Bellman equation links each value to the next and is the recursive engine of learning.
  • Good learning requires balancing exploration vs. exploitation, commonly via epsilon-greedy.
  • Q-learning updates estimates toward the Bellman target, \(Q(s,a) \leftarrow Q(s,a) + \alpha[r + \gamma \max_{a'} Q(s',a') - Q(s,a)]\); DQN replaces the Q-table with a neural network to handle huge or continuous states.
  • Policy-gradient and actor-critic methods learn the policy directly and excel with continuous action spaces.
  • RL drives real systems: AlphaGo and game AI, robotics, and control — wherever success can be scored but the correct action is never handed to you.

Responsible AI — Evaluation, Pitfalls and Ethics

In one line: A model is only as trustworthy as the way you evaluate it and the harm you prevent — so measure honestly, watch for the traps that fake good results, and account for the people the model affects.

What this chapter covers

  • How to evaluate models properly so your numbers reflect real-world performance, not lucky memorization.
  • Choosing the right metric for the job, and why accuracy can lie.
  • Overfitting vs underfitting and the bias-variance tradeoff (recap from weeks 8/14/15), plus how to detect and fix each.
  • The silent killers: data leakage, class imbalance, and distribution shift.
  • The ethics layer: bias and fairness, transparency, privacy, hallucination and over-reliance on LLMs, human oversight, and compute cost.
  • A short checklist you can run before shipping any model.

Evaluating models properly (simple explanation)

The whole point of machine learning is generalization — doing well on data the model has never seen. If you measure performance on the same data you trained on, you are testing memory, not learning. A model can score 100% on its training set and be useless in production.

The standard discipline is a three-way split: - Training set — the model learns its parameters here. - Validation set — you tune hyperparameters and compare model choices here (which architecture, how much regularization, when to stop). - Test set — touched once, at the very end, to estimate real-world performance. Never tune on it.

A common split is roughly 60/20/20 or 70/15/15, but proportions matter less than the rule: the test set must stay sealed.

Cross-validation. When data is scarce, a single split wastes data and gives a noisy estimate. k-fold cross-validation splits the data into \(k\) parts, trains on \(k-1\) of them and validates on the one held out, then rotates so every fold is used for validation exactly once. The final score is the average across folds — a more stable estimate that uses all the data. Typical choices are \(k=5\) or \(k=10\). For time-series data, use forward-chaining splits (train on the past, validate on the future) — never shuffle, or you leak the future into the past.

Why it matters: Without an honest, sealed evaluation you cannot tell whether your model learned a real pattern or just memorized the answer key. Every claim about a model’s quality is only as credible as the data it was measured on.

The right metric for the job (simple explanation)

A metric is the question you are really asking. Pick the wrong one and a “good” score answers a question nobody cares about.

Classification. Build intuition from the confusion matrix — true positives (TP), true negatives (TN), false positives (FP), false negatives (FN):

  • Accuracy \(= \dfrac{TP+TN}{TP+TN+FP+FN}\) — fraction correct. Fine when classes are balanced; dangerously misleading when they are not (see the illustration below).
  • Precision \(= \dfrac{TP}{TP+FP}\) — of the cases you flagged positive, how many really were. High precision = few false alarms.
  • Recall (sensitivity) \(= \dfrac{TP}{TP+FN}\) — of the actual positives, how many you caught. High recall = few misses.
  • F1 score \(= 2\cdot\dfrac{\text{precision}\cdot\text{recall}}{\text{precision}+\text{recall}}\) — the harmonic mean, used when you need one number that balances both and the classes are imbalanced.
  • ROC-AUC — how well the model ranks positives above negatives across all thresholds; useful but can look optimistic on heavily imbalanced data, where PR-AUC (precision-recall) is more honest.

There is almost always a precision-recall tradeoff: lowering the decision threshold catches more positives (recall up) but raises false alarms (precision down). Which way you lean is a domain decision — a cancer screen wants high recall (don’t miss a sick patient), a spam filter wants high precision (don’t trash real mail).

Regression. - MAE (mean absolute error) — average size of the error, in the original units, robust to outliers. - RMSE \(= \sqrt{\frac{1}{n}\sum (y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2}\) — like MAE but squares the errors first, so it punishes large mistakes harder. - \(R^2\) (coefficient of determination) — the fraction of variance the model explains, from 1 (perfect) down through 0 (no better than predicting the mean) and even negative (worse than the mean).

Why it matters: The metric is a contract with the real world. Optimizing accuracy on an imbalanced fraud dataset, or RMSE when outliers are data-entry errors you don’t care about, produces a model that scores well and fails the actual job.

Overfitting vs underfitting and the bias-variance tradeoff (recap)

This ties back to weeks 8/14/15. Total expected error decomposes into bias, variance, and irreducible noise.

  • Underfitting (high bias): the model is too simple to capture the pattern. It does poorly on both training and validation data. Think of fitting a straight line to a clearly curved relationship.
  • Overfitting (high variance): the model is too flexible and memorizes noise. It does great on training data but poorly on validation/test data. The tell-tale sign is a large gap between training and validation performance.
Symptom Training score Validation score Diagnosis
Both low low low Underfitting (bias)
Train high, val low high low Overfitting (variance)
Both high, small gap high high Good fit

The tradeoff: making a model more complex lowers bias but raises variance, and vice versa. The goal is the sweet spot that minimizes error on unseen data — found using the validation set, not the training set.

How to fix overfitting: - More (and more varied) data — the single most reliable cure; harder to memorize a large, diverse set. Data augmentation helps when you can’t collect more. - Regularization — penalize complexity: L2/ridge (\(\lambda \sum w_j^2\)) shrinks weights, L1/lasso (\(\lambda \sum |w_j|\)) drives some to zero, dropout in neural nets randomly disables units during training. The strength \(\lambda\) is itself tuned on validation. - Simpler models — fewer features, fewer parameters, shallower trees. - Early stopping — halt training when validation error stops improving, before the model starts fitting noise.

How to fix underfitting: add a more expressive model, add useful features, reduce regularization, train longer.

Why it matters: Overfitting is the most common way a model looks excellent in the lab and disappoints in production. The training-vs-validation gap is your early-warning system.

Data leakage (simple explanation)

Leakage is when information that won’t be available at prediction time sneaks into training — so the model “cheats.” Classic forms: - Target leakage: a feature that is really a stand-in for the answer (e.g. “account_closed_date” when predicting churn). - Preprocessing leakage: scaling, imputing, or selecting features using statistics computed over the whole dataset before splitting — the test set’s information bleeds into training. Fit transformers on the training fold only, then apply them to validation/test. - Temporal leakage: using future information to predict the past.

The signature of leakage is suspiciously high offline scores that collapse in production.

Why it matters: Leakage produces the most convincing fake results in ML — near-perfect metrics that evaporate on real data. It is subtle and extremely common.

Class imbalance (simple explanation)

When one class is rare (fraud, disease, defects), a model can score high by ignoring the rare class entirely. Fixes: - Use the right metric — precision, recall, F1, PR-AUC instead of accuracy. - Resampling — oversample the minority (e.g. SMOTE) or undersample the majority; only on the training set. - Class weights — make the loss penalize minority-class mistakes more. - Adjust the decision threshold — the default 0.5 is rarely optimal under imbalance.

Why it matters: The cases you most want to catch are usually the rare ones. A model that quietly never predicts them can still post a high accuracy and be worthless.

Generalization and distribution shift (simple explanation)

A model trained on yesterday’s data is deployed into tomorrow’s world. Distribution shift is when the live data drifts away from the training data: - Covariate shift — the inputs change (new user demographics, a new sensor). - Label/concept drift — the relationship between inputs and the target changes (spammers change tactics, prices behave differently after a shock).

The cure is not a one-time fix but a practice: monitor inputs and predictions in production, watch for performance decay, and retrain on fresh data.

Why it matters: Even a perfectly evaluated model decays over time. Good offline numbers are a starting condition, not a guarantee.

Bias and fairness (ethics)

Models learn the patterns in their data — including its prejudices. If historical data reflects discrimination, the model will reproduce and often amplify it (“garbage in, gospel out”). Bias enters through skewed samples, biased labels, or proxy features (a ZIP code standing in for race). Mitigation: audit the data and outcomes across groups, use fairness metrics (equal false-positive rates, demographic parity, equalized odds — which often conflict, so the choice is a value judgment), and include affected communities in the design.

Why it matters: A biased model can deny loans, jobs, or bail to people based on group membership, at scale and behind a veneer of objectivity.

Transparency and explainability (ethics)

Many strong models (deep nets, large ensembles) are black boxes — accurate but hard to interrogate. Explainability tools (feature importance, SHAP, LIME, attention/saliency) help, but are approximations. In high-stakes domains (credit, health, justice) a “right to explanation” may be legally or ethically required.

Why it matters: People affected by a decision deserve to know why; and you cannot debug, trust, or contest what you cannot explain.

Privacy (ethics)

Training data often contains personal information, and models can leak it — memorizing and regurgitating training examples, or enabling membership-inference attacks. Practices: collect the minimum needed, anonymize, get consent, and use privacy-preserving techniques. Federated learning trains across many devices without the raw data ever leaving them — only model updates are shared — and differential privacy adds calibrated noise so no single individual’s record can be reverse-engineered.

Why it matters: A model that quietly memorizes and exposes someone’s medical or financial data is a breach, regardless of how accurate it is.

Hallucination and over-reliance on LLMs (ethics)

Large language models generate fluent, confident text that can be factually wrong — a “hallucination.” They predict plausible words, not verified truth, and have no built-in sense of certainty. The danger compounds when users trust the polished output uncritically (automation bias). Defenses: ground answers in retrieved sources (RAG), cite and verify, keep a human in the loop for consequential decisions, and treat LLM output as a draft, not an authority.

Why it matters: Confidence is not correctness. Acting on an unverified hallucination in medicine, law, or engineering can cause real harm.

Safety and human oversight (ethics)

For consequential decisions, keep a human in the loop — the model advises, a person decides and is accountable. Design for graceful failure: let the model say “I don’t know” or abstain when uncertain, set conservative thresholds, and red-team for misuse before deployment.

Why it matters: Automated decisions at scale fail at scale. Human oversight is the circuit breaker.

Environmental and compute cost (ethics)

Training and serving large models consumes significant energy, water, and hardware, with a real carbon footprint and financial cost. Responsible practice asks whether a smaller model, fine-tuning instead of training from scratch, distillation, or simple caching would do — and weighs the benefit against the cost.

Why it matters: The biggest model is not always the right one. Efficiency is both an environmental and a budget concern.

A concrete illustration

A hospital builds a classifier to flag a rare disease that affects 1% of patients. The model reports 99% accuracy and the team celebrates.

Then someone looks closer. The model achieves 99% by predicting “healthy” for everyone — since 99% of patients are indeed healthy, it is right 99% of the time while catching zero sick patients. The confusion matrix exposes it:

  • Recall \(= \dfrac{TP}{TP+FN} = \dfrac{0}{0+10} = 0\%\) — it misses every single sick patient.
  • Precision is undefined (it never predicts “sick”), which is itself a red flag.
  • F1 \(= 0\).

Accuracy hid a catastrophic failure; precision and recall revealed it instantly. The fix combines the lessons above: evaluate with recall/F1/PR-AUC instead of accuracy, address the imbalance with class weights or resampling, and lower the decision threshold so the model is willing to flag suspicious cases — accepting more false alarms in exchange for catching real disease.

The same pattern appears in ethics. A hiring model trained on a company’s past hires “learns” who got hired before. If past hiring favored one group, the model scores those résumés higher — high accuracy against historical labels, but it is automating discrimination. Here the right move is not a better metric but a fairness audit across groups, removal of proxy features, and human review.

Key takeaways

  • Evaluate on data the model has never seen; keep the test set sealed and use cross-validation when data is scarce.
  • The metric is the real question — accuracy lies on imbalanced data; reach for precision, recall, F1, or PR-AUC, and RMSE/\(R^2\) for regression.
  • A large gap between training and validation performance means overfitting; both scores low means underfitting. Fix overfitting with more data, regularization, simpler models, and early stopping.
  • Data leakage produces the most convincing fake results in ML — fit all preprocessing on the training fold only and never use future information.
  • Models inherit the biases of their data; audit for fairness, explainability, and privacy, not just accuracy.
  • LLMs hallucinate confidently — verify, ground, and keep a human in the loop for anything consequential.
  • Good offline numbers are a starting condition, not a guarantee: monitor for distribution shift and retrain.

A checklist for trustworthy ML

Part II — The Course, Week by Week

All 18 weeks of the programme, each explained simply with key formulae, a deep-dive worked example, and — new in this edition — a Going Deeper expansion, exam-style Q&A, and a quick-revision cheat-sheet.

Week 1: Overview of the Course (Introduction to the Programme)

In one line: A guided tour of the entire programme — the math you need, the programming tools you’ll use, and the full sweep of machine learning and deep learning techniques and their real-world applications across text, image, video, and audio.

What this week covers

  • Who teaches the programme and its main objective (theory + practice of classical ML and deep learning)
  • Mathematical background: linear algebra (vectors, matrices, inner/dot product, matrix-vector transformation, eigenvalues/eigenvectors, singular value decomposition)
  • Calculus background: single-variable and multivariable differentiation, derivatives with respect to vectors/matrices, unconstrained vs constrained optimization, Lagrange multiplier method
  • Probability background: discrete vs continuous random variables, probability distributions vs probability density functions, sum and product rules, Bayes rule, Gaussian (normal) density
  • Programming fundamentals: Python syntax, NumPy, SciPy, scikit-learn, PyTorch, Matplotlib, TensorFlow; deployment/productionization; advanced Python (generators, iterators, decorators, context managers, performance)
  • Exploratory data analysis: data/variable types, central tendency, dispersion, five-point summary, skewness, box plots, covariance, correlation, encoding, scaling, normalization
  • Application domains: natural language processing (text), computer vision (image/video), audio (speech/music), plus other domains (autonomous vehicles, recommendation, games, education, banking, healthcare)
  • Machine learning paradigms: supervised, unsupervised, semi-supervised, self-supervised, active, transfer learning / domain adaptation, zero/one/few-shot, federated learning
  • Classical ML techniques: linear regression, overfitting and regularization (ridge regression), k-nearest neighbors, Bayes and naive Bayes classifiers, maximum likelihood estimation, Gaussian mixture models, support vector machines (hard/soft margin, kernels), dimensionality reduction (PCA, Fisher discriminant analysis), decision trees, random forests, ensemble methods (bagging, boosting, AdaBoost), k-means and hierarchical clustering
  • Deep learning models: perceptron, activation functions (sigmoid, softmax, ReLU), multi-layer feed-forward networks, backpropagation / gradient descent / stochastic gradient descent, logistic regression, optimizers (generalized delta rule, Adam), regularization (dropout), normalization (batch, layer), CNNs (1D/2D/3D, LeNet/VGG/GoogLeNet/ResNet, U-Net, YOLO), RNNs (LSTM, GRU, bidirectional, deep RNN, backprop through time), transformers and attention, pre-training/fine-tuning (BERT, ViLBERT, GPT/ChatGPT), GANs, reinforcement learning (Markov decision processes, Q-learning, deep Q-networks, actor-critic, exploration vs exploitation)

The topics, explained simply

The programme’s goal and structure

The course is run by IIT Madras Pravartak with EMERITUS, taught mainly by Prof. C. Chandrasekhar (IIT Madras) and Prof. A. D. Dileep (IIT Dharwad), with some modules from EMERITUS subject-matter experts. The aim is to give you both the theory behind classical machine learning and modern deep learning, and the practical skill to implement these models on real problems.

Artificial intelligence (AI) means getting computers to act and reason in ways we’d call “intelligent.” Machine learning (ML) is the part of AI where a computer learns patterns from data instead of being hand-coded with rules. Deep learning (DL) is a powerful subset of ML built on multi-layered “neural networks.” AI is about 75 years old, but the last 10–15 years of deep-learning breakthroughs are why it’s everywhere now.

Why it matters: This week is the map. Everything later is a stop on a journey that goes math, then programming, then applications, then classical ML, then deep learning, then reinforcement learning.

Linear algebra background

Real-world data — text, images, audio — is turned into feature vectors: ordered lists of numbers that summarize a piece of data. (A “feature” is one measurable property; a “vector” is just a column of numbers.) Stack many feature vectors together and you get a matrix (a grid of numbers).

The key operations you’ll review: the inner product (also called dot product), which multiplies two vectors element-by-element and sums the result — it measures how aligned two vectors are. And matrix-vector multiplication, which transforms a vector into a new one (rotating, stretching, projecting it).

\[ \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} = a_1 b_1 + a_2 b_2 + \cdots + a_n b_n = \sum_{i=1}^{n} a_i b_i \]

Two deeper tools: eigen analysis of a square matrix gives eigenvalues and eigenvectors — special directions that the matrix only stretches (doesn’t rotate), revealing the matrix’s core structure. For non-square (rectangular) matrices, the analogous tool is singular value decomposition (SVD), which breaks a matrix into simpler building blocks.

\[ \mathbf{A}\mathbf{v} = \lambda \mathbf{v} \quad (\mathbf{v} \text{ is an eigenvector, } \lambda \text{ is its eigenvalue}) \]

Why it matters: Nearly every ML method is, under the hood, operations on vectors and matrices. PCA, SVMs, and neural network layers all rely on these.

Calculus background

To “learn,” a model adjusts its internal parameters (the unknown numbers it tunes) to minimize an objective function (also called a loss function — a number measuring how wrong the model is). Finding the minimum requires derivatives, which tell you the slope: which way to nudge a parameter to reduce the loss.

You’ll go from single-variable differentiation, to multivariable, to differentiating with respect to whole vectors and matrices (because models have thousands or millions of parameters bundled together).

\[ \frac{d}{dx}\left( x^2 \right) = 2x \]

There are two flavors of optimization. Unconstrained optimization — no restrictions on the values. Constrained optimization — the variables must obey certain conditions, solved using the Lagrange multiplier method.

Why it matters: Training any model = solving an optimization problem. Gradient descent (the engine of deep learning) is calculus in action; SVMs use Lagrange multipliers directly.

Probability background

To capture the randomness and uncertainty in data, we treat features as random variables (quantities whose value isn’t fixed). They come in two kinds: discrete (countable values, like a dice roll) and continuous (any value in a range, like height).

For discrete variables we use a probability distribution; for continuous variables we use a probability density function (PDF). The single most important PDF is the Gaussian (also called the normal, or bell curve).

You’ll use the sum rule and product rule of probability to derive Bayes’ rule, which flips conditional probabilities — it lets you compute “probability of the cause given the evidence.”

\[ P(A \mid B) = \frac{P(B \mid A)\, P(A)}{P(B)} \]

Why it matters: Bayes’ rule is the backbone of probabilistic classifiers (Bayes and naive Bayes), and the Gaussian density underlies Gaussian mixture models and much of statistics.

Programming fundamentals

You’ll implement everything in Python, the dominant language for ML. The core toolkit: NumPy (fast array/matrix math), SciPy (scientific computing), scikit-learn (classical ML algorithms ready-made), Matplotlib (plotting and visualization), PyTorch and TensorFlow (the two main deep-learning frameworks). You’ll also cover deployment/productionization — turning a trained model into a usable product — and advanced Python: generators, iterators, decorators, context managers, and performance optimization.

Why it matters: Theory is useless without execution. These tools are how ideas become working systems.

Exploratory data analysis (EDA)

Before modeling, you inspect the data. EDA covers: types of data and variables; central tendency (mean, median, mode — the “typical” value); dispersion (spread, like variance/standard deviation); the five-point summary (min, Q1, median, Q3, max) and its box plot picture; skewness (asymmetry of the distribution); and covariance/correlation (how two variables move together). You’ll also learn to encode categorical data into numbers, and to scale/normalize features so they share a comparable range.

Why it matters: Garbage in, garbage out. Good scaling and clean data often matter more than the choice of algorithm.

Application domains

The three natural human communication modes — text (NLP: classification, sentiment analysis, summarization, translation, question answering, generation), visual (image/video: classification, annotation, clustering, retrieval, captioning, image-to-image translation, visual question answering, segmentation, generation), and audio (speech recognition, speaker/language/emotion identification, diarization, transcription, synthesis, speech-to-speech translation; and music: raga/instrument identification, transcription, retrieval, generation). Beyond these: autonomous vehicles, space-mission planning, recommendation systems, game playing, education, banking/fraud detection, and healthcare.

Why it matters: These domains are the “why” — concrete problems the techniques are built to solve.

Machine learning paradigms

Supervised learning uses labeled data (each example comes with the correct answer) — the standard for classification and regression. Unsupervised learning finds structure in unlabeled data (e.g., clustering). In between: semi-supervised (some labels, lots of unlabeled), self-supervised (the model invents its own labels), active learning (the model asks for the most useful labels), transfer learning / domain adaptation (reuse a model trained elsewhere), zero/one/few-shot learning (learn from zero, one, or very few examples), and federated learning (train across many devices without centralizing data).

Why it matters: Real projects rarely have abundant clean labels; knowing these paradigms lets you pick the right strategy for the data you actually have.

Classical machine learning techniques
