The single most important theoretical promise of the Bayes classifier is that it is the minimum-error (Bayes-optimal) classifier: if the true class-conditional densities \(P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_i)\) and priors \(P(c_i)\) are known exactly, no classifier — not an SVM, not a deep network — can achieve a lower expected error rate. The proof is short and worth internalising. At any point \(\mathbf{x}\), the probability of a mistake is \(1-\max_i P(c_i\mid\mathbf{x})\). To minimise the error pointwise (and hence on average over all \(\mathbf{x}\)), you must pick the class with the largest posterior at every \(\mathbf{x}\) — which is exactly the Bayes decision rule. The leftover error, \(\int \big(1-\max_i P(c_i\mid\mathbf{x})\big)P(\mathbf{x})\,d\mathbf{x}\), is the Bayes error rate, an irreducible floor caused by genuine class overlap. Everything we do in practice (assuming Gaussians, assuming independence, fitting GMMs) is an approximation of those true densities, so real classifiers sit somewhere above this floor. This reframes the whole week: we are not inventing classifiers, we are racing to estimate densities accurately enough to approach a known optimum.

A subtlety the transcript glosses over is what happens to the Gaussian decision boundary depending on the covariance structure. When two classes share the same covariance matrix \(\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_1=\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_2\), the quadratic term \(\mathbf{x}^\top\boldsymbol{\Sigma}^{-1}\mathbf{x}\) in the log-likelihood is identical for both classes and cancels, leaving a linear decision boundary — this special case is exactly Linear Discriminant Analysis (LDA). When the covariances differ, the quadratic term survives and the boundary is a conic section (a parabola, ellipse, or hyperbola) — this is Quadratic Discriminant Analysis (QDA). So the “full-covariance Gaussian Bayes” of this week is precisely QDA, and Naive Bayes with a shared diagonal covariance collapses toward LDA-like linear behaviour. The covariance assumption is therefore not a minor implementation detail; it directly controls how flexible (and how prone to over-fitting) your boundary is.

Two practical traps deserve emphasis. First, Laplace (add-one) smoothing for discrete Naive Bayes: if a feature value never co-occurred with a class in training, its count-based likelihood is exactly \(0\), and because Naive Bayes multiplies per-feature likelihoods, one zero annihilates the entire product — the class becomes impossible regardless of all other evidence (the “zero-frequency problem”). Smoothing replaces \(\frac{\text{count}}{N_c}\) with \(\frac{\text{count}+\alpha}{N_c+\alpha V}\), where \(V\) is the number of possible feature values and \(\alpha\) (usually \(1\)) is a pseudo-count, guaranteeing no probability is ever exactly zero. Second, log-space arithmetic at test time: just as MLE training uses log-likelihood to avoid underflow, prediction should compare \(\log P(c_i)+\sum_j \log P(x_j\mid c_i)\) rather than the raw product, because multiplying hundreds of small probabilities underflows to \(0.0\) in floating point. The argmax is unchanged by the monotonic log, but the numerics become stable.

Extra worked example — 1D Gaussian Bayes decision boundary. Consider two classes with univariate Gaussian likelihoods and equal priors \(P(c_1)=P(c_2)=0.5\). Class 1 (“short”) has \(\mu_1=100,\ \sigma_1^2=64\) (so \(\sigma_1=8\)); class 2 (“tall”) has \(\mu_2=130,\ \sigma_2^2=64\) (so \(\sigma_2=8\)). Because the priors and variances are equal, the boundary is where the two likelihoods cross, \(\mathcal{N}(x\mid\mu_1,\sigma^2)=\mathcal{N}(x\mid\mu_2,\sigma^2)\). Taking logs and cancelling the shared \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}}\) factor: \[-\frac{(x-\mu_1)^2}{2\sigma^2}=-\frac{(x-\mu_2)^2}{2\sigma^2}\ \Longrightarrow\ (x-\mu_1)^2=(x-\mu_2)^2.\] Expanding gives \(-2\mu_1 x+\mu_1^2=-2\mu_2 x+\mu_2^2\), so \(x^*=\frac{\mu_1+\mu_2}{2}=\frac{100+130}{2}=115\) — the boundary sits exactly at the midpoint of the means, as intuition demands for equal-variance, equal-prior Gaussians. Now suppose the prior shifts to \(P(c_1)=0.8,\ P(c_2)=0.2\). We solve \(\mathcal{N}(x\mid 100,64)\cdot 0.8=\mathcal{N}(x\mid 130,64)\cdot 0.2\). In log form the boundary equation becomes \[-\frac{(x-100)^2}{128}+\ln 0.8=-\frac{(x-130)^2}{128}+\ln 0.2.\] The quadratic terms again reduce to the linear relation \(\frac{(x-130)^2-(x-100)^2}{128}=\ln 0.8-\ln 0.2=\ln 4\approx1.386\). The numerator \((x-130)^2-(x-100)^2 = -60x+(130^2-100^2)= -60x+6900\), so \(\frac{-60x+6900}{128}=1.386\Rightarrow -60x+6900=177.4\Rightarrow x^*=\frac{6900-177.4}{60}\approx112.0\). The boundary moved toward the rarer class (from 115 down to ≈112), enlarging the decision region of the more common “short” class — exactly what a higher prior should do. To classify a test point \(x=118\): compare \(\log\) scores. For class 1: \(-\frac{(118-100)^2}{128}+\ln(0.8)=-\frac{324}{128}-0.223=-2.531-0.223=-2.754\). For class 2: \(-\frac{(118-130)^2}{128}+\ln(0.2)=-\frac{144}{128}-1.609=-1.125-1.609=-2.734\). Since \(-2.734>-2.754\), class 2 (“tall”) wins — even though \(118\) is numerically closer to \(\mu_1\)’s region, it lies just past the shifted boundary of \(112\), and the likelihood advantage of being nearer \(\mu_2=130\) overcomes the weaker prior.

Exam-Style Q&A — Week 16

Q1. State Bayes’ rule for classification and name each of the four quantities, explaining why the denominator can be dropped when comparing classes. A1. \(P(c_i\mid\mathbf{x})=\dfrac{P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_i)\,P(c_i)}{P(\mathbf{x})}\). Here \(P(c_i\mid\mathbf{x})\) is the posterior (probability of class \(i\) after seeing \(\mathbf{x}\)), \(P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_i)\) is the likelihood / class-conditional density (how well \(\mathbf{x}\) fits class \(i\)’s distribution), \(P(c_i)\) is the prior (estimated as \(n_i/n\)), and \(P(\mathbf{x})=\sum_{j}P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_j)P(c_j)\) is the evidence (a normaliser making posteriors sum to 1). The evidence is identical for every class, so \(\arg\max_i P(c_i\mid\mathbf{x})=\arg\max_i P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_i)P(c_i)\) — dropping the common denominator does not change which class is largest.

Q2. (Posterior computation.) Two classes have equal priors \(0.5\). For a test point, the likelihoods are \(P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_1)=4\times10^{-3}\) and \(P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_2)=1\times10^{-2}\). Compute both posteriors and give the predicted class. A2. Numerators (likelihood × prior): class 1 \(=4\times10^{-3}\times0.5=2\times10^{-3}\); class 2 \(=1\times10^{-2}\times0.5=5\times10^{-3}\). Evidence \(P(\mathbf{x})=2\times10^{-3}+5\times10^{-3}=7\times10^{-3}\). Posteriors: \(P(c_1\mid\mathbf{x})=\frac{2\times10^{-3}}{7\times10^{-3}}\approx0.286\) and \(P(c_2\mid\mathbf{x})=\frac{5\times10^{-3}}{7\times10^{-3}}\approx0.714\). Since \(0.714>0.286\), predict class 2. (Note the posteriors correctly sum to 1.)

Q3. Why does fitting a Gaussian by maximum likelihood reduce to the sample mean and sample covariance, and how many free parameters does a full-covariance Gaussian have in \(d\) dimensions? A3. Setting \(\frac{\partial}{\partial\boldsymbol\mu}\log L=\mathbf{0}\) and \(\frac{\partial}{\partial\boldsymbol\Sigma}\log L=\mathbf{0}\) for the Gaussian log-likelihood yields closed-form solutions: \(\hat{\boldsymbol\mu}=\frac1N\sum_n\mathbf{x}_n\) (the sample mean vector) and \(\hat{\boldsymbol\Sigma}=\frac1N\sum_n(\mathbf{x}_n-\hat{\boldsymbol\mu})(\mathbf{x}_n-\hat{\boldsymbol\mu})^\top\) (the sample covariance). The parameter count is \(d+\frac{d(d+1)}{2}\) — \(d\) means plus the unique entries of the symmetric covariance matrix. For \(d=2\) that is \(2+3=5\); for \(d=100\) it is \(100+5050=5150\), illustrating why high dimensions demand either Naive Bayes or large datasets.

Q4. What “naive” assumption defines Naive Bayes, what does it do to the covariance matrix and parameter count, and what is the zero-frequency problem (with its standard fix)? A4. It assumes features are conditionally independent given the class, so \(P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_i)=\prod_{j=1}^d P(x_j\mid c_i)\). This forces the covariance matrix to be diagonal (all off-diagonal covariances \(=0\)), cutting parameters from \(d+\frac{d(d+1)}{2}\) down to just \(d\) means \(+\ d\) variances. The zero-frequency problem: if a feature value never appeared with a class in training, its likelihood is \(0\), and the product zeroes out the whole class. The fix is Laplace smoothing: \(P(x_j\mid c_i)=\frac{\text{count}+\alpha}{N_{c_i}+\alpha V}\) with pseudo-count \(\alpha\) (typically 1) and \(V\) possible values.

Q5. In the EM algorithm for a GMM, write the E-step responsibility and explain how it is “Bayes’ rule over clusters.” What does the M-step do, and how is \(K\) chosen? A5. The E-step responsibility is \(\gamma(z_{nk})=\dfrac{w_k\,\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}_n\mid\boldsymbol\mu_k,\boldsymbol\Sigma_k)}{\sum_{j=1}^{K}w_j\,\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}_n\mid\boldsymbol\mu_j,\boldsymbol\Sigma_j)}\) — the posterior probability that component \(k\) generated \(\mathbf{x}_n\). Identifying the mixing weight \(w_k\) as a prior over clusters and the Gaussian as a likelihood, this is literally Bayes’ rule with clusters playing the role of classes; responsibilities sum to 1 across \(k\). The M-step treats responsibilities as soft counts and re-estimates weighted means, weighted covariances, and \(w_k=\frac{1}{N}\sum_n\gamma(z_{nk})\). Iterate E and M until the log-likelihood stops increasing (it never decreases). Choose \(K\) by the elbow method: plot total log-likelihood vs \(K\) and pick where the curve bends from steep to flat.

Quick-Revision Cheat-Sheet — Week 16

  • Bayes’ rule: \(P(c_i\mid\mathbf{x})=\dfrac{P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_i)\,P(c_i)}{P(\mathbf{x})}\); decide by \(\arg\max_i\). Drop \(P(\mathbf{x})\) when comparing.
  • Three ingredients: prior \(P(c_i)=n_i/n\) • likelihood / class-conditional density \(P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_i)\) • evidence \(P(\mathbf{x})=\sum_{i}P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_i)P(c_i)\).
  • Optimality: with the true densities, Bayes is the minimum-error classifier; the floor is the Bayes error rate from class overlap.
  • MLE: maximise \(\log L(\boldsymbol\theta)=\sum_n\log P(\mathbf{x}_n\mid\boldsymbol\theta)\) (log turns product→sum, avoids underflow); solve \(\frac{\partial}{\partial\boldsymbol\theta}\log L=\mathbf{0}\).
  • Gaussian MLE: \(\hat{\boldsymbol\mu}=\) sample mean vector, \(\hat{\boldsymbol\Sigma}=\) sample covariance matrix.
  • Univariate Gaussian: \(\mathcal{N}(x\mid\mu,\sigma^2)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}}\exp\!\big(-\frac{(x-\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2}\big)\); multivariate: \(\frac{1}{(2\pi)^{d/2}\sqrt{\det\boldsymbol\Sigma}}\exp\!\big(-\frac12(\mathbf{x}-\boldsymbol\mu)^\top\boldsymbol\Sigma^{-1}(\mathbf{x}-\boldsymbol\mu)\big)\).
  • Param count (full Gaussian): \(d+\frac{d(d+1)}{2}\) per class (\(d{=}2\to5\), \(d{=}100\to5150\)).
  • Decision boundary: equal \(\boldsymbol\Sigma\) → linear (LDA); different \(\boldsymbol\Sigma\) → quadratic (QDA). Equal var + equal prior in 1D → \(x^*=\frac{\mu_1+\mu_2}{2}\).
  • Naive Bayes: conditional independence → \(P(\mathbf{x}\mid c_i)=\prod_{j=1}^d P(x_j\mid c_i)\) = diagonal covariance; params drop to \(d\) means \(+\ d\) variances. Use Laplace smoothing \(\frac{\text{count}+\alpha}{N_c+\alpha V}\).
  • GMM: \(P(\mathbf{x})=\sum_{k=1}^{K}w_k\,\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}\mid\boldsymbol\mu_k,\boldsymbol\Sigma_k)\), with \(\sum_k w_k=1,\ w_k\ge0\); models multi-modal / arbitrary shapes.
  • EM E-step (responsibility): \(\gamma(z_{nk})=\frac{w_k\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}_n\mid\boldsymbol\mu_k,\boldsymbol\Sigma_k)}{\sum_j w_j\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}_n\mid\boldsymbol\mu_j,\boldsymbol\Sigma_j)}\) (Bayes over clusters); M-step: weighted \(\boldsymbol\mu_k,\boldsymbol\Sigma_k\), and \(w_k=\frac1N\sum_n\gamma(z_{nk})\).
  • Choosing K: elbow method on log-likelihood vs \(K\); ensure ≈10× more samples than parameters to avoid over-fitting (incomplete-data / latent cluster problem solved by EM).

Week 17: Support Vector Machines — I (Discriminant Functions & Perceptron)

In one line: Classification is really about learning a discriminant function whose sign tells you the class, and this week builds the foundation — what discriminant functions are, how they hide inside Bayes/kNN/GMM classifiers, and how the Perceptron learns a linear one directly from data.

What this week covers

  • Classification reframed as learning a discriminant function — the mapping \(y = f(\mathbf{x})\) learned during training that discriminates the region between classes and whose shape defines the decision boundary.
  • The two big families: non-discriminative learning (model a discriminant function per class, independent of other classes) vs discriminative learning (learn one boundary directly from the data of two or more classes).
  • The discriminant function inside the Gaussian Bayes classifier, and how the covariance matrix decides whether the boundary is linear or quadratic (three cases: same covariance + same variance, same covariance, arbitrary covariance).
  • Boundary shapes that kNN, Bayes (unimodal Gaussian), and Bayes (GMM) implicitly learn — piecewise/smooth non-linear, at-most-quadratic, and any-complex-non-linear respectively.
  • The linear discriminant function \(g(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0\), its positive and negative sides, and the augmented-vector form.
  • The Perceptron learning algorithm: update rule, learning rate (\(\eta\)), epochs, the convergence theorem, and its two key limitations.

The topics, explained simply

What a discriminant function is

During training a classifier learns a mapping from the input vector to its class label. That learned mapping is called the discriminant function — literally, the function that discriminates (separates) the regions of the classes. Everything on one side of it is one class; everything on the other side is the other class.

The shape this function takes is the decision boundary. It can be linear (a line in 2D, a plane in 3D, a hyperplane in higher dimensions) or non-linear (a curve or surface).

\[g(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0\]

Why it matters: Every classifier you have seen — kNN, Bayes — is secretly learning a discriminant function, even if it never wrote one down. Naming it lets you compare classifiers by the shape of boundary they can produce.

Two families: non-discriminative vs discriminative learning

There are two ways to get a discriminant function.

Non-discriminative (modelling) learning: define a separate discriminant function \(g_i(\mathbf{x}, \boldsymbol{\theta}_i)\) for each class \(C_i\), built only from that class’s own data, independent of the others. At test time you assign \(\mathbf{x}\) to whichever class gives the highest discriminant value.

\[\text{Class label for } \mathbf{x} = \arg\max_i \; g_i(\mathbf{x}, \boldsymbol{\theta}_i), \quad i = 1, 2, \ldots, M\]

Examples: kNN (\(g_i\) = Euclidean distance from \(\mathbf{x}\) to class \(i\)’s training data) and Bayes classifier (\(g_i\) = posterior probability of class \(i\)). These never learn a boundary directly; the boundary only appears when you test.

Discriminative learning: learn the separating surface directly from the data of at least two classes — you place the hyperplane (or curved surface) in the region of separation. Examples: Perceptron and SVM (linear), neural networks (non-linear).

Why it matters: This is the conceptual hinge of the whole week. SVM and the Perceptron belong to the second family — they go straight after the boundary instead of modelling each class separately.

The discriminant function inside the Gaussian Bayes classifier

For a Bayes classifier where each class is a multivariate unimodal Gaussian, the discriminant function for class \(i\) is its posterior probability. Since the denominator \(P(\mathbf{x})\) of Bayes’ rule is common to all classes, you keep only the numerator (likelihood × prior) and take the log:

\[g_i(\mathbf{x}, \boldsymbol{\theta}_i) = -\tfrac12 (\mathbf{x} - \boldsymbol{\mu}_i)^\top \Sigma_i^{-1} (\mathbf{x} - \boldsymbol{\mu}_i) - \tfrac12\ln(2\pi) - \tfrac12\ln(|\Sigma_i|) + \ln(P(C_i))\]

The boundary between two classes \(i\) and \(j\) is where their discriminant functions are equal:

\[g(\mathbf{x}) = g_i(\mathbf{x}, \boldsymbol{\theta}_i) - g_j(\mathbf{x}, \boldsymbol{\theta}_j) = 0\]

When you expand that subtraction, you get a general expression of the form \(-\tfrac12 \mathbf{x}^\top W \mathbf{x} + \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0 = 0\). Whether the quadratic term \(\mathbf{x}^\top W \mathbf{x}\) survives depends entirely on the covariance matrices.

Why it matters: The covariance matrix decides the boundary shape. Same covariance for both classes kills the quadratic term, leaving a line; different covariances keep it, giving a curve.

The three covariance cases (what decides linear vs quadratic)

Case 1 — \(\Sigma_i = \sigma^2 I\) (isotropic, same for all classes): features are independent and every variance is equal, so the density contours are perfect circles. The boundary is linear and is the perpendicular bisector of the line joining the two class centres. (If the priors are equal, it sits exactly in the middle.)

Case 2 — \(\Sigma_i = \Sigma\) (same covariance for both, but variances not all equal / full matrix): contours are tilted ellipses of the same shape. The boundary is still linear (a hyperplane) but is no longer the perpendicular bisector — it cuts the centre-joining line at an angle.

Case 3 — \(\Sigma_i\) arbitrary (different for each class): boundary becomes quadratic (hyper-quadratic in higher dimensions) — parabola, hyperbola, circle, ellipse, or pair of lines in 2D. If the covariances are diagonal, the boundary’s axes are parallel to the coordinate axes; if full, they are tilted.

Why it matters: Same covariance ⇒ linear boundary. Different covariance ⇒ at-most-quadratic boundary. This is the single most testable fact of the Bayes half of the week. The unimodal Gaussian Bayes classifier’s limitation is that it can never produce a boundary higher than second order.

Boundary shapes of kNN, Bayes, and GMM

  • kNN implicitly learns a non-linear boundary. For each test point, the boundary between its two nearest opposite-class neighbours is the perpendicular bisector of the segment joining them; stitching these together gives a piecewise-linear (\(k=1\)) boundary that smooths into a clean non-linear curve as \(k\) grows.
  • Bayes + unimodal Gaussian learns at most a quadratic boundary (see the three cases above).
  • Bayes + GMM (multimodal Gaussian) represents each class as a sum of several Gaussians. Each Gaussian pair contributes a quadratic piece, and stitching them together approximates any complex non-linear boundary — so GMM can separate shapes (e.g. interleaving moons) that a single Gaussian cannot.

Why it matters: This gives a clean ranking of expressive power: kNN (non-linear) and GMM (arbitrary non-linear) are flexible; unimodal Gaussian Bayes is capped at quadratic. It also motivates why we move to discriminative methods like SVM.

The linear discriminant function and its two sides

In 2D, the linear discriminant function is a line: \(w_1 x_1 + w_2 x_2 + w_0 = 0\). Rearranging into \(x_2 = m x_1 + c\) shows the weights are the line’s geometry: slope \(m = -w_1/w_2\) and y-intercept \(c = -w_0/w_2\).

In \(d\) dimensions it becomes a hyperplane. Augmenting the input with a constant \(x_0 = 1\) lets you fold \(w_0\) into the weight vector and write it as a single dot product:

\[g(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}} = \sum_{i=0}^{d} w_i x_i, \quad \text{with } x_0 = 1 \quad \left(\text{equivalently } g(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0\right)\]

A hyperplane splits space into a positive side (where \(g(\mathbf{x}) > 0\)) and a negative side (where \(g(\mathbf{x}) < 0\)); points on it give \(g(\mathbf{x}) = 0\).

Why it matters: This sign rule is the classifier. Compute \(g(\mathbf{x})\); positive ⇒ class \(+1\), negative ⇒ class \(-1\). Training labels are set as \(y_n = +1\) for the positive side and \(y_n = -1\) for the negative side.

The Perceptron learning algorithm

The Perceptron is the simplest discriminative linear classifier — a binary classifier that learns the hyperplane directly from data. Its one unrealistic assumption: the two classes are linearly separable.

It learns by adaptation (error correction). Start with some initial weight vector \(\mathbf{w}\). Pick a training example, check it, and if it is misclassified, nudge \(\mathbf{w}\) toward fixing it:

  • A positive example (\(y_n = +1\)) wrongly scored \(\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}} \le 0\): add to the weights.
  • A negative example (\(y_n = -1\)) wrongly scored \(\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}} > 0\): subtract from the weights.

\[\text{(update for a missed } +1\text{): } \mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} + \eta\,\hat{\mathbf{x}}\] \[\text{(update for a missed } -1\text{): } \mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} - \eta\,\hat{\mathbf{x}}\]

Here \(\eta\) is the learning rate, \(0 < \eta \le 1\) (it must be strictly positive — \(\eta = 0\) means no update ever happens). Large \(\eta\) moves fast but can oscillate between the classes before settling; small \(\eta\) converges slowly. A common heuristic: shrink \(\eta\) each epoch, e.g. \(\eta = 1/m\) at epoch \(m\) (1, then 0.5, then 0.33, …), but floor it (e.g. don’t let it drop below \(0.0001\)) so updates never stop.

An epoch = presenting all training examples once. Each epoch you reset the misclassification count, sweep every example, update on each mistake, and count the mistakes. You repeat epochs until the convergence criterion: an epoch with zero misclassifications. At that point \(\mathbf{w}\) defines the separating hyperplane.

\[\text{(test-time rule): assign } +1 \text{ if } \mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}} > 0, \text{ else assign } -1\]

Perceptron Convergence Theorem: if the classes are linearly separable, the algorithm is guaranteed to reach a solution in a finite number of steps.

Limitations (the motivation for SVM): 1. It fails on non-separable / overlapping data — it never converges. 2. Even when data is separable, the learned hyperplane is not optimal — it stops the instant training error hits zero, so it can sit right against one class and misclassify nearby unseen test points. The ideal hyperplane sits in the middle of the gap for best generalization. Getting that optimal middle hyperplane is exactly what the SVM delivers — the bridge to next week.

Deep-dive practical example

(A) Classifying 2D points by the sign of g(x)

Take the line \(g(\mathbf{x}) = x_1 + x_2 - 1\). Here \(w_1 = 1\), \(w_2 = 1\), \(w_0 = -1\). - Slope \(m = -w_1/w_2 = -1\); y-intercept \(c = -w_0/w_2 = -(-1)/1 = +1\). So the line passes through \((1, 0)\) and \((0, 1)\).

Now read the sign of \(g(\mathbf{x})\) for several points:

Point (x1, x2) g(x) = x1 + x2 - 1 Sign Verdict
(1, 0) 1 + 0 - 1 = 0 0 on the line (boundary)
(0, 1) 0 + 1 - 1 = 0 0 on the line (boundary)
(1, 1) 1 + 1 - 1 = +1 + positive side ⇒ class +1
(0, 0) 0 + 0 - 1 = -1 - negative side ⇒ class -1

So \((1,1)\) lies on the positive side and \((0,0)\) on the negative side — and the two points \((1,0)\) and \((0,1)\) confirm where the line actually is. The single computation \(g(\mathbf{x})\) plus its sign is the entire classification step.

(B) Perceptron weight updates, step by step (eta = 1)

Suppose we have two 1D points (made 2D by augmenting with \(x_0 = 1\), so \(\hat{\mathbf{x}} = [1, x]^\top\)): - Point A: \(x = 2\), true label +1, so \(\hat{\mathbf{x}}_A = [1, 2]^\top\). - Point B: \(x = -1\), true label -1, so \(\hat{\mathbf{x}}_B = [1, -1]^\top\).

Start with weights \(\mathbf{w} = [0, 0]^\top\) and \(\eta = 1\).

Epoch 1, example A: score \(\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}}_A = 0\cdot 1 + 0\cdot 2 = 0\). This is not \(> 0\), so the \(+1\) example is misclassified. Update (missed \(+1\), add): \(\mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} + \eta\,\hat{\mathbf{x}}_A = [0, 0]^\top + 1\cdot[1, 2]^\top = [1, 2]^\top\). (misclassification count = 1)

Epoch 1, example B: score \(\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}}_B = 1\cdot 1 + 2\cdot(-1) = 1 - 2 = -1\). This is \(\le 0\), which is correct for a \(-1\) example. No update. (count stays 1)

End of epoch 1: 1 misclassification ⇒ not converged, reset count, run epoch 2.

Epoch 2, example A: score \(\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}}_A = 1\cdot 1 + 2\cdot 2 = 1 + 4 = 5 > 0\). Correct (it’s \(+1\)). No update.

Epoch 2, example B: score \(\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}}_B = 1\cdot 1 + 2\cdot(-1) = -1 \le 0\). Correct (it’s \(-1\)). No update.

End of epoch 2: zero misclassifications ⇒ converged. Final weights \(\mathbf{w} = [1, 2]^\top\), i.e. boundary \(g(\mathbf{x}) = 1 + 2x = 0\), which sits at \(x = -0.5\) — neatly between A (\(x=2\), \(+1\) side) and B (\(x=-1\), \(-1\) side). Notice it converged in a finite number of steps exactly because the two points are linearly separable — the convergence theorem in action.

Key takeaways

  • A classifier’s job is to learn a discriminant function; its shape is the decision boundary, and you classify by reading the sign of \(g(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0\).
  • Non-discriminative methods (kNN, Bayes) model one function per class independently and only reveal their boundary at test time; discriminative methods (Perceptron, SVM, neural nets) learn the boundary directly.
  • In Gaussian Bayes, the covariance matrix decides boundary shape: same covariance ⇒ linear (perpendicular bisector only when variances are also equal); different covariance ⇒ quadratic / hyper-quadratic.
  • Expressive-power ranking of implicit boundaries: kNN (non-linear) ≈ GMM Bayes (any complex non-linear) > unimodal Gaussian Bayes (capped at quadratic).
  • The Perceptron updates \(\mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} + \eta\,\hat{\mathbf{x}}\) for a missed \(+1\) and \(\mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} - \eta\,\hat{\mathbf{x}}\) for a missed \(-1\), sweeping the data in epochs until an epoch has zero mistakes; \(0 < \eta \le 1\).
  • The Convergence Theorem guarantees a solution in finite steps if the data is linearly separable.
  • Two Perceptron limitations — fails on non-separable data and yields a non-optimal (non-maximal-margin) hyperplane — are the direct motivation for the Support Vector Machine.

Going Deeper — Week 17

Why the covariance matrix algebraically picks linear vs. quadratic. The existing notes state the rule (same covariance ⇒ linear, different covariance ⇒ quadratic), but it pays to see where the quadratic term physically goes. Write the per-class log-discriminant in its expanded form, dropping the constant \(-\tfrac12\ln(2\pi)\) that cancels across classes:

\[g_i(\mathbf{x}) = -\tfrac12\,\mathbf{x}^\top\Sigma_i^{-1}\mathbf{x} + \boldsymbol{\mu}_i^\top\Sigma_i^{-1}\mathbf{x} - \tfrac12\boldsymbol{\mu}_i^\top\Sigma_i^{-1}\boldsymbol{\mu}_i - \tfrac12\ln|\Sigma_i| + \ln P(C_i)\]

The decision boundary is \(g_i(\mathbf{x}) - g_j(\mathbf{x}) = 0\). Look only at the second-order piece: \(-\tfrac12\,\mathbf{x}^\top(\Sigma_i^{-1} - \Sigma_j^{-1})\mathbf{x}\). If \(\Sigma_i = \Sigma_j\), then \(\Sigma_i^{-1} - \Sigma_j^{-1} = \mathbf{0}\) and the entire \(\mathbf{x}^\top(\cdot)\mathbf{x}\) term vanishes — what remains is purely \(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0\), a hyperplane. The \(-\tfrac12\ln|\Sigma_i|\) terms also cancel. This is the algebra behind “same covariance ⇒ linear.” When the covariances differ, \(\Sigma_i^{-1} - \Sigma_j^{-1} \neq \mathbf{0}\) survives, and a non-zero quadratic form \(\mathbf{x}^\top W\mathbf{x}\) is exactly what bends the boundary into a conic (parabola/hyperbola/ellipse) — capped at degree 2 because \(\mathbf{x}\) never appears at a power above 2.

Worked example — same vs. different covariance, same two means. Put \(\boldsymbol{\mu}_1 = (0,0)\), \(\boldsymbol{\mu}_2 = (4,0)\), equal priors. Case A (shared \(\Sigma = I\)): both inverse matrices are \(I\), so \(\Sigma_1^{-1}-\Sigma_2^{-1}=\mathbf{0}\). The boundary collapses to \(\boldsymbol{\mu}_1^\top\mathbf{x} - \boldsymbol{\mu}_2^\top\mathbf{x} + \text{const} = 0\), i.e. \(-4x_1 + 8 = 0 \Rightarrow x_1 = 2\) — a vertical line, the perpendicular bisector of the two centres, exactly Case 1. Case B (different covariances, \(\Sigma_1 = I\) but \(\Sigma_2 = \text{diag}(4,1)\)): now \(\Sigma_1^{-1} - \Sigma_2^{-1} = \text{diag}(1 - \tfrac14,\,1-1) = \text{diag}(\tfrac34, 0)\). The quadratic term \(-\tfrac12(\tfrac34 x_1^2)\) refuses to cancel, and additionally \(-\tfrac12\ln|\Sigma_2| = -\tfrac12\ln 4 \neq 0\) shifts the constant. The boundary equation now contains an \(x_1^2\) term — a parabola, not a line. Two identical means, and the only thing that flipped the boundary from a straight line to a curve was the covariance choice. That is the whole lesson of the Bayes half of the week in one comparison.

The perceptron’s “stop at zero error” flaw, made concrete. The transcript stresses that the perceptron halts the instant training error hits zero — it never tries to centre the hyperplane in the gap. Geometrically, the final \(\mathbf{w}\) can lie flush against the last-corrected example. If a test point sits just past that example on the wrong side, it is misclassified even though a hyperplane parked in the middle of the margin would have caught it. This is not a tuning bug; it is structural. The perceptron has no notion of distance to the boundary in its objective — it only counts sign errors. SVM repairs precisely this by maximising the margin (the distance from the boundary to the nearest point of either class), which is why next week’s SVM is framed directly as “the perceptron, but with the optimal hyperplane.”

A subtlety about the learning rate. For a linearly separable problem the perceptron converges regardless of \(\eta\) (the convergence theorem doesn’t depend on \(\eta\) — scaling all updates by a constant only rescales \(\mathbf{w}\), not its sign behaviour). So why bother tuning \(\eta\) at all? Because in practice you watch the trajectory: a large \(\eta\) can make \(\mathbf{w}\) overshoot and oscillate across the gap for many epochs before settling, while a tiny \(\eta\) crawls. The \(\eta = 1/m\) schedule (with a floor like \(0.0001\) so it never hits zero) gives big early corrections and gentle late ones — fast approach, no end-stage oscillation. The floor matters: once \(\eta = 0\), \(\eta\,\hat{\mathbf{x}} = \mathbf{0}\), updates die, and a not-yet-separated dataset can never converge.

Extra worked example — Perceptron update sequence with three points, \(\eta = 1\)

Take three augmented points (\(\hat{\mathbf{x}} = [1, x_1, x_2]^\top\)), starting from \(\mathbf{w} = [0,0,0]^\top\):

pt \(\hat{\mathbf{x}}\) true \(y\)
A \([1, 2, 1]^\top\) \(+1\)
B \([1, -1, -1]^\top\) \(-1\)
C \([1, 0, 2]^\top\) \(+1\)

Rule: misclassified iff (\(y=+1\) and \(\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}} \le 0\)) or (\(y=-1\) and \(\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}} > 0\)); update \(\mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} + y\,\hat{\mathbf{x}}\).

Epoch 1 (start \(\mathbf{w}=[0,0,0]\)): - A: score \(=0 \le 0\), but \(y=+1\) ⇒ miss. \(\mathbf{w} \leftarrow [0,0,0]+[1,2,1]=[1,2,1]\). - B: score \(=1(1)+2(-1)+1(-1)=1-2-1=-2 \le 0\), \(y=-1\) ⇒ correct. No update. - C: score \(=1(1)+2(0)+1(2)=1+0+2=3 > 0\), \(y=+1\) ⇒ correct. No update. - Misclassifications this epoch: 1 ⇒ not converged.

Epoch 2 (start \(\mathbf{w}=[1,2,1]\)): - A: score \(=1+4+1=6>0\), \(y=+1\) ⇒ correct. - B: score \(=1-2-1=-2\le0\), \(y=-1\) ⇒ correct. - C: score \(=1+0+2=3>0\), \(y=+1\) ⇒ correct. - Misclassifications: 0 ⇒ converged.

Final \(\mathbf{w}=[1,2,1]\), boundary \(g(\mathbf{x}) = 1 + 2x_1 + x_2 = 0\). Check it separates: A and C give \(+6\) and \(+3\) (positive side), B gives \(-2\) (negative side). Just one correction sufficed because the three points are comfortably linearly separable — the convergence theorem guarantees finite termination, and here “finite” was a single update.

Exam-Style Q&A — Week 17

Q1. A Gaussian Bayes classifier is fit to two classes with \(\boldsymbol{\mu}_1=(1,1)\), \(\boldsymbol{\mu}_2=(5,1)\). In experiment X both classes share \(\Sigma = \text{diag}(2,2)\); in experiment Y class 1 has \(\Sigma_1=\text{diag}(2,2)\) and class 2 has \(\Sigma_2=\text{diag}(2,8)\). State the boundary shape in each and justify in one line. A1. Experiment X: linear boundary, because \(\Sigma_1=\Sigma_2 \Rightarrow \Sigma_1^{-1}-\Sigma_2^{-1}=\mathbf{0}\), so the quadratic term cancels (and here it is the perpendicular bisector \(x_1=3\) since variances are equal and priors assumed equal). Experiment Y: quadratic boundary, because \(\Sigma_1^{-1}-\Sigma_2^{-1}=\text{diag}(0,\,\tfrac12-\tfrac18)=\text{diag}(0,\tfrac38)\neq\mathbf{0}\), leaving an \(x_2^2\) term.

Q2. Classify the point \(\mathbf{x}=(2,-3)\) using the linear discriminant \(g(\mathbf{x}) = 2x_1 + x_2 - 1\), and state which side of the hyperplane it lies on. A2. \(g(2,-3) = 2(2) + (-3) - 1 = 4 - 3 - 1 = 0\). The sign is zero, so the point lies exactly on the hyperplane — it is a boundary point, assignable to neither side strictly (by the test-time tie rule \(\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}}\le 0 \Rightarrow\) class \(-1\), it would be labelled \(-1\)).

Q3. Run one perceptron update. Current \(\mathbf{w}=[0,1,-1]^\top\) (augmented, \(\hat{\mathbf{x}}=[1,x_1,x_2]^\top\)), \(\eta=1\). The example is \(x=(1,2)\) with true label \(y=+1\). Is it misclassified, and what is the new \(\mathbf{w}\)? A3. \(\hat{\mathbf{x}}=[1,1,2]^\top\); score \(=\mathbf{w}^\top\hat{\mathbf{x}} = 0(1)+1(1)+(-1)(2) = 1-2 = -1 \le 0\). Since \(y=+1\) but the score is non-positive, it is misclassified. Update (add, because missed \(+1\)): \(\mathbf{w} \leftarrow [0,1,-1] + 1\cdot[1,1,2] = [1,2,1]^\top\).

Q4. Why does the perceptron yield a non-optimal hyperplane even on linearly separable data, and which algorithm fixes this and how? A4. Its objective only counts sign errors, so it stops the instant training error reaches zero — the boundary can rest flush against the last-corrected point with no margin, misclassifying nearby unseen test points. The SVM fixes it by maximising the margin (placing the hyperplane in the middle of the gap, maximising distance to the nearest point of each class), giving better generalisation.

Q5. A practitioner schedules \(\eta = 1/m\) at epoch \(m\) but the perceptron never converges on clearly separable data. What is the likely cause and the fix? A5. As \(m\) grows, \(\eta \to 0\), so updates \(\eta\,\hat{\mathbf{x}} \to \mathbf{0}\) and the weights effectively stop changing before separation is reached (if \(\eta\) ever hits 0 exactly, no update can ever occur). Fix: floor \(\eta\) at a small positive constant (e.g. \(0.0001\)) so corrections continue. \(\eta\) must always satisfy \(0 < \eta \le 1\).

Quick-Revision Cheat-Sheet — Week 17

  • Discriminant function: \(g(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0\); its shape is the decision boundary, its sign gives the class.
  • Sign → class: \(g(\mathbf{x})>0 \Rightarrow\) class \(+1\) (positive side); \(g(\mathbf{x})<0 \Rightarrow\) class \(-1\); \(g(\mathbf{x})=0 \Rightarrow\) on the boundary.
  • Non-discriminative (kNN, Bayes): model \(g_i(\mathbf{x},\boldsymbol{\theta}_i)\) per class independently, assign \(\arg\max_i g_i\); boundary only emerges at test time.
  • Discriminative (Perceptron, SVM, NN): learn the separating surface directly from \(\ge 2\) classes’ data.
  • Bayes log-discriminant: \(g_i(\mathbf{x}) = -\tfrac12(\mathbf{x}-\boldsymbol{\mu}_i)^\top\Sigma_i^{-1}(\mathbf{x}-\boldsymbol{\mu}_i) - \tfrac12\ln|\Sigma_i| + \ln P(C_i)\); boundary at \(g_i - g_j = 0\).
  • Covariance → boundary: quadratic term is \(-\tfrac12\mathbf{x}^\top(\Sigma_i^{-1}-\Sigma_j^{-1})\mathbf{x}\) — vanishes iff \(\Sigma_i=\Sigma_j\).
  • Three cases: \(\Sigma=\sigma^2 I\) (isotropic, shared) ⇒ linear = perpendicular bisector; \(\Sigma_i=\Sigma\) (shared, unequal variances) ⇒ linear but tilted; \(\Sigma_i\) arbitrary ⇒ quadratic / hyper-quadratic.
  • Expressive ranking: kNN (piecewise→smooth non-linear) ≈ GMM-Bayes (any complex non-linear) > unimodal-Gaussian Bayes (capped at degree 2).
  • Perceptron update: missed \(+1\): \(\mathbf{w}\leftarrow\mathbf{w}+\eta\hat{\mathbf{x}}\); missed \(-1\): \(\mathbf{w}\leftarrow\mathbf{w}-\eta\hat{\mathbf{x}}\) (compactly \(\mathbf{w}\leftarrow\mathbf{w}+\eta\,y_n\hat{\mathbf{x}}\)).
  • Learning rate: \(0<\eta\le 1\) (\(\eta=0\) ⇒ no update, never converges); schedule \(\eta=1/m\) with a floor (e.g. \(0.0001\)); large \(\eta\) oscillates, small \(\eta\) crawls.
  • Epoch & convergence: one epoch = one full pass; stop when an epoch has zero misclassifications. Convergence Theorem: finite steps to a solution iff data is linearly separable.
  • Two limitations (→ SVM): (1) fails / never converges on non-separable or overlapping data; (2) even when separable, the hyperplane is non-optimal (zero margin) — SVM’s max-margin boundary is the fix.

Week 18: Support Vector Machines — II (Margins, Kernels & Multiclass)

In one line: SVM finds the unique separating boundary that sits exactly in the middle of the gap between two classes (the maximum-margin hyperplane), then extends to messy and curved data using slack variables and the kernel trick, and to many classes via one-vs-rest or one-vs-one.

What this week covers

  • Optimal separating hyperplane and the idea of a margin (distance of the nearest training point to the boundary).
  • Maximum-margin hyperplane — Case 1 (hard margin, linearly separable): the primal problem, its dual, support vectors, and the KKT condition.
  • Case 2 (linearly non-separable, soft margin): slack variables (zeta), hinge loss, the trade-off parameter C, and how the dual changes (alpha bounded by C).
  • Case 3 (non-linear): the kernel trick, Cover’s theorem, and learning a linear boundary in a transformed space that is non-linear in the input space.
  • Kernel functions (linear, polynomial, Gaussian/RBF), Mercer’s theorem, the kernel Gram matrix (symmetric + positive semi-definite), and kernel parameters as hyperparameters.
  • Multiclass SVM: one-vs-rest (winner-takes-all) and one-vs-one (majority voting), how many SVMs each needs, and when to prefer one.
  • Why SVM is free from the curse of dimensionality (parameters scale with number of training samples, not data dimension).

The topics, explained simply

Optimal separating hyperplane and the margin

When two classes are linearly separable, there are infinitely many lines (hyperplanes) that sit in the gap between them and classify the training data correctly. They are not equally good. The perceptron stops at any one of them, even one that hugs one class closely. SVM instead looks for the single best one: the hyperplane that sits exactly in the middle of the gap, equidistant from the nearest points of both classes. This is the “optimal” separating hyperplane, because a line in the middle leaves the most room on both sides, so more unseen test points fall on the correct side and get classified correctly (better generalization).

To make “middle” precise we need a notion of distance. The discriminant (decision) function of a hyperplane is \(g(\mathbf{x})\), and the distance of any point \(\mathbf{x}\) to the hyperplane is the magnitude of \(g(\mathbf{x})\) divided by the length of the weight vector \(\mathbf{w}\).

\[g(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0\] \[\text{distance of } \mathbf{x} \text{ to hyperplane} = \frac{|g(\mathbf{x})|}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert}\]

The margin of a hyperplane is then defined as the distance of the nearest training example to it. Maximizing this margin is the whole game.

Why it matters: A bigger margin is a buffer zone. A boundary jammed against one class misclassifies the first slightly-shifted test point; a centered, wide-margin boundary tolerates noise and generalizes better.

Maximum-margin hyperplane — Case 1: hard margin (linearly separable)

We can freely rescale \(\mathbf{w}\) and \(w_0\) without moving the actual boundary (only the magnitude changes, not the direction). We pick the scaling so that the nearest points satisfy \(|g(\mathbf{x})| = 1\). This is called the canonical separating hyperplane. With that convention, the two margin lines become \(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0 = +1\) (positive side) and \(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0 = -1\) (negative side), and the full width between them is \(\frac{2}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert}\).

\[\text{margin width} = \frac{2}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert}\]

Every training point must lie on its own side of the gap. Using class labels \(y_n \in \{+1, -1\}\), this single constraint captures both sides at once:

\[y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n + w_0) \ge 1, \quad \text{for all } n = 1, 2, \ldots, N\]

Maximizing \(\frac{2}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert}\) is the same as minimizing \(\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert\) (reciprocal), which is the same as minimizing \(\tfrac12\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert^2\) (squaring removes the square root and the \(\tfrac12\) makes the derivative clean). So the learning problem is:

\[\min_{\mathbf{w}, w_0} J(\mathbf{w}, w_0) = \tfrac12\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert^2 \quad \text{subject to} \quad y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n + w_0) \ge 1 \text{ for all } n\]

This is a constrained optimization, so we use a Lagrangian. Each constraint gets a Lagrange multiplier \(\alpha_n\) (always \(\ge 0\)). Setting the derivatives with respect to \(\mathbf{w}\) and \(w_0\) to zero gives two key results: \(\mathbf{w}\) is a weighted sum of the data, and the alphas are balanced across the two classes.

\[\mathbf{w} = \sum_n \alpha_n y_n \mathbf{x}_n\] \[\sum_n \alpha_n y_n = 0\]

Substituting these back turns the problem into the dual (a maximization in the alphas only):

\[\max_{\alpha} J(\alpha) = \sum_n \alpha_n - \tfrac12 \sum_m \sum_n \alpha_m \alpha_n y_m y_n (\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n), \quad \text{with } \alpha_n \ge 0 \text{ and } \sum_n \alpha_n y_n = 0\]

This is a quadratic optimization problem, solved in practice with Sequential Minimal Optimization (SMO).

Why it matters: The dual depends on the data only through dot products \(\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n\). This single fact is what later lets us swap in a kernel and handle curved boundaries without ever leaving this framework.

Support vectors and the KKT condition

After solving the dual, most alpha values come out as zero; only a few are non-zero. The training points with non-zero alphas are the support vectors — they are exactly the points sitting on the margin lines. They are the only points that matter; the rest can be thrown away.

This is enforced by the Karush-Kuhn-Tucker (KKT) condition:

\[\alpha_n \left[ y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n + w_0) - 1 \right] = 0, \quad \text{for all } n\]

Read it as a product that must equal zero. If \(\alpha_n\) is non-zero, the bracket must be zero, meaning \(y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n + w_0) = 1\) — the point is exactly on the margin (a support vector). If the point is strictly past the margin (the bracket is positive), then \(\alpha_n\) must be zero. The optimal weight vector is therefore a sum over support vectors only:

\[\mathbf{w} = \sum_{\text{support vectors}} \alpha_n y_n \mathbf{x}_n\]

At test time, compute \(g(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + w_0\); assign \(\mathbf{x}\) to class \(C_1\) if \(g(\mathbf{x}) \ge 0\), else \(C_2\).

Why it matters: The model is defined by a handful of border points, which makes it compact and is the reason SVM is so memory- and computation-light at prediction time.

Case 2: soft margin (linearly non-separable)

Real data overlap. A few points sit inside the gap or on the wrong side of the boundary. A hard margin is impossible, so we allow controlled violations using a slack variable \(\zeta_n\) per point that measures how far a point deviates from where it “should” be. Points on or beyond their correct margin have \(\zeta = 0\); points that merely cross into the gap have \(0 < \zeta < 1\); misclassified points have \(\zeta > 1\). Slack is computed by the hinge loss:

\[\zeta_n = \max(0,\, 1 - y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n + w_0))\]

The constraint relaxes from “\(\ge 1\)” to “\(\ge 1 - \zeta_n\)”, and we add the total slack to the objective, weighted by a trade-off parameter C:

\[\min_{\mathbf{w}, w_0, \zeta} J(\mathbf{w}, w_0, \zeta) = \tfrac12\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert^2 + C \sum_n \zeta_n, \quad \text{subject to} \quad y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n + w_0) \ge 1 - \zeta_n \text{ and } \zeta_n \ge 0\]

C is a user-chosen hyperparameter balancing a wide margin against few errors. Small C: errors weighted lightly, so the margin can be wide but more points are allowed to violate it. Large C: errors weighted heavily, so the model fits training points tightly with a narrower margin. The margin you get here is called a soft margin.

The dual is identical to Case 1 except the multipliers are now bounded above by C:

\[0 \le \alpha_n \le C\]

The KKT condition gains a second part for the slack multipliers. Support vectors are now the points on the margin plus all points causing error (margin violations and misclassifications).

Why it matters: Soft margin is what makes SVM usable on real, noisy data; C is one of the two knobs (with the kernel parameter) you actually tune in practice.

Case 3: non-linear data and the kernel trick

When classes are separated by a curved (e.g. circular or elliptical) boundary, no straight line works in the input space. The idea: map the data through a non-linear function \(\phi\) into a much higher-dimensional feature space. Cover’s theorem says that data which is non-linearly separable in the low-dimensional input space tends to become linearly separable (or close to it) in a high-enough feature space. So we map up, run the same linear SVM there, and the linear boundary we learn there corresponds to a curved boundary back in the input space.

The catch: we don’t know which \(\phi\) to use, and the feature space can be enormous (even infinite). The escape is the kernel trick. Recall the SVM only ever needs dot products between examples. A kernel function \(K\) computes, directly from the original input vectors, a value equal to the dot product of their images in feature space — without ever building \(\phi\) explicitly.

\[K(\mathbf{x}_m, \mathbf{x}_n) = \phi(\mathbf{x}_m)^\top \phi(\mathbf{x}_n)\]

We just replace every dot product \(\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n\) in the dual (and in the test-time discriminant) with \(K(\mathbf{x}_m, \mathbf{x}_n)\). The test discriminant becomes a sum of kernels between the test point and each support vector:

\[g(\mathbf{x}) = \sum_{\text{support vectors}} \alpha_n y_n K(\mathbf{x}_n, \mathbf{x}) + w_0\]

Why it matters: The kernel trick lets SVM learn arbitrarily complex non-linear boundaries at the cost of linear math, never paying for the high-dimensional space directly. This is the single most important idea in the week.

Kernel functions and Mercer’s theorem

A kernel acts as a similarity measure between a pair of examples. To be a valid kernel (to genuinely correspond to some dot product in a feature space) it must satisfy Mercer’s theorem. A practical, checkable sufficient condition: form the kernel Gram matrix \(K\) (the \(N\)-by-\(N\) matrix of \(K(\mathbf{x}_m, \mathbf{x}_n)\) over all training pairs). It must be (a) symmetric — \(K(\mathbf{x}_m, \mathbf{x}_n) = K(\mathbf{x}_n, \mathbf{x}_m)\) — and (b) positive semi-definite — its eigenvalues are all non-negative. PSD-ness is what guarantees the quadratic optimization converges.

Common kernels: - Linear kernel: \(K(\mathbf{x}_m, \mathbf{x}_n) = \mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n\). Just the input-space dot product; gives a linear boundary (no real transformation). - Polynomial kernel: \[K(\mathbf{x}_m, \mathbf{x}_n) = (a\,\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n + b)^p\] where \(a\) is a multiplicative coefficient, \(b\) an additive coefficient, \(p\) the polynomial degree. Implicitly maps to a feature space of all monomials up to degree \(p\). - Gaussian / RBF kernel: \[K(\mathbf{x}_m, \mathbf{x}_n) = \exp\!\left(-\frac{\lVert\mathbf{x}_m - \mathbf{x}_n\rVert^2}{\sigma}\right)\] where \(\sigma\) is the width parameter. It resembles a Gaussian centered at \(\mathbf{x}_n\); because it contains polynomial terms of all degrees, its feature space is effectively infinite-dimensional.

The kernel choice and its parameters (\(a\), \(b\), \(p\), or \(\sigma\)) are hyperparameters tuned experimentally.

Why it matters: RBF is the default workhorse: an infinite-dimensional feature space means almost any data becomes linearly separable there, so a well-tuned RBF SVM gives near-optimal non-linear boundaries.

Multiclass classification with SVM

SVM is fundamentally a binary classifier, so a C-class problem is decomposed into several binary problems whose outputs are combined.

  • One-vs-Rest (OvR): Build one SVM per class — that class as positive, all other classes lumped as negative. Number of SVMs \(= C\). A test point is run through all of them and the class whose SVM gives the highest discriminant value wins (winner-takes-all).
  • One-vs-One (OvO): Build one SVM for every pair of classes. Number of SVMs \(= \frac{C(C-1)}{2}\). Each SVM votes for one of its two classes; the class with the most votes (majority/max-wins voting) is the prediction.

Both usually give comparable accuracy. The interesting trade-off appears with many classes. Suppose 1000 classes, 100 samples each. OvR builds only 1000 SVMs, but each is trained on all 100,000 samples and is badly class-imbalanced (100 positive vs ~99,900 negative). OvO builds ~500,000 SVMs, but each trains on just 200 balanced samples, so each optimization is small and fast. With many classes, OvO is often faster overall and easier on the optimizer despite building far more SVMs. With few classes it doesn’t matter. (Library defaults differ: scikit-learn and LIBSVM default to OvO; LIBLINEAR, SVM-light, SVM-torch default to OvR.)

Why it matters: Knowing the per-SVM training size and balance — not just the SVM count — is the real basis for choosing OvO vs OvR.

Why SVM is free from the curse of dimensionality

For most classifiers, more input dimensions means more parameters to estimate, and without enough samples the model becomes suboptimal. SVM’s parameters are the Lagrange multipliers \(\alpha\), which are tied to training samples, not to data dimensions. Even when an RBF kernel works in an infinite-dimensional space, the number of parameters equals the number of training examples. The minimum to build an SVM is two points (one per class).

Why it matters: This is why SVM trains reliably even in very high-dimensional spaces (like the kernel feature space) where other classifiers would need impossibly many samples.

Deep-dive practical example

(A) A 1-D / 2-D maximum-margin computation with support vectors and margin width

Take a simple 1-D problem along a single axis. Class \(-1\) has a point at \(x = 1\); class \(+1\) has a point at \(x = 3\). By symmetry the optimal boundary sits exactly in the middle, at \(x = 2\), which is equidistant (distance 1) from both points. Both points are the nearest examples, so both are support vectors, and the margin (nearest-point distance) is 1, giving a full margin width of 2.

Now derive the canonical SVM weights. In 1-D, \(g(x) = w x + w_0\). We need the support vectors to sit on the margin lines, i.e. \(g = +1\) at \(x = 3\) and \(g = -1\) at \(x = 1\): - \(w \cdot 3 + w_0 = +1\) - \(w \cdot 1 + w_0 = -1\)

Subtracting: \(2w = 2\), so \(w = 1\). Then \(w_0 = -1 - w \cdot 1 = -2\). Check the margin width: \(\frac{2}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert} = \frac{2}{1} = 2\) — exactly the gap we expected. The boundary is at \(g(x) = x - 2 = 0\), i.e. \(x = 2\).

Verify the labels: at \(x = 3\), \(g = 1 > 0 \to\) class \(+1\) (correct); at \(x = 1\), \(g = -1 < 0 \to\) class \(-1\) (correct). A new test point at \(x = 2.4\) gives \(g = 0.4 > 0 \to\) class \(+1\), comfortably inside the margin’s correct side. This is the entire SVM idea in miniature: only the two border points define everything, and the boundary is centered for maximum buffer.

(B) Kernel trick: mapping circular (non-separable) data into a higher dimension

Consider 2-D data where one class sits inside a circle and the other outside — say inner-class points near the origin like \((0, 0)\) and \((0.5, 0.5)\), and outer-class points like \((3, 0)\), \((0, 3)\), \((-3, 0)\). No straight line separates inside-the-circle from outside-the-circle in the \((x_1, x_2)\) plane.

Apply a degree-2 polynomial map to a 3-D feature space using the monomials \(z_1 = x_1^2\), \(z_2 = x_2^2\), \(z_3 = \sqrt{2}\, x_1 x_2\):

\[\phi(x_1, x_2) = \left( x_1^2,\; x_2^2,\; \sqrt{2}\, x_1 x_2 \right)\]

Now look at \(z_1 + z_2 = x_1^2 + x_2^2\), which is the squared radius. Inner points have small \(z_1 + z_2\) (e.g. \((0,0) \to 0\); \((0.5,0.5) \to 0.5\)), outer points have large \(z_1 + z_2\) (e.g. \((3,0) \to 9\); \((0,3) \to 9\)). In the 3-D feature space the two classes now separate by a flat plane like \(z_1 + z_2 = 4\) (a constant-radius threshold). That plane is a perfectly linear boundary in feature space — and mapping it back to the input space, \(z_1 + z_2 = 4\) is the circle \(x_1^2 + x_2^2 = 4\). A linear cut up top became the desired circular boundary down below.

The kernel trick means we never compute \(\phi\). Using the polynomial kernel \(K(\mathbf{x}_m, \mathbf{x}_n) = (\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n + 1)^2\) with \(a = 1\), \(b = 1\), \(p = 2\), one can expand it and confirm it equals \(\phi(\mathbf{x}_m)^\top \phi(\mathbf{x}_n)\) for the degree-2 monomial map (including the \(\sqrt{2}\) cross-term that makes the algebra match). So we get the 3-D (here 6-monomial) feature-space dot product for free, straight from the 2-D inputs. The XOR problem works the same way: XOR is not linearly separable in 2-D, but adding the product feature \(x_1 x_2\) (exactly the degree-2 cross term) makes the four points linearly separable in the lifted space. For circular data with no known polynomial degree, the RBF kernel \(\exp\!\left(-\frac{\lVert\mathbf{x}_m - \mathbf{x}_n\rVert^2}{\sigma}\right)\) does the same job implicitly in an infinite-dimensional space.

Key takeaways

  • The optimal separating hyperplane is the maximum-margin hyperplane: centered in the gap, equidistant from the nearest points of both classes, for best generalization.
  • Margin width \(= \frac{2}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert}\), so maximizing the margin \(=\) minimizing \(\tfrac12\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert^2\) subject to \(y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n + w_0) \ge 1\).
  • Solving the dual yields support vectors (non-zero alphas); only they define the boundary, enforced by the KKT condition.
  • Hard margin (Case 1) requires perfect separation; soft margin (Case 2) allows errors via slack \(\zeta\) and hinge loss, traded off by C (which also bounds alpha: \(0 \le \alpha \le C\)).
  • The kernel trick replaces dot products with \(K(\mathbf{x}_m, \mathbf{x}_n) = \phi(\mathbf{x}_m)^\top \phi(\mathbf{x}_n)\), learning a linear boundary in a high-dimensional space that is non-linear in the input space (Cover’s theorem).
  • Valid kernels satisfy Mercer’s theorem; check via a symmetric, positive semi-definite Gram matrix. Common kernels: linear, polynomial \((a\,\mathbf{x}^\top\mathbf{x} + b)^p\), and Gaussian/RBF (infinite-dimensional, the default workhorse).
  • Kernel choice and its parameters (\(p\), \(\sigma\), …) plus C are the hyperparameters you tune.
  • Multiclass: one-vs-rest (\(C\) SVMs, winner-takes-all) or one-vs-one (\(\frac{C(C-1)}{2}\) SVMs, majority vote); OvO is often faster with many classes due to small, balanced per-SVM training sets.
  • SVM is free from the curse of dimensionality — parameters scale with the number of training samples, not the data dimension.

Going Deeper — Week 18

SVM is secretly a single-hidden-layer neural network. Once you write the test-time discriminant in kernel form, \(g(\mathbf{x}) = \sum_{n \in SV} \alpha_n y_n K(\mathbf{x}_n, \mathbf{x}) + w_0\), a picture emerges: the input \(\mathbf{x}\) fans out to one hidden node per support vector, each hidden node computes the kernel \(K(\mathbf{x}_n, \mathbf{x})\) (the kernel plays the role of the activation function), the learned weights on those connections are \(\alpha_n y_n\), and a single output node sums them and thresholds at \(0\). The difference from an ordinary neural network is profound and favourable: in an MLP you must guess the number of hidden neurons and tune it experimentally, whereas in an SVM the number of hidden nodes is determined automatically by the optimisation — it equals the number of support vectors. The convex quadratic program has a unique global optimum (no local minima, no random initialisation), yet a single-hidden-layer kernel SVM can carve decision regions an ordinary single-hidden-layer net cannot. This is why SVMs were the dominant non-linear classifier before deep learning matured.

The bias \(w_0\) comes for free from the KKT condition. The dual gives you \(\mathbf{w} = \sum_n \alpha_n y_n \mathbf{x}_n\) directly, but \(w_0\) is not produced by the dual — it has to be recovered. Use the fact that every support vector lying on the margin satisfies \(y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n + w_0) = 1\) exactly (this is the KKT complementary-slackness condition with \(\alpha_n \neq 0\)). Pick any on-margin support vector \(s\) and solve \(w_0 = y_s - \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_s\) (using \(y_s = 1/y_s\) since \(y_s \in \{+1,-1\}\)). In the kernel case you never form \(\mathbf{w}\) explicitly, so you compute \(w_0 = y_s - \sum_{n \in SV} \alpha_n y_n K(\mathbf{x}_n, \mathbf{x}_s)\). In practice one averages this over all on-margin support vectors for numerical stability. Note: in the soft-margin case you must pick a support vector with \(0 < \alpha_n < C\) (strictly inside the bound) — those are the ones genuinely on the margin; points with \(\alpha_n = C\) are margin violators and do not satisfy the on-margin equality.

The feature-space dimension explodes, but the kernel never pays for it. For a degree-\(p\) polynomial kernel on \(d\)-dimensional inputs, the explicit feature map \(\phi\) lands in a space of dimension \(D = \binom{d+p}{p} = \frac{(d+p)!}{d!\,p!}\). With \(d=2, p=2\) that is \(\binom{4}{2}=6\) monomials — manageable. But with \(d=100\) inputs and degree \(p=4\) you would need \(D = \binom{104}{4} \approx 4.6\) million features; computing \(\phi(\mathbf{x})\) explicitly is hopeless, yet \(K(\mathbf{x}_m,\mathbf{x}_n)=(\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n+1)^4\) costs one dot product plus a 4th power. The Gaussian/RBF kernel takes this to the limit: it is equivalent to a polynomial of every degree from \(1\) to \(\infty\), so \(D = \infty\) — an infinite-dimensional feature space evaluated in finite time. This is exactly why SVM is free from the curse of dimensionality: the number of parameters it estimates equals the number of training samples (one \(\alpha_n\) each), never the dimension \(D\). You can work in an infinite-dimensional space with as few as two training points.

Worked example — the effect of \(C\) on the soft margin. Imagine two nearly-separable clusters with one blue outlier sitting deep inside the red cloud. With a large \(C\) (say \(C = 1000\)), the penalty term \(C\sum_n \zeta_n\) dominates, so the optimiser refuses to tolerate slack: it contorts the boundary to put that outlier on the correct side, producing a narrow, wiggly margin that overfits — low training error, poor generalisation. With a small \(C\) (say \(C = 0.01\)), the \(\tfrac12\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert^2\) term dominates, so the optimiser happily pays a little hinge loss for the outlier (\(\zeta > 1\), a misclassification) in exchange for a wide, smooth margin — a few training errors but better generalisation. Concretely, recall \(\zeta_n = \max(0,\, 1 - y_n g(\mathbf{x}_n))\): for the outlier with \(y_n = -1\) but \(g(\mathbf{x}_n) = +2\), \(\zeta_n = \max(0, 1-(-1)(2)) = 3\). Large \(C\) multiplies that \(3\) by a big weight and fights hard to eliminate it; small \(C\) shrugs it off. The dual mirrors this through the bound \(0 \le \alpha_n \le C\): a large \(C\) lets a single point grab a large \(\alpha_n\) (high influence), while a small \(C\) caps every point’s influence, spreading the decision across more support vectors. Choosing a kernel for a data shape: roughly-linear or very high-dimensional sparse data (e.g. text) → linear kernel; smooth curved boundaries of known order → polynomial; blobby/circular/unknown-shape data → RBF (the default workhorse), where you additionally tune \(\sigma\) (small \(\sigma\) = very local, wiggly, overfits; large \(\sigma\) = smooth, approaches linear).

Exam-Style Q&A — Week 18

Q1. A trained soft-margin SVM has solved its dual and returned the multipliers \(\alpha_n\). For a particular training point you observe \(\alpha_n = 0\). What does this tell you about that point’s location, and is it a support vector?

A1. \(\alpha_n = 0\) means the point is strictly on the correct side of its margin (the KKT condition forces \(y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n + w_0) > 1\) there). It is not a support vector and contributes nothing to \(\mathbf{w} = \sum \alpha_n y_n \mathbf{x}_n\); it could be deleted from the training set without changing the boundary. Only points with \(\alpha_n > 0\) are support vectors: those with \(0 < \alpha_n < C\) sit exactly on the margin, and those with \(\alpha_n = C\) are margin violators or misclassified points.

Q2. In the canonical (hard-margin) SVM the margin width is \(\tfrac{2}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert}\). Explain why maximising the margin is equivalent to minimising \(\tfrac12\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert^2\), and why we use the squared norm with the \(\tfrac12\).

A2. Maximising \(\tfrac{2}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert}\) means making \(\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert\) as small as possible (the margin is the reciprocal of the norm), so the maximisation converts to the minimisation \(\min \lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert\). We then square the norm because \(\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert = \sqrt{\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{w}}\) contains an awkward square root that is hard to differentiate; \(\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert^2 = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{w}\) has the same minimiser but a clean gradient. The \(\tfrac12\) is cosmetic: differentiating \(\tfrac12\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{w}\) gives exactly \(\mathbf{w}\) (the factor of 2 from the power cancels the \(\tfrac12\)), keeping the optimality equations tidy.

Q3. You apply the degree-2 polynomial map \(\phi(x_1,x_2) = (x_1^2,\ x_2^2,\ \sqrt{2}\,x_1 x_2)\) to data that is separated by a circle in 2-D. Show why the data becomes linearly separable and state which theorem guarantees this generally.

A3. A circular boundary in input space is \(x_1^2 + x_2^2 = r^2\). In feature space the first two coordinates are \(z_1 = x_1^2\) and \(z_2 = x_2^2\), so \(z_1 + z_2 = x_1^2 + x_2^2\) is the squared radius — the circle becomes the flat plane \(z_1 + z_2 = r^2\), which a linear SVM can separate. Inside-the-circle points have small \(z_1+z_2\), outside points have large \(z_1+z_2\), cleanly split by that plane. Cover’s theorem guarantees this in general: data that is non-linearly separable in a low-dimensional input space becomes (approximately) linearly separable when non-linearly mapped into a sufficiently high-dimensional feature space.

Q4. What two conditions must a function’s kernel Gram matrix satisfy to be a valid kernel, and why does one of them matter for actually solving the SVM?

A4. The \(N\times N\) Gram matrix \(K\) with entries \(K(\mathbf{x}_m,\mathbf{x}_n)\) must be (1) symmetric — \(K(\mathbf{x}_m,\mathbf{x}_n) = K(\mathbf{x}_n,\mathbf{x}_m)\) — and (2) positive semi-definite (PSD), i.e. all its eigenvalues are non-negative. These are the checkable sufficient conditions for satisfying Mercer’s theorem, which certifies the kernel corresponds to a genuine dot product \(\phi(\mathbf{x}_m)^\top\phi(\mathbf{x}_n)\) in some feature space. PSD-ness matters operationally because it makes the dual a convex quadratic program — only then is the quadratic optimisation guaranteed to converge to a unique global optimum (SMO relies on this); a non-PSD “kernel” can leave the optimiser without a well-defined solution.

Q5. You have a 1000-class problem with 100 samples per class. By raw SVM count, one-vs-rest needs only 1000 SVMs while one-vs-one needs ~500,000. Yet one-vs-one is often the faster, easier choice here. Explain the reasoning.

A5. SVM count is the wrong metric; what matters is per-SVM training size and class balance. In one-vs-rest each of the 1000 SVMs trains on all 100,000 samples and is brutally imbalanced (100 positive vs ~99,900 negative), so each optimisation is large, slow, and skewed. In one-vs-one each of the ~500,000 SVMs trains on just the 200 samples of its two classes — small, perfectly balanced problems the quadratic optimiser solves almost instantly. The total wall-clock time and the load on the optimiser are therefore typically lower for one-vs-one when classes are numerous. (With only a few classes the difference is negligible and one-vs-rest’s smaller SVM count wins.)

Quick-Revision Cheat-Sheet — Week 18

  • Optimal hyperplane = max-margin hyperplane, centred in the gap, equidistant from nearest points of both classes → best generalisation. Distance of a point: \(\dfrac{|g(\mathbf{x})|}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert}\), where \(g(\mathbf{x})=\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}+w_0\).
  • Canonical scaling: rescale so nearest points give \(|g|=1\) → margin lines \(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}+w_0=\pm1\), width \(=\dfrac{2}{\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert}\). Max margin \(\equiv \min \tfrac12\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert^2\) s.t. \(y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n+w_0)\ge1\).
  • Dual (depends on data only via dot products): \(\max_\alpha \sum_n\alpha_n - \tfrac12\sum_{m,n}\alpha_m\alpha_n y_m y_n (\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n)\), with \(\alpha_n\ge0,\ \sum_n\alpha_n y_n=0\). Solved by SMO (quadratic program).
  • Support vectors = points with \(\alpha_n\neq0\); \(\mathbf{w}=\sum_{n\in SV}\alpha_n y_n\mathbf{x}_n\). KKT: \(\alpha_n[y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n+w_0)-1]=0\) → \(\alpha_n\neq0 \Rightarrow\) point on margin. Recover bias: \(w_0=y_s-\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_s\) from an on-margin SV.
  • Hard vs soft margin: hard requires perfect separation; soft adds slack \(\zeta_n=\max(0,\,1-y_n g(\mathbf{x}_n))\) (hinge loss). On/inside-correct \(\zeta=0\); margin violation \(0<\zeta<1\); misclassified \(\zeta>1\).
  • Soft-margin primal: \(\min \tfrac12\lVert\mathbf{w}\rVert^2 + C\sum_n\zeta_n\) s.t. \(y_n(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_n+w_0)\ge1-\zeta_n,\ \zeta_n\ge0\). Dual unchanged except \(0\le\alpha_n\le C\). Small \(C\) = wide margin, more errors tolerated; large \(C\) = narrow margin, tight fit.
  • Kernel trick: replace every \(\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n\) with \(K(\mathbf{x}_m,\mathbf{x}_n)=\phi(\mathbf{x}_m)^\top\phi(\mathbf{x}_n)\) — implicit dot product in feature space, \(\phi\) never computed. Test: \(g(\mathbf{x})=\sum_{n\in SV}\alpha_n y_n K(\mathbf{x}_n,\mathbf{x})+w_0\). Cover’s theorem justifies the lift.
  • Kernels: linear \(\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n\); polynomial \((a\,\mathbf{x}_m^\top\mathbf{x}_n+b)^p\) → feature dim \(\binom{d+p}{p}\); Gaussian/RBF \(\exp\!\big(-\tfrac{\lVert\mathbf{x}_m-\mathbf{x}_n\rVert^2}{\sigma}\big)\) → infinite-dim (all polynomial degrees). Each is a similarity measure.
  • Mercer / validity: Gram matrix must be symmetric and positive semi-definite (non-negative eigenvalues) → guarantees a convex QP that converges. Kernel choice + its params (\(a,b,p,\sigma\)) and \(C\) are the tuned hyperparameters.
  • Multiclass: one-vs-rest = \(C\) SVMs, winner-takes-all (highest \(g\)); one-vs-one = \(\tfrac{C(C-1)}{2}\) SVMs, majority vote. OvO often faster with many classes (small, balanced per-SVM training sets). Defaults: scikit-learn/LIBSVM → OvO; LIBLINEAR/SVM-light/SVM-torch → OvR.
  • SVM as single-hidden-layer net: one hidden node per support vector, kernel = activation, weights \(\alpha_n y_n\); number of hidden nodes auto-set by the optimiser (not hand-tuned).
  • Free from curse of dimensionality: parameters = number of training samples (the \(\alpha_n\)), independent of data/feature dimension. Minimum to build an SVM: 2 points (one per class).

Part III — Study Toolkit

Real-world applications across domains, a concept map that ties everything together, and a full glossary of key terms.

AI in the Real World — Applications Across Domains

In one line: Across text, vision, speech, and many other domains, the same small toolbox of ML/DL techniques — classification, detection, sequence modeling, generation, anomaly detection, and reinforcement learning — is reused to solve real problems, with each domain mainly differing in how its data is turned into features.

What this chapter covers

  • The three “natural” data modes the course centers on — text, visual (image/video), and audio — and the concrete tasks each enables.
  • High-impact application domains beyond raw perception: healthcare, finance, autonomous systems, recommendation, and a grab-bag of others (education, games, science, agriculture, climate).
  • For each domain: concrete real-world examples plus the underlying technique (CNN, RNN/LSTM, Transformer/LLM, SVM, GMM, regression, clustering, GAN/diffusion, RL) so you can connect tasks back to the models studied in the course.
  • The recurring idea from the lectures: most of these are pattern recognition problems — capture the structure in the data, then predict, classify, or generate.

Natural Language & Text

Most language tasks split into understanding (NLU) and generation (NLG), and many combine both. The simplest is text classification: routing an email to spam/not-spam, tagging a news article as politics/business/sports/entertainment, or sentiment analysis on a product or movie review (the lecture’s example — “the food was good, not bad at all” is positive, while the same words reordered, “the food was bad, not good at all,” is negative — shows why word order and context matter, which is exactly what RNNs/LSTMs and Transformers capture). Lower-level NLU tasks include part-of-speech tagging and named entity recognition (e.g. spotting that “George Washington” is a person but “Washington State” is a location), used in resume parsers, search indexing, and clinical-text mining.

The bigger payoffs come from generation and combined tasks. Search uses embeddings to match a query to relevant documents (web search, enterprise document retrieval, semantic product search). Machine translation — Google Translate, DeepL — must handle different word orders (English subject-verb-object vs. Japanese subject-object-verb) and morphology (the plural of “man” is “men,” not “mans”); modern systems are sequence-to-sequence Transformers. Summarization powers tools that condense long reports or research abstracts, either extractive (select key sentences) or abstractive (paraphrase the meaning). Chatbots and question answering run customer-support assistants (closed-domain) and open-domain web QA, while LLM assistants like ChatGPT (a GPT-family generative pre-trained Transformer) handle drafting, coding help, and reasoning. These rest on the pre-train/fine-tune recipe: BERT-style encoders for understanding, GPT-style decoders for generation, both built on the attention mechanism.

Computer Vision — Image & Video

The foundational task is image classification — assigning one label to an image (animal-species apps, defect detection on a factory line) — which must cope with intra-class variability (a tiger seen front-on, partially occluded, or against different backgrounds) and inter-class similarity (cheetah vs. tiger). When an image has several objects, annotation/multi-label tagging assigns multiple labels (Google Photos search by content). Object detection draws bounding boxes and labels — YOLO is the canonical real-time detector used in retail checkout, security, and traffic monitoring — while segmentation marks pixel-precise regions of interest (U-Net for separating a cat from its background, or tumor regions in a scan). All of these are powered by deep CNNs: LeNet, VGGNet, GoogLeNet, ResNet for classification; YOLO for detection; U-Net for segmentation; 3D-CNNs for video.

Higher-level and generative vision tasks abound. Image captioning generates a sentence describing a photo (“a group of people at an outdoor market”) by combining a CNN encoder with a language decoder; visual question answering answers “is there something to cut the vegetables with?” by locating a knife in the image; video classification and captioning label events across frames (multi-modal, mixing visual, audio, and subtitle streams). Practical deployments include medical imaging (detecting diabetic retinopathy, lung nodules, or fractures from X-rays, CT, and MRI), face recognition (phone unlock, identity verification), OCR (digitizing documents, reading license plates and bank cheques), and self-driving perception (lane, pedestrian, and obstacle detection feeding the control stack). Generation is now mainstream: GANs and diffusion models do image-to-image translation (sketch-to-photo of a face), text-controlled editing (“make the petals white with yellow patches”), and text-to-image synthesis (“a painting of a squirrel eating a burger”) in tools like Stable Diffusion and DALL-E.

Speech & Audio

The workhorse is speech-to-text (ASR) — dictation, meeting transcription, and captioning — which must be robust to vocabulary size, speaker, language, channel (microphone vs. mobile vs. landline noise), accent, emotion, and speaking rate. Voice assistants (Alexa, Siri, Google Assistant) chain ASR with understanding and a spoken dialogue system (recognize, understand, retrieve, respond, synthesize). Speaker identification/verification is used as a biometric for access control (a voiceprint instead of a fingerprint), and speech emotion recognition (happy/sad/angry/neutral) helps call-center analytics. In multi-speaker recordings, diarization answers “who spoke when,” and spoken-language diarization flags which language each segment is in; together with ASR and emotion these form full speech transcription of meetings.

On the generation and translation side, text-to-speech (TTS) produces natural-sounding voices for screen readers, audiobooks, and navigation, with prosody (pitch, stress) and even voice conversion to switch speaker identity. Speech-to-speech translation stacks ASR → machine translation → TTS to let two people converse across languages in real time. Music has its own family: raga and instrument identification, music transcription to notation, music retrieval (Shazam-style matching), and generative text-to-music systems. Classic statistical models (GMMs) were long used for speaker and language recognition; modern systems use RNN/LSTM and Transformer architectures on spectral (frequency-domain) features.

Healthcare

AI assists across the clinical pipeline. Diagnosis from scans uses CNNs to flag disease in X-ray, CT, MRI, ultrasound, and pathology slides — diabetic retinopathy screening, mammography, and skin-lesion classification are deployed examples. Disease-progression prediction estimates whether a condition is early or advanced and how fast it may deteriorate, using regression and sequence models over longitudinal records. Drug discovery uses ML to screen candidate compounds and predict toxicity, reducing the need for physical lab testing, and to predict how a patient will respond to a given medication or therapy.

Patient monitoring processes real-time vitals from wearables and ICU sensors to raise early alarms (arrhythmia or sepsis detection), often via anomaly detection on time-series signals. Personalized treatment fuses genetic, clinical, and lifestyle data to tailor a treatment plan, and virtual assistants triage symptoms and route patients to the right provider. A theme from the lecture: healthcare data is multi-modal (text notes, image scans, signal waveforms), so real systems must integrate evidence across these forms.

Finance & Banking

Fraud detection is the classic case: fraudulent transactions are anomalies — abnormal behavior against a learned normal baseline — so it is framed as anomaly detection over transaction and text data (card fraud, money laundering, account takeover, cyber threats). Closely related, models produce a fraud-likelihood score via regression. Credit scoring analyzes a customer’s financial history to assess creditworthiness — will they repay? — and to auto-approve or reject loan and credit-card applications.

Beyond risk, algorithmic trading and portfolio management use prediction and optimization to time trades and allocate investments, while financial planning tools advise on whether to invest in a given stock. Customer-facing chatbots handle queries in text or speech (needing ASR plus understanding), and sentiment analysis on customer feedback routes complaints. ML also supports regulatory compliance review. The dominant techniques are regression for scoring, anomaly/outlier detection for fraud, and optimization methods for allocation.

Autonomous Systems & Robotics

Self-driving cars (Waymo, Tesla, Cruise) integrate a perception stack (CNN-based detection and segmentation of lanes, vehicles, pedestrians, signs) with a control stack, where reinforcement learning is used for control and decision-making — choosing speed, steering, and maneuvers. The lecture notes RL is central to building the control modules of these unmanned/autonomous vehicles. Drones apply the same perception-plus-control loop for inspection, delivery, and mapping.

Robotics uses RL (Markov decision processes, Q-learning, deep Q-networks, actor-critic methods, with exploration/exploitation strategies) to learn manipulation and locomotion — a warehouse robot learning to grasp varied objects, or a legged robot learning to walk. In space and operations, autonomous planning and scheduling lets remote agents plan tasks and sequence them without ground control, the kind of system flown on deep-space probes.

Recommendation & Personalization

Recommendation engines drive a large share of e-commerce, streaming, and advertising revenue: Amazon “customers who bought this also bought,” Netflix and YouTube next-watch suggestions, Spotify playlists, and ad targeting. The lecture frames the two main approaches: collaborative filtering uses the history of a user plus other users with similar tastes (“people like you liked X”), while content-based filtering recommends items similar to ones the user already liked, based on item features.

In practice large platforms blend both (hybrid systems) and add deep models that learn embeddings of users and items, plus sequence models that capture the order of recent interactions. The core ML idea is the same pattern-capture-then-predict loop: model preferences from past behavior, then predict the next item the user is most likely to engage with.

Other

Education uses AI for personalized learning that adapts modules to a student’s grasping ability, automated grading with immediate feedback on quizzes, and generative AI to create supplementary study material — intelligent tutoring systems are a long-running example. Game playing is a landmark domain: chess engines and, more famously, AlphaGo / AlphaZero mastering Go through deep reinforcement learning and self-play, requiring the system to reason and anticipate an opponent’s moves — sometimes surpassing human champions.

Scientific discovery applies ML to protein structure prediction (AlphaFold), materials design, and analyzing data from telescopes and particle detectors. Agriculture uses vision for crop-disease detection, yield prediction, and precision spraying from drone imagery.
