Climate and environment applications include weather and extreme-event forecasting, energy-demand optimization, satellite monitoring of deforestation, and emissions modeling. In every case the underlying machinery is the same family of models from this course — classifiers, regressors, sequence models, generative models, and RL agents — applied to a new kind of data.

Key takeaways

  • Nearly every application reduces to pattern recognition: find the structure in the data, then predict, classify, or generate — the same principle whether the data is text, an image, a waveform, or a transaction log.
  • A small toolbox is reused everywhere: CNNs for images/video, RNN/LSTM and Transformers for sequences (text, speech), SVM/GMM/regression/clustering for classic tasks, GANs/diffusion for generation, and reinforcement learning for control and decision-making.
  • Domains differ mainly in feature representation — how raw text, pixels, audio, or records become numerical vectors — not in the core learning algorithms.
  • Generation (text, image, speech, music) and anomaly detection (fraud, health monitoring) are two cross-cutting capabilities that recur across many domains.
  • Real systems are often multi-modal and multi-stage: speech-to-speech translation chains ASR + MT + TTS; video understanding fuses visual, audio, and text; healthcare fuses notes, scans, and signals.
  • The goal across all domains is generalization — performing well on new, unseen data — which is why training, regularization, and avoiding overfitting matter as much as picking the right model.

How It All Connects — Concept Map

In one line: A bird’s-eye view of how the maths, the paradigms, the techniques, and the applications in this companion fit together — so each week stops feeling like an isolated topic.

AI ⊃ Machine Learning ⊃ Deep Learning

Artificial Intelligence is the broad goal (machines that act intelligently). Machine Learning is the subset that learns from data instead of hand-coded rules. Deep Learning is the subset of ML that uses many-layered neural networks.

The end-to-end machine-learning workflow

Almost every project — classical or deep — follows the same pipeline. Each stage maps to topics in this companion.

Map of the territory (text version)

  • Maths foundations — Linear Algebra (W2), Calculus (W3), Probability (W4)
    • feed into → every model below.
  • Tooling — Python (W5–W7), EDA (W8)
    • prepare → data and features.
  • Learning paradigms (W11–W12) — how the model learns:
    • Supervised → Regression (W13–W14), Classification (W15–W18)
    • Unsupervised → clustering, dimensionality reduction
    • Plus semi-/self-/active/online/meta/reinforcement learning.
  • Classical techniques — Linear/Polynomial Regression (W13–W14) · kNN & metrics (W15) · Bayes/Naive Bayes/GMM (W16) · Discriminant + Perceptron (W17) · SVM + kernels (W18).
  • Deep learning (Big-Picture chapters) — Neural nets & training → CNNs (vision) · RNN/LSTM (sequences) · Transformers (language) · GANs/Diffusion/LLMs (generation) · Reinforcement Learning (control).
  • Applications — Vision · Speech · NLP · Healthcare · Finance · Autonomous systems · Recommendation (W9, W10, and the Applications chapter).
  • Cross-cutting — Optimization (the engine, W3), Overfitting & Regularization (W14), Evaluation & Responsible AI (W15 + the Responsible-AI chapter).

The single thread

Data becomes vectors (linear algebra) → a model is a function with parameters → training is an optimization problem solved with gradients (calculus) → many models are probabilistic (Bayes, Gaussian) → you evaluate honestly and guard against overfitting → deep learning stacks the same ideas into layers. Master that thread and every week is a variation on it.

Glossary of Key Terms

A

Accuracy — The fraction of all predictions a classifier got right, \(\frac{\text{correct}}{\text{total}}\); simple but misleading on imbalanced data.

Activation function — A non-linear function applied to a neuron’s weighted output (e.g. ReLU, sigmoid, softmax, tanh) that lets a network learn non-linear patterns rather than just linear combinations.

Active learning — A training setting where the model chooses which unlabelled examples a human should label next, aiming to learn well from as few labels as possible.

Adam — A popular adaptive gradient-descent optimizer that combines momentum and per-parameter learning-rate scaling using running estimates of the first and second moments of the gradients.

Attention — A mechanism that lets a model weigh the relevance of different input elements to each other, computing outputs as a weighted sum of values based on query-key similarity.

B

Backpropagation — The algorithm that trains neural networks by applying the chain rule backward through the layers to compute the gradient of the loss with respect to every weight.

Batch normalization — A layer that standardizes activations within each mini-batch (zero mean, unit variance) to stabilize and speed up training.

Bayes’ theorem — A rule for updating probabilities given new evidence: \(P(A\mid B) = \frac{P(B\mid A)\,P(A)}{P(B)}\).

Bias-variance tradeoff — The tension between a model too simple to fit the data (high bias, underfitting) and one too flexible that fits noise (high variance, overfitting).

Bias (model parameter) — A learnable constant added to a neuron’s weighted input that shifts the activation, letting the function fit data not passing through the origin.

C

Class imbalance — When some classes have far more examples than others, biasing a model toward the majority class and making accuracy a poor metric.

Classification — A supervised task that assigns inputs to discrete categories (e.g. spam vs. not spam).

Clustering — An unsupervised task that groups similar data points together without using labels (e.g. k-means).

Confusion matrix — A table of true vs. predicted classes showing counts of true positives, false positives, true negatives and false negatives.

Convolution — An operation that slides a small filter (kernel) across an input to detect local patterns such as edges, producing a feature map.

Convolutional Neural Network (CNN) — A network using convolution and pooling layers to learn spatial hierarchies of features, especially effective on images.

Correlation — A measure (typically Pearson’s \(r \in [-1, 1]\)) of how strongly two variables move together linearly.

Cross-validation — A technique that splits data into folds, training and testing on different folds in turn, to estimate generalization more reliably than a single split.

D

Data leakage — When information from outside the training data (or from the test set) sneaks into training, giving overly optimistic results that don’t hold in deployment.

Derivative — The instantaneous rate of change of a function with respect to its input; the slope of its tangent line.

Diffusion model — A generative model that learns to reverse a gradual noising process, generating data by iteratively denoising random noise (e.g. Stable Diffusion).

Discriminant function — A function whose value (or sign) decides which class an input belongs to, defining the decision boundary between classes.

Dot product — The sum of element-wise products of two vectors, \(\mathbf{a}\cdot\mathbf{b} = \sum_i a_i b_i\); measures alignment and underlies projection and similarity.

Dropout — A regularization technique that randomly disables a fraction of neurons during training to prevent co-adaptation and overfitting.

E

Eigenvalue / Eigenvector — For a matrix \(A\), a vector \(\mathbf{v}\) and scalar \(\lambda\) satisfying \(A\mathbf{v} = \lambda \mathbf{v}\); the eigenvector’s direction is unchanged by \(A\), only scaled by \(\lambda\).

Embedding — A learned dense vector representation of a discrete item (word, token, user) that places semantically similar items close together in space.

Encoding — Converting categorical or non-numeric data into a numeric form a model can use (e.g. one-hot encoding, label encoding).

EM (Expectation-Maximization) — An iterative algorithm for fitting models with latent variables, alternating between estimating the hidden assignments (E-step) and updating parameters (M-step); used to fit GMMs.

Expectation — The probability-weighted average value of a random variable, \(E[X] = \sum_x x\,P(x)\) (or the integral version for continuous variables).

Explainability — The degree to which a model’s predictions can be understood and justified by humans (e.g. via feature importances or SHAP values).

Exploratory Data Analysis (EDA) — The initial process of summarizing and visualizing a dataset to find patterns, spot anomalies and form hypotheses before modeling.

F

F1 score — The harmonic mean of precision and recall, \(2\cdot\frac{P\cdot R}{P+R}\); balances the two in a single metric, useful under class imbalance.

Fairness — The principle that a model should not produce systematically biased or discriminatory outcomes across protected groups.

Feature — An individual measurable input variable (column) used to describe each example fed to a model.

Few-shot learning — Learning to perform a task from only a handful of labelled examples, often by leveraging prior knowledge or in-context examples.

Fine-tuning — Continuing to train a pre-trained model on a smaller task-specific dataset to adapt it to a particular application.

G

GAN (Generative Adversarial Network) — A generative model where a generator and a discriminator are trained against each other, the generator learning to produce data realistic enough to fool the discriminator.

Gaussian (Normal) distribution — The bell-shaped continuous distribution defined by its mean \(\mu\) and variance \(\sigma^2\); ubiquitous due to the Central Limit Theorem.

Generalization — A model’s ability to perform well on new, unseen data rather than just the data it was trained on.

GMM (Gaussian Mixture Model) — A probabilistic model representing data as a weighted combination of several Gaussian distributions, typically fit with EM.

Gradient — The vector of all partial derivatives of a function; it points in the direction of steepest increase and is the basis of gradient-based optimization.

Gradient descent — An optimization algorithm that iteratively moves parameters opposite the gradient of the loss to minimize it, scaled by a learning rate.

GRU (Gated Recurrent Unit) — A streamlined recurrent unit with update and reset gates that captures long-range dependencies with fewer parameters than an LSTM.

H

Hallucination — When a generative model (especially an LLM) produces fluent but factually false or fabricated content.

Hyperparameter — A configuration value set before training (e.g. learning rate, number of layers, \(k\) in kNN) rather than learned from data.

J

Jacobian — The matrix of all first-order partial derivatives of a vector-valued function, generalizing the gradient to multiple outputs.

K

Kernel (kernel trick) — A function that computes inner products in a high-dimensional feature space without explicitly mapping there, enabling SVMs and other methods to learn non-linear boundaries.

kNN (k-Nearest Neighbors) — A simple algorithm that classifies (or regresses) a point based on the majority label (or average) of its \(k\) closest training examples.

L

Label — The target output (ground truth) associated with an example in supervised learning.

Lagrange multiplier — A method for optimizing a function subject to equality constraints by introducing auxiliary variables, central to deriving the SVM solution.

LLM (Large Language Model) — A Transformer-based model with billions of parameters trained on vast text corpora to predict and generate language (e.g. GPT, Claude).

Loss function — The objective that quantifies how wrong a model’s predictions are; training minimizes it (e.g. MSE, cross-entropy).

LSTM (Long Short-Term Memory) — A recurrent unit with input, forget and output gates and a cell state, designed to learn long-range dependencies while avoiding vanishing gradients.

M

Margin — In an SVM, the distance between the decision boundary and the nearest data points; SVMs seek the maximum-margin separating hyperplane.

Matrix — A rectangular array of numbers representing a linear transformation or a table of data, central to nearly all ML computation.

Meta-learning — “Learning to learn”: training models that can adapt quickly to new tasks from limited data by leveraging experience across many tasks.

MLE (Maximum Likelihood Estimation) — A method that chooses model parameters making the observed data most probable under the model.

MLP (Multilayer Perceptron) — A fully connected feedforward neural network with one or more hidden layers and non-linear activations.

N

Naive Bayes — A probabilistic classifier applying Bayes’ theorem with the simplifying assumption that features are conditionally independent given the class.

Neural network — A model of interconnected layers of neurons that transform inputs through weighted sums and non-linear activations to approximate complex functions.

Norm — A measure of a vector’s length/magnitude; the L2 norm is Euclidean length \(\sqrt{\sum_i x_i^2}\) and the L1 norm is \(\sum_i |x_i|\).

Normalization — Rescaling data to a common range or distribution (e.g. min-max to \([0,1]\)) so features contribute comparably and training is stable.

O

Online learning — Training a model incrementally as data arrives one example (or small batch) at a time, rather than over a fixed full dataset.

Optimization — The process of finding parameter values that minimize (or maximize) an objective function, the mathematical core of model training.

Outlier — A data point that deviates markedly from the rest, possibly due to error or a rare event, which can distort models and statistics.

Overfitting — When a model learns the training data too closely, including its noise, and consequently generalizes poorly to new data.

P

Partial derivative — The derivative of a multivariable function with respect to one variable, holding the others fixed.

Perceptron — The simplest neural unit: a linear combination of inputs passed through a step (threshold) function; the historical building block of neural networks.

Pooling — A downsampling operation in CNNs (e.g. max pooling) that reduces spatial size and provides small translation invariance.

Pre-training — Training a model on a large, often unlabelled or self-supervised dataset to learn general representations before task-specific fine-tuning.

Precision — Of the items a model predicted positive, the fraction that truly are positive: \(\frac{TP}{TP+FP}\).

Probability — A number in \([0,1]\) quantifying how likely an event is, with 0 meaning impossible and 1 meaning certain.

Prompt — The input text or instruction given to a generative model to elicit a desired response.

R

RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) — A technique that retrieves relevant documents from an external knowledge source and feeds them to an LLM to ground its answers and reduce hallucination.

Random variable — A variable whose value is determined by the outcome of a random process, described by a probability distribution.

Recall — Of the items that are truly positive, the fraction the model correctly identified: \(\frac{TP}{TP+FN}\).

Regression — A supervised task that predicts a continuous numeric output (e.g. house price) from input features.

Regularization — Techniques (e.g. L1/L2 penalties, dropout) that constrain a model’s complexity to reduce overfitting and improve generalization.

Reinforcement learning — A paradigm where an agent learns to act by interacting with an environment and maximizing cumulative reward through trial and error.

ReLU (Rectified Linear Unit) — The activation \(f(x)=\max(0,x)\); cheap, mitigates vanishing gradients, and is the default in many deep networks.

RLHF (Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback) — A fine-tuning method that aligns a model’s outputs with human preferences using a reward model trained on human comparisons.

RMSE (Root Mean Squared Error) — A regression metric, the square root of the average squared prediction error, in the same units as the target.

RNN (Recurrent Neural Network) — A network that processes sequences by maintaining a hidden state passed from one time step to the next.

S

Scaling — Adjusting the magnitude of features (e.g. standardization to zero mean and unit variance) so no single feature dominates due to its units.

Self-supervised learning — Learning from unlabelled data by generating supervisory signals from the data itself (e.g. predicting masked words), key to pre-training LLMs.

Semi-supervised learning — Learning from a small amount of labelled data combined with a large amount of unlabelled data.

SGD (Stochastic Gradient Descent) — Gradient descent that estimates the gradient from one example or a mini-batch at a time, making training faster and able to escape some local minima.

Sigmoid — The activation \(\sigma(x)=\frac{1}{1+e^{-x}}\) squashing inputs to \((0,1)\), often used for binary probabilities.

Softmax — A function that converts a vector of scores into a probability distribution summing to 1, used for multi-class outputs.

Supervised learning — Learning a mapping from inputs to outputs using labelled training examples.

Support vector — In an SVM, the training points lying closest to the decision boundary; they alone determine the maximum-margin hyperplane.

SVD (Singular Value Decomposition) — A factorization of any matrix \(A = U\Sigma V^\top\) into rotations and a scaling, used for dimensionality reduction, PCA and compression.

SVM (Support Vector Machine) — A classifier that finds the maximum-margin separating hyperplane, optionally using kernels to handle non-linear boundaries.

T

Test set — Data held out entirely from training and tuning, used once to give an unbiased estimate of final model performance.

TF-IDF (Term Frequency-Inverse Document Frequency) — A weighting scheme that scores a word in a document by how frequent it is there but rare across the corpus, highlighting distinctive terms.

Tokenization — Splitting text into smaller units (words, subwords or characters) that a model processes as discrete tokens.

Training set — The data used to fit a model’s parameters during learning.

Transfer learning — Reusing knowledge from a model trained on one task or domain to improve learning on a related task with less data.

Transformer — A sequence model built on self-attention rather than recurrence, enabling parallel processing of sequences and underpinning modern LLMs.

U

Underfitting — When a model is too simple to capture the underlying structure of the data, performing poorly on both training and test sets.

Unsupervised learning — Learning patterns or structure from data that has no labels (e.g. clustering, dimensionality reduction).

V

VAE (Variational Autoencoder) — A generative model that encodes data into a probabilistic latent space and decodes samples from it, learning a smooth, continuous representation.

Validation set — Data used during development to tune hyperparameters and compare models, separate from both training and final test data.

Variance (statistics) — A measure of how spread out a random variable’s values are around its mean, \(\text{Var}(X)=E[(X-E[X])^2]\).

Vector — An ordered list of numbers representing a point or direction in space; the fundamental data structure for features and embeddings.
